The Self-Evident Nature of the Preponderator Principle
Razi clarifies the certainty of the preponderator principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proposition 'preponderator needed' is as self-evident as 'one is half of two', with the difference only in conceptualization.

Core Idea
Certainty of judgment does not depend on the familiarity of the subject matter.

Imam Razi opens his critique by addressing a common doubt: is the principle that 'preponderator needed' less certain than basic arithmetic truths? He argues that the difference lies not in the judgment's certainty but in how we conceptualize the terms. This clarification is essential for understanding the foundation of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Razi compares two statements.
The OCR is uncertain in places, but the overall argument is clear.
2
Identify the two statements
Find the two statements being compared: 'preponderance requires a preponderator' and 'one is half of two'. Understand the objection that the first is less certain.
3
Understand Razi's refutation
Razi says the difference is only in conceptualization of subject and predicate, not in the judgment itself. The judgment 'preponderator needed' is just as certain.
This is key to his critique.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: if the premise is self-evident, then any proof built on it is starting from a secure foundation, but Razi will later argue the proof is redundant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: التفاوت بين قولنا رجع أحد المتساويين يكون المرجح، وبين قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين بدل على تطرق الاحتماء إلى الأول فلا يكون تعيينا تاما ليس بصحيح ، لأن التفاوت يمكن أن يكون بسبب التفاوت في تصور المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به دون الحكم، أما في الحكم نفسه فلا يتفاوت كما ذكر هو ؟ أيضا فى الجواب، وأما إقامة برهانه على ذلك الحكم الضرورى فليس بشيء لأن وجوب الممكن المنتفى لوجود المرصوف به لا يمكن أن يكون قائما بمؤثره لأنه وصف للممكن ، ووصف الشيء يستحيل أن يقوم بغيره والقائم بالمؤثر إن كان ولا بد منه فهو إيجاب لا الوجوب . والحق : أن ذلك الوجوب أمر عقلى كسائر الصفات ويسكون قائما بالتصور من الممكن عند الحكم بحدوثه وأقول من رأى أن البرهان الذي أقامه مبنى على حكم هو قول الممسكن ما لم يجب لم يوجد وهذا انضية لا يصح الحكم فيها إلا إذا علم أن كل مسبب فله سبب ، وفي قولنا ترجح أحد المتساويين يحتاج إلى مرجح هذا المعنى بعينه موجود ولكن بعبارة أخرى فإذا البرهان الذي أقامه مبنى على ما يتضمنه الحكم البديهى المذكور الذي عدل عنه إلى ذلك البرهان ، فقد وضح من ذلك أن ذلك البرهان فضلة غير محتاج إليه.
I say: The difference between our statement 'When one of two equals is preponderant, there is a preponderator' and our statement 'One is half of two' indicates that doubt may attach to the first, so it is not a complete determination—this is not correct. Because the difference can be due to the difference in the conceptualization of the subject and predicate, not the judgment itself. As for the judgment itself, it does not differ, as he also mentioned in the answer. And as for establishing a proof for that necessary judgment, it is nothing, because the necessity of the contingent that is negated for the existence of the thing described by it cannot be subsisting in its effecter, for it is an attribute of the contingent, and the attribute of a thing is impossible to subsist in other than it; and what subsists in the effecter, if it must be, is obligation (ījāb), not necessity (wujūb). The truth is that that necessity is an intellectual matter like other attributes, and it subsists in the concept of the contingent when judging its origination. And I say: One who sees that the proof he constructed is based on a judgment, which is the statement 'The contingent, as long as it is not necessary, does not exist'—this is a proposition that is not valid to judge unless it is known that every effect has a cause. In our statement 'Preponderance of one of two equals needs a preponderator' this same meaning is present but in other words. So if the proof he constructed is based on what is contained in the aforementioned self-evident judgment from which he turned away to that proof, then it is clear that that proof is superfluous and not needed.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary (perhaps by the author himself or a later editor) on the earlier proof that a contingent needs a preponderator. The argument is that the proof is redundant because its core premise—'the contingent, as long as it is not necessary, does not exist'—is itself a self-evident judgment. Trying to prove it by a separate argument (e.g., necessity being a positive attribute) is unnecessary. The author also points out that the difference between two self-evident propositions (like preponderance needing a preponderator vs. one being half of two) lies in conceptualization, not in the judgment's certainty. He further argues that the necessity (wujūb) of the contingent cannot inhere in the effecter; it is an intellectual attribute of the contingent when we judge its origination. Thus the whole proof is a 'superfluity' (fuḍla) not needed. This is a meta-critique of the argument's methodology.

Key Terms
تفاوت
حكم
بديهي
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proposition 'preponderator needed' is less certain than basic mathematical truths.
Correction: Razi shows that the certainty lies in the judgment, not in how easily we conceive the terms. Both are equally self-evident once understood.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does the statement 'one of two equals needs a preponderator' seem less certain than 'one is half of two'?
A) Because the judgment itself is weaker.
B) Because the terms are harder to conceptualize.
C) Because it is not a self-evident truth.
D) Because it depends on empirical observation.

Key Takeaway
The judgment 'preponderator needed' is as self-evident as basic arithmetic; any perceived uncertainty is due to conceptual unfamiliarity, not a flaw in the judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a truth that seems difficult to grasp, do you dismiss it as uncertain or do you strive to understand its terms? How does Razi's distinction between conceptualization and judgment challenge your tendency to reject unfamiliar truths?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you consider self-evident. Could it be that you are confusing familiarity with certainty? How does this lesson call you to examine the foundations of your convictions?
?
If the preponderator principle is as certain as arithmetic, why do you often act as if chance or randomness governs events? What would it mean for your daily decisions to truly believe that every preponderance has a cause?

The Redundancy of the Proof for Necessity
Razi argues that the proof for necessity is redundant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi considers the proof for necessity of the contingent to be superfluous and not needed.

Core Idea
Attempting to prove a self-evident truth only creates unnecessary complexity.

After defending the self-evident nature of the preponderator principle, Razi turns to the proof that was built upon it. He argues that the proof is redundant because its core premise—that the contingent must have a cause—is itself a self-evident judgment. Trying to prove it via necessity (wujūb) being an attribute of the contingent is misguided and adds nothing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the second part of the passage. Note Razi's criticism of the proof and his alternative view of necessity.
Pay attention to the distinction between necessity (wujūb) and obligation (ījāb).
2
Understand the critique of the proof
Razi says the proof is 'nothing' because necessity cannot subsist in the effector. It is an attribute of the contingent, not of the cause.
He also states that necessity is an intellectual attribute.
3
Identify the self-evident judgment
Razi points out that the proof is based on the statement 'the contingent, as long as it is not necessary, does not exist', which is itself a self-evident proposition that already contains the principle that every effect has a cause.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the proof merely restates a self-evident truth, it is superfluous (fuḍla) and not needed. The original self-evident judgment suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: التفاوت بين قولنا رجع أحد المتساويين يكون المرجح، وبين قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين بدل على تطرق الاحتماء إلى الأول فلا يكون تعيينا تاما ليس بصحيح ، لأن التفاوت يمكن أن يكون بسبب التفاوت في تصور المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به دون الحكم، أما في الحكم نفسه فلا يتفاوت كما ذكر هو ؟ أيضا فى الجواب، وأما إقامة برهانه على ذلك الحكم الضرورى فليس بشيء لأن وجوب الممكن المنتفى لوجود المرصوف به لا يمكن أن يكون قائما بمؤثره لأنه وصف للممكن ، ووصف الشيء يستحيل أن يقوم بغيره والقائم بالمؤثر إن كان ولا بد منه فهو إيجاب لا الوجوب . والحق : أن ذلك الوجوب أمر عقلى كسائر الصفات ويسكون قائما بالتصور من الممكن عند الحكم بحدوثه وأقول من رأى أن البرهان الذي أقامه مبنى على حكم هو قول الممسكن ما لم يجب لم يوجد وهذا انضية لا يصح الحكم فيها إلا إذا علم أن كل مسبب فله سبب ، وفي قولنا ترجح أحد المتساويين يحتاج إلى مرجح هذا المعنى بعينه موجود ولكن بعبارة أخرى فإذا البرهان الذي أقامه مبنى على ما يتضمنه الحكم البديهى المذكور الذي عدل عنه إلى ذلك البرهان ، فقد وضح من ذلك أن ذلك البرهان فضلة غير محتاج إليه.
I say: The difference between our statement 'When one of two equals is preponderant, there is a preponderator' and our statement 'One is half of two' indicates that doubt may attach to the first, so it is not a complete determination—this is not correct. Because the difference can be due to the difference in the conceptualization of the subject and predicate, not the judgment itself. As for the judgment itself, it does not differ, as he also mentioned in the answer. And as for establishing a proof for that necessary judgment, it is nothing, because the necessity of the contingent that is negated for the existence of the thing described by it cannot be subsisting in its effecter, for it is an attribute of the contingent, and the attribute of a thing is impossible to subsist in other than it; and what subsists in the effecter, if it must be, is obligation (ījāb), not necessity (wujūb). The truth is that that necessity is an intellectual matter like other attributes, and it subsists in the concept of the contingent when judging its origination. And I say: One who sees that the proof he constructed is based on a judgment, which is the statement 'The contingent, as long as it is not necessary, does not exist'—this is a proposition that is not valid to judge unless it is known that every effect has a cause. In our statement 'Preponderance of one of two equals needs a preponderator' this same meaning is present but in other words. So if the proof he constructed is based on what is contained in the aforementioned self-evident judgment from which he turned away to that proof, then it is clear that that proof is superfluous and not needed.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary (perhaps by the author himself or a later editor) on the earlier proof that a contingent needs a preponderator. The argument is that the proof is redundant because its core premise—'the contingent, as long as it is not necessary, does not exist'—is itself a self-evident judgment. Trying to prove it by a separate argument (e.g., necessity being a positive attribute) is unnecessary. The author also points out that the difference between two self-evident propositions (like preponderance needing a preponderator vs. one being half of two) lies in conceptualization, not in the judgment's certainty. He further argues that the necessity (wujūb) of the contingent cannot inhere in the effecter; it is an intellectual attribute of the contingent when we judge its origination. Thus the whole proof is a 'superfluity' (fuḍla) not needed. This is a meta-critique of the argument's methodology.

Misconception: The proof that necessity of the contingent inheres in the cause is valid and needed.
Correction: Razi shows that necessity is an attribute of the contingent itself, not of the cause, and the proof merely restates a self-evident truth, making it redundant.

What does Razi conclude about the proof that the contingent needs a cause?
A) It is a necessary proof.
B) It is valid but incomplete.
C) It is redundant and not needed.
D) It contradicts self-evident truths.

Key Takeaway
Razi's critique shows that the proof for the necessity of a cause is redundant; the principle is self-evident and needs no proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself defending your faith with complex arguments, are you trusting in those arguments or in the self-evident truth of God's existence that they rest upon? Can you settle your heart on that foundational certainty without the scaffolding?
?
Razi says the proof is superfluous. Is there a belief you hold that you have been trying to 'prove' with elaborate reasoning, when deep down you already know it to be true? What would it cost you to simply admit that you know it directly, without the argument?
?
If you strip away every layer of philosophical justification for your faith, what remains that is undeniable to you? Are you willing to live from that place of raw certainty, even if it feels intellectually bare?

Effecter-ness: A Mental Relational Attribute
Imam Razi defends the mental existence of effecter-ness as a relational concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that effecter-ness (mu'aththiriyya) exists only in the mind and how this addresses the first objection.

Core Idea
Effecter-ness is a relation in the intellect, not a thing in the external world.

Imam Razi responds to a critical objection: if effecter-ness is not external, does that make it ignorance? He clarifies that it is a relational concept established in the mind when we conceive an effecter's action. This lesson unpacks his defense of the mental reality of effecter-ness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Claim
Read the passage and note that effecter-ness (mu'aththiriyya) is described as a relational matter (amr iḍāfī) that exists only in the intellect.
2
Understand the Relational Nature
Recognize that effecter-ness is not an attribute in external reality but a relation conceived when one thinks of an effecter producing an effect.
Compare to other relational concepts like paternity.
3
Distinguish Ignorance from Mental Existence
Notice that lack of external correspondence does not imply ignorance; ignorance is judging something exists externally when it does not.
The example of believing the world is eternal is ignorance because it's judged externally.
4
Address the Attribute Before Minds
Understand the rebuttal to the claim that effecter-ness is an attribute 'before minds'—the disposition that generates the relation is before minds, not the relation itself.
5
Distinguish Effecter-ness from Knowledge of It
See that effecter-ness and knowledge of effecter-ness are distinct mental entities, both established in the intellect but not identical.
This prevents circularity in the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعارضة الأولى فالمؤرية المذكورة فيها أمر إضافي يثبت في العقل عند تعقل صدور الأمر عن المؤثر، فإن تنقل ذلك يقتضى ثبوت أمر فى العقل هو المؤثرية كما فى سائر الإضافيات . وعدم مطابقته للخارج لا يقتضى كونه جهلا فإن ذلك إنما يكون جهلا إذا حكم بثبوته فى الخارج، ولم يثبت في الخارج اعتقاد كون العالم قديما مع كونه ليس بقديم الذي يمثل به فى الجهل يدل على ما ذكرنا لا على ما أورده في مثاله ، وعدم مطابقته لا يقتضى أيضا أن لا يكون شيء مؤرًا أصلا كما قال ، بل إذا حكم بثبوته في العقل فقط فمطابقته ثبوته في المقل دون الخارج . وقوله : المؤثرية صفة قبل الأذهان وصفة لشيء يستحيل قيامها بغيره . الجوابه أن كون الشيء بحيث لو دقله عاقل حصل لعقله إضافة الك الشيء إلى غيره هو الحاصل قبل الأذهان لا الذى يحصل في العقل ، فإن ذلك يستحيل أن يحصل قبل وجود العقل . وأما قوله : إلا أن يقال الوجود في الذهن هو العلم بالمؤثرية لكن ذلك لا يفيد لما تقدم ، فجوابه الصحيح أن المؤثرية غير العلم بالمؤثرية مع كونهما ثابتين في العقل لاما أحال عليه فيما تقدم ، والقول في باقي كلامه في فساد كون المزثرية ثبوتية ظاهر ما ذكرنا .
As for the first objection, the effecter-ness (al-mu'aththiriyya) mentioned therein is a relational matter (amr iḍāfī) that is established in the intellect when conceiving the issuance of the thing from the effecter. For that transference necessitates the establishment of a thing in the intellect, which is effecter-ness, as in other relational things. And its not corresponding to the external does not entail that it is ignorance, for that is only ignorance if one judges it to be existent externally. And not establishing it externally—the belief that the world is eternal while it is not eternal, which is used as an example of ignorance—indicates what we said, not what he mentioned in his example. And its not corresponding does not entail that there is no effecter at all, as he said; rather, if one judges its existence only in the intellect, then its correspondence is its existence in the intellect, not externally. And his statement: 'Effecter-ness is an attribute before minds (qabl al-adhhān) and an attribute of a thing, impossible to subsist in other than it.' The answer is that the thing being such that if a rational being considers it, an addition of the thing to another occurs in his intellect—that is what is 'before minds,' not what occurs in the intellect, for that is impossible to occur before the existence of the intellect. And as for his statement: 'Unless it is said that existence in the mind is knowledge of effecter-ness, but that does not benefit because of what preceded,' the correct answer is that effecter-ness is different from knowledge of effecter-ness, while both are established in the intellect, for what was earlier referred to regarding impossibility. And the statement on the rest of his words regarding the corruption of effecter-ness being positive is apparent from what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the first objection concerning the concept of effecter-ness (mu'aththiriyya). The author argues that mu'aththiriyya is a relational attribute (amr iḍāfī) that exists only in the intellect when one conceives the effecter's action. Its lack of external correspondence does not make it ignorance; ignorance is judging it externally false. The author defends that mu'aththiriyya is real in the mind, not in external reality. He then responds to the opponent's claim that mu'aththiriyya is an attribute 'before minds' (qabl al-adhhān). The author clarifies that what is 'before minds' is the disposition that gives rise to the relation in the mind, not the relation itself. Finally, he distinguishes between mu'aththiriyya and knowledge of mu'aththiriyya, emphasizing that both are mental but distinct. This defends the epistemological legitimacy of the concept in the proof.

Misconception: Effecter-ness must be an external attribute to be real or meaningful.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that effecter-ness is a valid mental relational concept that does not require external existence; its reality is in the intellect.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why does the lack of external correspondence of effecter-ness not constitute ignorance?
A) Because ignorance is only when one judges something to exist externally falsely.
B) Because effecter-ness is a universal concept that exists in all minds.
C) Because the objection is about the existence of the effecter, not the attribute.
D) Because ignorance cannot occur regarding relational attributes.

Key Takeaway
Effecter-ness is a valid mental concept that does not require external existence, and it is distinct from knowledge of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to admit that some of the most profound truths you hold—like God's causality—may be mental relations rather than physically observable facts? Does that disturb you or liberate you? What will you do with that discomfort when it arises in your prayer or daily life?
?

?
How often do you mistake a mental construct (like your image of 'success') for an external reality, causing you distress when it doesn't match? What one false judgment about your life or faith will you commit to correcting today based on this distinction?

The Possible in Itself is Equally Balanced
Razi proves the possible in itself is equally balanced and replies to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possible in itself must be equally balanced between existence and non-existence without an external cause, and how Razi defends this principle against objections.

Core Idea
A possible thing cannot favor its own existence without a cause.

Before we can understand causality in the world, we must first grasp the nature of the possible. Imam Razi presents a foundational argument: a thing that is possible in itself has no inherent inclination toward existence or non-existence. This may seem abstract, but it is the key to proving that everything that exists requires a cause. In this passage, Razi proves this through a reductio ad absurdum and then addresses a subtle objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the argument. Then read the translation carefully, noting the logical structure.
Pay attention to the conditional phrases: 'إذا كان', 'فإما', 'فإن'.
2
Follow the proof structure
Trace the main proof: Razi assumes one side is more appropriate, then examines whether the opposite side can occur. Identify the two branches and their implications.
The proof is a reductio: assuming the opposite leads to absurdity.
3
Understand the objection and response
Read the objection and Razi's answer. Note how he distinguishes between the possible in itself and the impossible due to another.
This clarifies that the argument applies only to genuine possibles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الممكن لذاته متساوى الطرفين
لأنه لا يجوز أن يكون أحد طرفيه أولى من الآخر، لأنه مع تلك الأولوية إما أن يمكن طريان الطرف الآخر أو لا يمكن، فإن أمكن فإما أن يكون طريانه لسبب أو لا لسبب فإن كان لسبب لم تكن تلك الأولوية كافية في بقاء الطرف الراجح بل لابد معها من عدم سبب الطرف المرجوح، وإن كان لا لسبب فقد وقع الممكن الموجود لا لعلة وهذا محال؛ لأن أحد [القساويين؟] أقوى من المرجوح، فلما امتنع الوقوع حال التساوى فلأن يمتنع حال المرجوحية كان ذلك أولى، وإن لم يمكن طريان المرجوح كان الراجح واجبا و المرجوح ممتنعا
(1) أول ما ذكره يقتضى نفى الأولوية مطلقا، ولقائل أن يقول: طرف الأولى يكون أكثر وقوعا وأشد [؟] الوقوع أو أهل شرطا للوقوع، وأنت ما أبطلت ذلك، وقد قيل في رجحان العدم في الموجودات الغير القارة كالصوت والحركة أن العدم لو لم يكن أولى بها لجاز عليها البقاء، وأجيب عنه بأن كلامنا في الممكن لذاته لا في الممتنع بغيره، وبقاء الغير القارة ممتنع لغيره.
Issue: The possible in itself is equally balanced between the two sides. Because it is not permissible that one of its sides be more appropriate than the other, because with that appropriateness, either the other side can occur or it cannot. If it can occur, then its occurrence is either due to a cause or not due to a cause. If it is due to a cause, then that appropriateness is not sufficient for the persistence of the preponderant side; rather, along with it, there must be the absence of the cause of the outweighed side. And if it is not due to a cause, then the possible existent has occurred without a cause, and this is impossible; because one of the two sides is stronger than the outweighed side. So when occurrence in the state of equalness is impossible, its being impossible in the state of being outweighed is even more so. And if the occurrence of the outweighed side is not possible, then the preponderant side is necessary and the outweighed side is impossible. (1) That which he first mentioned necessitates the negation of appropriateness absolutely. And it might be said: the side of appropriateness is more frequent in occurrence and more severe [?] in occurrence or more qualified as a condition for occurrence, and you have not invalidated that. And it has been said concerning the preponderance of non-existence in non-permanent existents such as sound and motion, that if non-existence were not more appropriate for them, persistence would be possible for them. And it was answered that our discussion is about the possible in itself, not about the impossible due to another, and the persistence of non-permanent things is impossible due to another.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the possible in itself is equally balanced between existence and non-existence without a preponderating factor. Razi argues by considering a scenario where one side is supposedly more appropriate. He analyzes whether the opposite side can occur or not. If it can occur, the occurrence is either caused or uncaused. If caused, the original appropriateness is insufficient; if uncaused, then the possible exists without a cause, which is absurd. Thus, if equality prevents occurrence without cause, then inequality (outweighing) would be even more preventing. If the opposite side cannot occur at all, then the preponderant side becomes necessary and the outweighed impossible, contradicting the nature of the possible. The footnote addresses an objection about absolute negation of appropriateness and the case of non-permanent existents. The answer clarifies that the discussion is about the possible in itself, not the impossible due to another, and the impossibility of persistence for non-permanent things is due to another cause.

Key Terms

المتساوي الطرفين
الراجح
المرجوح
الأولوية
طريان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The possible in itself can have an inherent preference for one side over the other.
Correction: Razi's proof shows that any apparent preference must come from an external cause; the possible itself is neutral.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's proof, why can a possible in itself not have one side more appropriate than the other?
A) Because the possible must be equally balanced by definition.
B) Because if it were more appropriate, it would either require a cause or lead to an uncaused occurrence, both absurd.
C) Because the possible is always accompanied by necessity.
D) Because the outweighed side can never occur.

Key Takeaway
The possible in itself has no inherent inclination; any outcome requires an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You exist right now—are you truly convinced that your existence is not due to any inherent quality in you, but entirely due to a cause outside yourself? How does this realization shift your understanding of your own contingency and dependence on your Creator?
?
If the possible in itself is equally balanced, then every moment you exist is a continuous gift of existence from God. What would change in your daily life if you lived with this constant awareness?
?
The proof shows that any preponderance must come from an external cause. Consider a habit or belief you hold strongly—can you trace its 'preponderance' in your life back to an external cause (e.g., upbringing, society, or divine guidance)? Are you willing to re-evaluate if that cause is worthy?

The Two Necessities Accompanying the Possible
Razi proves that the possible is always accompanied by necessity externally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possible is always accompanied by necessity both before and after existence, yet remains possible in itself.

Core Idea
The possible never escapes necessity, but that necessity is not internal to it.

Imam Razi presents a sophisticated argument: every possible being is surrounded by necessity on two sides—before it exists and after it exists. This might seem to undermine its possiblity, but Razi clarifies that these necessities are external, preserving the possible's essential contingency. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two distinct claims: one about a preceding necessity and one about a following necessity.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the first necessity (preceding)
Focus on the phrase 'ما لم يترجح صدوره عن المؤثر على لا صدوره عنه لم يوجد'. Razi says the possible's existence requires preponderance, which only occurs with necessity. This means the cause must necessitate the effect.
3
Identify the second necessity (following)
Now look at 'وجود ينافي عدمه فكان منافياً لإمكان عدمه فكان مستلزماً للوجوب'. Once the possible exists, its existence contradicts its non-existence, so non-existence becomes impossible—another kind of necessity.
This is a logical necessity, not a causal one.
4
Understand 'external not internal'
Razi concludes that these two necessities are 'outside' the possible's essence (خارجان لا داخلان). The possible does not become necessary in itself; it is always contingent in its own nature.
This preserves the concept of contingency while acknowledging the deterministic causal framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : رجحان الممكن لذاته مسبوق بوجوب وملحوق بوجوب
أما السابق فلأنه ما لم يترجح صدوره عن المؤثر على لا صدوره عنه لم يوجد، وقد دللنا على أن الراجح لا يحصل إلا مع الوجوب، وأما اللاحق فلان وجوده ينافي عدمه فكان منافيا لإمكان عدمه فكان مستلزما للوجوب.
واعلم أن شيئاً من الممكنات لا ينفك عن هذين الوجودين، لكنهما خارجان لا داخلان.
(2) أقول: قد مر تقرير هذين الوجهين والفرق بينهما فيما مضى، وما كان الممكن لذاته لا ينفك عن الوجود أو عن العدم فهو لا ينفك كل واحد من حاليه عن هذين الوجوبا لوجوده أو لعدمه شيئا منهما كما يقتضى أحد الطرفين لذاته، وهو معنى قوله لكنهما خارجان لا داخلان.
وهر لا يقتضى
Issue: The preponderance of the possible in itself is preceded by a necessity and followed by a necessity. As for the preceding, because as long as its emanation from the efficient cause has not been preponderated over its not emanating from him, it does not exist, and we have demonstrated that the preponderant does not occur except with necessity. As for the following, because its existence contradicts its non-existence, so it is contradictory to the possibility of its non-existence, and thus it entails necessity. And know that none of the possibles are free from these two existences, but they are external to it, not internal. (2) I say: The exposition of these two aspects and the difference between them has already passed. And whatsoever the possible in itself is not free from existence or non-existence, each of its states is not free from these two necessities for its existence or its non-existence (both of them) just as one of the two sides requires by itself. That is the meaning of his statement 'but they are external, not internal'. And it does not require [fragment]
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the preponderance of the possible is associated with necessity in two ways. First, before existence, the possible's emanation from the efficient cause must be preponderated over non-emanation, and this preponderance only occurs with necessity (the cause necessitates the effect). Second, after existence, the existence of the possible contradicts its non-existence, so it excludes the possibility of non-existence, thereby necessitating existence. Thus, the possible is always accompanied by necessity, but this necessity is external to its essence—it does not become necessary in itself. The footnote clarifies that these two necessities (the necessitating cause before and the impossibility of non-existence after) are external to the possible's essence; it remains possible in itself.

Key Terms
رجحان
مسبوق بوجوب
ملحوق بوجوب
الوجودان
خارجان لا داخلان
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The possible becomes necessary in itself once it exists or once its cause necessitates it.
Correction: Razi explicitly says the necessities are external (خارجان لا داخلان). The possible's essence remains possible; the necessity is from the cause or from the logical impossibility of non-existence after existence.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is the possible said to be 'followed by a necessity'?
A) Because after existence, it becomes necessary in itself.
B) Because its existence makes its non-existence impossible, entailing necessity.
C) Because it needs a cause to continue existing.
D) Because it becomes eternal.

Key Takeaway
The possible is always enveloped by necessity from its cause and from its own existence, but these necessities are external, preserving its essential contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is preceded and followed by necessity—both beyond your control—what does that reveal about your own power and independence? In light of this, how should you approach your daily reliance on Allah, the only Necessary Being?
?
You are a possible being: you could have not existed, and now that you exist, your non-existence is impossible for as long as you live. How does this double necessity make you feel about the gift of life? What responsibility does that impose on you right now?
?
The passage says the necessities are external, meaning your essence is still needy. When you act, do you often feel self-sufficient? How can you internalize the truth that every preponderance in your life—every choice, every breath—is only possible through an external necessity from Allah?

Razi's Verbal Resolution of the Eternal-Agent Disagreement
Razi presents and resolves the apparent conflict on attributing the eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi considers the disagreement between mutakallimun and philosophers on attributing the eternal to God to be verbal rather than substantive.

Core Idea
The seeming dispute is settled when we distinguish between the agent by essence and the free agent.

Razi presents a key insight: the mutakallimun and philosophers appear to disagree on whether the eternal can be attributed to an agent, but this disagreement is only verbal. In this lesson, we will explore Razi's analysis of both positions and his conclusion that both sides agree on the underlying reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how Razi states the mutakallimun's view and the philosophers' view on attributing the eternal.
Pay attention to the key terms: qadim, muhdath, fa'il, mujib bil-dhat, mukhtar.
2
Understand Razi's claim that the disagreement is verbal
Razi says the disagreement is lafzi (verbal). Find the sentence where he states this and note his reasoning: the mutakallimun did not prohibit attributing the eternal to the necessitating cause.
3
Recognize the real point of agreement
Razi concludes that both groups agree that the eternal can be attributed to the eternal necessitator but not to the free agent. Identify this conclusion in the passage.
This conclusion resolves the apparent conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خواص القديم والمحدث ( ) مسألة : اتفق المتكلمون على أن القديم يستحيل إسناده إلى الفاعل واتفقت الفلاسفة على أنه غير ممتنع زمانا ) فإن العالم قديم عندهم زماناً مع أنه فعل الله تعالى ، وعندى أن الخلاف في هذا المقام لفظي [؟]، لأن المتكلمين لم يمنعوا [؟] إسناد القديم إلى المؤثر الموجب بالذات ، ولذلك زعم مثبت الأحوال [؟] منا أن عالمية الله تعالى معللة [؟] بالعلم ، وزعم أبو هاشم أن العالمية والقادرية والحبيبة [؟] والموجودية معللة بحالة [؟] قديمان [؟]، مع خامسة مع أن الكل قديم، وزعم أبو الحسين أن العالمية حالة معللة بالذات، وهؤلاء وإن كانوا يمتنعون عن إطلاق لفظ القديم على هذه الأحوال لكنهم يعطون المعنى في الحقيقة (1) وأما الفلاسفة فإنهم إنما جوزوا إسناد العالم إلى البارى تعالى ، لكنه عندهم موجب بالذات حتى لو اعتقدوا فيه كونه فاعلا بالاختيار الماجوز [؟] واكونه موجد للعالم القديم، فظهر من هذا اتفاق الكل على جواز إسناد القديم إلى الموجب القديم وامتناع إسناده إلى المختار (۲) (1) أقول إنما ذهب المتكلمون إلى أن القديم يستحيل إسناده إلى الفاعل لا لقولهم علة الحاجة هو الحدوث، فإن هذا القول مختص ببعضهم كما مر لكن لقوطم [؟] بأن مأسوى الله تعالى وصفاته محدث وللأحوال التي ذكرها عند مثبتها ليست موجودة ولا معدومة فلا يوصف بالقدم على ما ذكره ، وفى تفسير القديم بما لا أول لوجوده إلا أن تغير التفسير وتقول القديم مالا أول لثبوته ، على أن الوجود والثبوت عنده مترادفان لكنا نقول ههنا ما قال المتكلمون وليس عند بعضهم معناهما واحداً وأبو الحسين لا يقول بالحال لكنه يقول العلم صفة الله قديمة معلقة بالذات وأما أصحاب أبي الحسن الأشعرى فيقولون بصفات قديمة لكنهم يقولون لا هى الذات ولا غيرها ، فلذلك لا يطلبون المعلولية عليها ، أو الحق في أن جميعهم أعطوا معنى القديم في الحقيقة على هذه الصفات معه فإن إبائهم عن إطلاق لفظ القديم عليها ليس بحقيق . (٢) أقول : اختلفوا أيضاً في معنى الاختيار فإن الفلاسفة يطلقون اسم المختار على الله تعالى ولكن لا بالمعنى الذي يفسر المتكلمون الاختيار به ، وذلك أنهم يقولون بوجوب صدور الفعل عنه تعالى دائما والمتكلمون ينفون دوام الصدور عنه ويقول بعضهم بوجوب الصدور نظراً إلى قدرته وإرادته، وينفى بعضهم وجوب الصدور عنه أصلا، ويقولون إنه تعالى يختار أحد الطرفين المتساويين على الآخر لا المرجح .
Properties of the eternal and the originated. ( ) Issue: The mutakallimun agreed that the eternal cannot be attributed to the agent, and the philosophers agreed that it is not impossible temporally. For the world is eternal according to them in time, even though it is an act of God. And in my view, the disagreement in this place is verbal: because the mutakallimun did not prohibit [did not prevent] the attribution of the eternal to the necessitating cause by essence. Therefore, the proponent of states [among us] claimed that God's being knowing is caused [?] by knowledge, and Abu Hashim claimed that being knowing, being powerful, being wise [?] and being existent are caused by a state [?] two eternals [?], with a fifth, even though all are eternal. And Abu al-Husayn claimed that being knowing is a state caused by the essence. And these, even though they refrain from applying the term 'eternal' to these states, they do give the meaning in reality (1). As for the philosophers, they only permitted attributing the world to the Creator, but according to them He is a necessitator by essence, so that even if they believed in Him being an agent by choice, they would not permit Him being the originator of the eternal world. So from this it is apparent that all agree on the permissibility of attributing the eternal to the eternal necessitator and the impossibility of attributing it to the free agent (2). (1) I say: the mutakallimun only held that the eternal cannot be attributed to the agent not because they say the cause of need is origination, for this saying is specific to some of them as previously, but because they say that everything other than God and His attributes is originated, and the states which they mention according to their proponents are neither existent nor nonexistent, so they are not described as eternal according to what he mentioned. And in the interpretation of 'eternal' as that which has no beginning to its existence, unless one changes the interpretation and says 'eternal' is that which has no beginning to its subsistence, given that existence and subsistence are synonymous according to him. But we say here what the mutakallimun said, and according to some of them they are not one meaning. And Abu al-Husayn does not hold the state but he says knowledge is an attribute of God, eternal, attached to the essence. As for the followers of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, they affirm eternal attributes, but they say they are neither the essence nor other, therefore they do not seek causation for them. The truth is that all of them gave the meaning of eternal in reality to these attributes, and their refusal to apply the term 'eternal' to them is not real. (2) I say: they also differed on the meaning of choice. The philosophers apply the name 'free agent' to God but not with the meaning by which the mutakallimun interpret choice. For they say the necessity of the act emanating from Him always, while the mutakallimun negate the perpetuity of emanation from Him. Some of them affirm the necessity of emanation considering His power and will, and some negate necessity of emanation from Him absolutely, and they say that He chooses one of two equal sides over the other without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's analysis of a key theological issue: whether the eternal (qadim) can be attributed to an agent (fa'il). The mutakallimun (theologians) held it impossible to attribute the eternal to an agent, while the philosophers allowed it, as they considered the world eternal yet an act of God. Razi begins by stating the two positions. He then gives his own view: the disagreement is verbal (lafzi). He explains that the mutakallimun did not actually deny attributing the eternal to the necessitating cause (mu'aththir mujib bil-dhat); they merely refrained from calling it 'agent' in that context. To illustrate, he mentions the positions of various scholars on divine attributes (such as 'being knowing' and 'being powerful') and their states (ahwal). Abu Hashim held that these attributes are caused by a state, while Abu al-Husayn held that 'being knowing' is a state caused by the essence. These scholars, despite refusing to call such states 'eternal', in reality grant them the meaning of eternity. The philosophers, on the other hand, permitted attributing the world to God only because they consider Him a necessitator by essence, not a free agent by choice. Thus, Razi concludes that everyone agrees that the eternal can be attributed to the eternal necessitator but not to the free agent (mukhtar). In the first footnote, Razi elaborates on why the mutakallimun took their position, clarifying that it is not based on the principle that 'need is caused by origination' (which some had held), but because everything besides God and His attributes is originated, and the states (ahwal) are neither existent nor nonexistent, so they cannot be described as eternal. He then discusses different interpretations of 'eternal' (qadim) and the views of al-Ash'ari's followers and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri on attributes and states. He affirms that all these theologians effectively attribute eternity to the divine attributes, even if they avoid the term. In the second footnote, Razi examines the disagreement on the meaning of 'choice' (ikhtiyar). The philosophers call God 'free agent' but understand it as necessary emanation, while the mutakallimun either affirm necessity based on power and will or deny any necessity and affirm that God chooses between equals without a preponderator. This shows that the central point is the concept of free agency, not just the term.

القديم
المحدث
الفاعل
الموجب بالذات
المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disagreement between mutakallimun and philosophers is substantive and cannot be resolved.
Correction: Razi argues the disagreement is merely verbal because both sides agree that the eternal can be attributed to the necessitating cause but not to the free agent.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the real point of agreement between the mutakallimun and the philosophers regarding attributing the eternal to God?
A) They agree that the eternal cannot be attributed to God at all.
B) They agree that the eternal can be attributed to the necessitating cause but not to the free agent.
C) They agree that the world is eternal and an act of God.
D) They agree that the disagreement is purely verbal and has no substance.

Key Takeaway
The eternal can be attributed to the necessitating cause but not to the free agent, a point on which all parties agree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth simply because of the label attached to it, losing sight of the meaning? This lesson shows scholars agreeing on essence while differing in words. Where in your life are you letting terminology prevent you from seeing the truth?
?
The mutakallimun and philosophers both affirmed God's eternal action, but one called it 'agent' and the other did not. Are you willing to accept God's transcendence even when it challenges your familiar categories? What would you need to let go of to embrace a deeper understanding?
?
Razi notes that even those who refrained from calling states 'eternal' still gave the meaning. Can you honestly say you prioritize the reality of God's attributes over the comfort of familiar terms? What would change in your worship if you focused more on meaning than on labels?

Why Mutakallimun Reject Calling Attributes 'Eternal'
Razi explains the mutakallimun's position on the eternity of attributes and states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's reasoning for not applying the term 'eternal' to the divine states (ahwal) and how they still effectively affirm their eternity.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun affirm the eternity of divine attributes but avoid the term 'eternal' due to the ontological status of states.

In this lesson, we delve into Razi's explanation of why the mutakallimun, while denying that the eternal can be attributed to an agent, nevertheless grant the reality of eternal attributes. Razi clarifies that their position is not based on the principle that need is caused by origination, but on the nature of states (ahwal) and the definition of 'eternal'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reason Razi rejects
Razi first dismisses the idea that the mutakallimun's position is based on the claim that 'the cause of need is origination'. Find this part in the passage.
This reason is specific to some mutakallimun, not all.
2
Learn the actual reason
The real reason: states are neither existent nor nonexistent, so they cannot be described as eternal. Identify this key point.
3
See the different views on eternal attributes
Razi mentions the definitions of qadim and the positions of Abu al-Husayn and the Ash'aris. Understand how each handles the issue.
All effectively give the meaning of eternity to the attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خواص القديم والمحدث ( ) مسألة : اتفق المتكلمون على أن القديم يستحيل إسناده إلى الفاعل واتفقت الفلاسفة على أنه غير ممتنع زمانا ) فإن العالم قديم عندهم زماناً مع أنه فعل الله تعالى ، وعندى أن الخلاف في هذا المقام لفظي [؟]، لأن المتكلمين لم يمنعوا [؟] إسناد القديم إلى المؤثر الموجب بالذات ، ولذلك زعم مثبت الأحوال [؟] منا أن عالمية الله تعالى معللة [؟] بالعلم ، وزعم أبو هاشم أن العالمية والقادرية والحبيبة [؟] والموجودية معللة بحالة [؟] قديمان [؟]، مع خامسة مع أن الكل قديم، وزعم أبو الحسين أن العالمية حالة معللة بالذات، وهؤلاء وإن كانوا يمتنعون عن إطلاق لفظ القديم على هذه الأحوال لكنهم يعطون المعنى في الحقيقة (1) وأما الفلاسفة فإنهم إنما جوزوا إسناد العالم إلى البارى تعالى ، لكنه عندهم موجب بالذات حتى لو اعتقدوا فيه كونه فاعلا بالاختيار الماجوز [؟] واكونه موجد للعالم القديم، فظهر من هذا اتفاق الكل على جواز إسناد القديم إلى الموجب القديم وامتناع إسناده إلى المختار (۲) (1) أقول إنما ذهب المتكلمون إلى أن القديم يستحيل إسناده إلى الفاعل لا لقولهم علة الحاجة هو الحدوث، فإن هذا القول مختص ببعضهم كما مر لكن لقوطم [؟] بأن مأسوى الله تعالى وصفاته محدث وللأحوال التي ذكرها عند مثبتها ليست موجودة ولا معدومة فلا يوصف بالقدم على ما ذكره ، وفى تفسير القديم بما لا أول لوجوده إلا أن تغير التفسير وتقول القديم مالا أول لثبوته ، على أن الوجود والثبوت عنده مترادفان لكنا نقول ههنا ما قال المتكلمون وليس عند بعضهم معناهما واحداً وأبو الحسين لا يقول بالحال لكنه يقول العلم صفة الله قديمة معلقة بالذات وأما أصحاب أبي الحسن الأشعرى فيقولون بصفات قديمة لكنهم يقولون لا هى الذات ولا غيرها ، فلذلك لا يطلبون المعلولية عليها ، أو الحق في أن جميعهم أعطوا معنى القديم في الحقيقة على هذه الصفات معه فإن إبائهم عن إطلاق لفظ القديم عليها ليس بحقيق . (٢) أقول : اختلفوا أيضاً في معنى الاختيار فإن الفلاسفة يطلقون اسم المختار على الله تعالى ولكن لا بالمعنى الذي يفسر المتكلمون الاختيار به ، وذلك أنهم يقولون بوجوب صدور الفعل عنه تعالى دائما والمتكلمون ينفون دوام الصدور عنه ويقول بعضهم بوجوب الصدور نظراً إلى قدرته وإرادته، وينفى بعضهم وجوب الصدور عنه أصلا، ويقولون إنه تعالى يختار أحد الطرفين المتساويين على الآخر لا المرجح .
Properties of the eternal and the originated. ( ) Issue: The mutakallimun agreed that the eternal cannot be attributed to the agent, and the philosophers agreed that it is not impossible temporally. For the world is eternal according to them in time, even though it is an act of God. And in my view, the disagreement in this place is verbal: because the mutakallimun did not prohibit [did not prevent] the attribution of the eternal to the necessitating cause by essence. Therefore, the proponent of states [among us] claimed that God's being knowing is caused [?] by knowledge, and Abu Hashim claimed that being knowing, being powerful, being wise [?] and being existent are caused by a state [?] two eternals [?], with a fifth, even though all are eternal. And Abu al-Husayn claimed that being knowing is a state caused by the essence. And these, even though they refrain from applying the term 'eternal' to these states, they do give the meaning in reality (1). As for the philosophers, they only permitted attributing the world to the Creator, but according to them He is a necessitator by essence, so that even if they believed in Him being an agent by choice, they would not permit Him being the originator of the eternal world. So from this it is apparent that all agree on the permissibility of attributing the eternal to the eternal necessitator and the impossibility of attributing it to the free agent (2). (1) I say: the mutakallimun only held that the eternal cannot be attributed to the agent not because they say the cause of need is origination, for this saying is specific to some of them as previously, but because they say that everything other than God and His attributes is originated, and the states which they mention according to their proponents are neither existent nor nonexistent, so they are not described as eternal according to what he mentioned. And in the interpretation of 'eternal' as that which has no beginning to its existence, unless one changes the interpretation and says 'eternal' is that which has no beginning to its subsistence, given that existence and subsistence are synonymous according to him. But we say here what the mutakallimun said, and according to some of them they are not one meaning. And Abu al-Husayn does not hold the state but he says knowledge is an attribute of God, eternal, attached to the essence. As for the followers of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, they affirm eternal attributes, but they say they are neither the essence nor other, therefore they do not seek causation for them. The truth is that all of them gave the meaning of eternal in reality to these attributes, and their refusal to apply the term 'eternal' to them is not real. (2) I say: they also differed on the meaning of choice. The philosophers apply the name 'free agent' to God but not with the meaning by which the mutakallimun interpret choice. For they say the necessity of the act emanating from Him always, while the mutakallimun negate the perpetuity of emanation from Him. Some of them affirm the necessity of emanation considering His power and will, and some negate necessity of emanation from Him absolutely, and they say that He chooses one of two equal sides over the other without a preponderator.

The Meaning of Divine Choice: Philosophers vs. Mutakallimun
Razi explains the different meanings of divine choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers and mutakallimun differ in their understanding of divine choice (ikhtiyar) and why this affects the attribution of the eternal to God.

Core Idea
The concept of choice differs: philosophers see it as necessary emanation, mutakallimun as free selection between equals.

Razi extends his analysis to the concept of choice (ikhtiyar). The philosophers call God 'free agent' but mean something different: necessary emanation. The mutakallimun, on the other hand, either affirm necessity based on power and will or deny any necessity and assert that God chooses between equals without a preponderator. This distinction underlies the verbal disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophical definition of choice
Read the passage. Note that philosophers interpret choice as the necessity of the act emanating from God always.
They use the term 'mukhtar' but mean something else.
2
Compare with the mutakallimun's definitions
The mutakallimun have two views: some affirm necessity through power and will, some negate necessity entirely and affirm selection between equals.
This shows the range within kalam.
3
Connect to the main thesis
Understand that the different meanings of choice are why the main disagreement about attributing the eternal becomes verbal: each side uses 'agent' differently.

He affirms that all these theologians effectively attribute eternity to the divine attributes, even if they avoid the term. In the second footnote, Razi examines the disagreement on the meaning of 'choice' (ikhtiyar). The philosophers call God 'free agent' but understand it as necessary emanation, while the mutakallimun either affirm necessity based on power and will or deny any necessity and affirm that God chooses between equals without a preponderator. This shows that the central point is the concept of free agency, not just the term.

Key Terms
الاختيار
المختار
الموجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers and mutakallimun mean the same thing by 'choice' when applied to God.
Correction: Philosophers mean necessary emanation, while mutakallimun mean free selection, often without a preponderator.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what do the philosophers mean by calling God 'mukhtar' (free agent)?
A) That He chooses between equals without a preponderator.
B) That His act emanates necessarily and always.
C) That He has power and will to choose.
D) That He temporally creates the world.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' understanding of choice as necessary emanation differs fundamentally from the mutakallimun's view of free selection, leading to the verbal disagreement on attributing the eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's freedom, do you lean more towards the philosophers' view (necessary emanation) or the mutakallimun's view (free selection)? What does that reveal about how you understand God's relationship to the world – and to your own life?
?
If God's choice is truly free, without any external compulsion, then your existence is a direct result of His unforced will. How does that change the weight of your gratitude? What would it mean to live as if every moment you are chosen by God?
?
The mutakallimun say God chooses without a preponderator – meaning no reason outside His own will determines His actions. Does this unsettle you? Do you secretly wish God were more predictable? Confess honestly: where do you want to control God rather than be controlled by Him?

Relation as a Conceptual Entity
The commentator establishes that real relation is an intellectual concept, not an external accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that real relation exists only in the intellect and why this does not lead to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Relation is a mental construct, not a real accident in the external world.

The commentator responds to the denial of relation by clarifying that relation is a conceptual category. He argues that while external events exist, the relations we perceive between them are generated in the mind. This lesson explores his key example of fatherhood and sonship to illustrate that conceptual relations do not entail an infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Focus on the overall argument before details.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the distinction between external and mental
Notice how the commentator distinguishes between external existence (the birth event) and the mental concept of relation (fatherhood/sonship).
This is key to understanding his position.
3
Examine the fatherhood example
How does the example of fatherhood and sonship demonstrate that relations are mental? Why does it not lead to infinite regress?
4
Reflect on the verification
The commentator says real relation exists only in the intellect. Ask yourself: what would change if relations were real accidents?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : لو كانت هذه المقولات نسبا لكانت أنواعا لجنس عال هو النسبة ولم تكن أجناسا عالية ، وهم لا يعنون بها ما يدخل النسبة فى ذاتها بل بما يعرض لها النسب إلا الإضافة ، فإن مفهومها النسبة ويستدعى تحكوار النسبة ، وأما كون الإضافة عرضا حالا في محل فحلولها فى ذلك المحل لا يكون إضافة ، بل الإضافة تفرض للحال إلى المحل والمحل إلى الحال بعد الحلول ، كما تفرض للرأس ولدى الرأس والتحقيق ههنا أن وجود الإضافة الحقيقية لا يكون إلا فى العقل ولا يكون فى الخارج إلا كون الموجود بحيث يحدث في العقل من تصوره الإضافة ، فإن ولادة شخص من شخص أمر موجود في الخارج ، وإذا تصوره العاقل يعقل أبوة في أحدهما وبنوة في الآخر ولا يلزم التسلسل ، لأن الأبوة إذا عرضت لشخص وإن كان ذلك المعروض إضافة أخرى لكنها لا تكون بأبوة أخرى فلا تتسلسل الأبوة وتلك الإضافة أيضاً أمر عقلى ولا تتسلسل، لأنها تنقطع عند وقوف العقل ، وهم يقولون إن لله تعالى صفات إضافية كالأول والآخر والخالق والرازق والمبدع والصانع وغير ذلك ، ويلتزمون القول بهذه الصفات غير المعية الزمانية الله تعالى وأما قوله : حصول الوجود للماهية إضافة بينهما فليس بشيء لأن الإضافة مهنا ليست إلا بمعنى الانضمام وليس ذلك ما نحن فيه ، وكون الشىء فى الزمان نسبية ككون الجسم في المكان الذى يقول بوجوده المتكلم ، وأما النسبة فيلحقها بعد ثبوتها، وأما التأثير فليس كل تأثير من هذه المقولة بل يريدون التأثير الصادر عن المؤثر في زمان غير قار الذات كقطع السكين اللحم ، فإن الجزءين لا يقعان في زمان واحد ، فالهيئة الحاصلة للسكين حين يقال له هوذا يقطع لا قبله ولا بعده هي المعنية بأن يفعل ، وقس عليه الانفعال. والنسبة إنما تعرض للعقل بين القاطع والمقطوع والإنصاف يقتضى أن ينقل مذاهب الخصوم على ما ذهبوا لئلا يلحق الناقلين شناعة بسبب سوء العقل .
I say: If these categories were relations, they would be species of a higher genus which is relation, and they would not be highest genera. But they do not intend by them that relation enters into their essence; rather, what occurs to them are relations, except for relation (al-idāfa) itself, for its concept is relation and it requires the determination of the relation. As for relation being an accident inhering in a locus, its inherence in that locus is not a relation; rather, relation is posited from the accident to the locus and from the locus to the accident after inherence, just as it is posited for the head and what is below the head. The verification here is that the existence of real relation is only in the intellect, and in the external world there is only the existence such that from its conception the relation is generated in the intellect. For example, the birth of a person from a person is an existent in the external world, and when the rational person conceives it, he understands fatherhood in one and sonship in the other, and no infinite regress follows, because when fatherhood occurs to a person, even if that subject is another relation, it is not by another fatherhood, so fatherhood does not regress, and that relation is also an intellectual matter and does not regress because it stops when the intellect stops. And they (the mutakallimun) say that God has relational attributes such as the First, the Last, the Creator, the Provider, the Originator, the Maker, and others, and they affirm these attributes other than temporal being-with (al-ma‘iyya al-zamāniyya). As for his statement 'the existence of the quiddity is a relation between them', it is nothing, because relation here is only in the sense of conjunction, and that is not what we are dealing with. And the thing's being in time is a relation, like the body's being in place, which the mutakallimun affirm. As for relation, it affects them after their existence. As for efficacy, not every efficacy is of this category; rather, they mean the efficacy issuing from the agent in a time that is not stable in essence, like the cutting of the knife through flesh, for the two parts do not occur in one time. So the state obtained for the knife when it is said 'it cuts'—neither before it nor after it—is what is meant by 'doing'. And analogously for undergoing (al-infi‘āl). And relation only occurs in the intellect between the cutter and the cut. Fairness requires that the doctrines of opponents be transmitted according to what they actually held, lest transmitters incur disgrace due to poor understanding.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This is a detailed commentary (a‘qūl) responding to the mutakallimun's denial of relational attributes. The commentator argues that relational attributes (al-idāfa) are not real accidents inhering in substances but are conceptual entities existing only in the intellect. He distinguishes between categories that might be considered relations and the specific category of relation itself. He gives the example of fatherhood and sonship: the external event (birth) is real, but the relations of fatherhood and sonship are generated in the mind when the event is conceived, and no infinite regress follows because these intellectual relations stop at the intellect's stopping point. He then notes that even the mutakallimun affirm relational attributes for God (e.g., Creator, First, Last) but deny temporal being-with (ma‘iyya). He addresses specific objections: (a) the claim that existence adds a relation to quiddity is rejected because 'relation' there means mere conjunction, not the technical relation; (b) the thing's being in time is like a body's being in place, which is a relation; (c) efficacy (ta’thīr) is not always a relational accident but refers to a process in time, like cutting, where the agent's state at the moment of cutting is the real action. The passage emphasizes that conceptual relations do not entail infinite regress and that misrepresenting opponents' views leads to error.

Key Terms
الإضافة
التسلسل
الأبوة
البنوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Relational attributes are real accidents inhering in God or substances.
Correction: The commentator asserts that real relation exists only in the intellect, not in external reality.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the example of fatherhood and sonship not lead to an infinite regress?
A) Because fatherhood is not a real attribute.
B) Because the relation stops when the intellect stops.
C) Because fatherhood is eternal and unchanging.
D) Because sonship cancels fatherhood.

Key Takeaway
Real relation is a mental construct that does not create an infinite chain because it terminates when the intellect stops.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that relations like fatherhood are merely mental concepts, not external realities. How does this affect the way you think about your own relationships—like being a parent, a child, or a spouse? Are you treating these bonds as ultimate realities or as intellectual constructs that should lead you to reflect on the Creator? What would change in your daily interactions if you reminded yourself that the true reality is only the external event (like birth) and the relation is a mental tool?
?
The commentator argues that there is no infinite regress of relations because the mind stops when it conceives the final relation. Think of a time when you kept overthinking a relationship—perhaps a grievance or a duty—and it seemed endless. How does this lesson invite you to 'stop the intellect' and not let mental relations spiral? Are there relations you are obsessing over that, in truth, are only in your mind? What would it mean to let them go?
?
If real relations exist only in the intellect, then what does that say about your relationship with Allah? The term 'Sonship' is used as an example, but for you, the relation of 'servant' to 'Lord' is also a mental construct. Does that make it less real, or more real? How can you ensure that your intellectual conception of being a servant is constantly generated from the external reality of your dependence on Him?

Applications of Relational Attributes in Theology
The commentator applies his view of relation to divine attributes and refutes alternative theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator applies his theory of conceptual relation to God's attributes, time, and efficacy.

Core Idea
Even relational attributes of God are conceptual, not causing multiplicity or change in the divine essence.

Having established that relations are conceptual, the commentator now addresses how this applies to divine attributes like 'Creator' and 'First'. He also refutes the notion that existence adds a real relation to quiddity, and discusses how time and efficacy involve relational concepts. Finally, he emphasizes the importance of accurately representing opponents' views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on God's relational attributes

They affirm relational attributes like Creator but deny temporal being-with.
2

Why does the commentator reject the idea that existence adds a relation to quiddity?
He says 'relation' there means mere conjunction, not the technical relation.
3
Understand time and efficacy as relational
How does the commentator explain time and efficacy as relational concepts, not as real accidents?
4

The Philosophers' Argument for Real Relations
Philosophers argue that relative attributes are real positive states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers prove that relative attributes like above-ness are real, positive attributes distinct from both privations and essential properties.

Core Idea
Relative attributes are real, positive states, not mere mental constructs or negations.

The philosophers (al-ḥukamā') offer a logical proof for the reality of relative attributes. Using the example of the sky being above the earth, they argue that such relations cannot be mere privations because they can come into existence while the subject remains unchanged. This lesson unpacks their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
The passage is a short but dense argument.
2
Identify the key moves
Notice how the argument progresses: observation of the relation's reality, rejection of privation, affirmation of positive attribute, and distinction from the essence.
Look for the logical connectors like 'because' and 'otherwise'.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider why this matters for kalam: if relative attributes are real, they require an explanation, and they are not reducible to the essence of things.
This connects to broader debates about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحكماء : فقد احتجوا على ثبوت هذه النسب بأن كون السماء فوق الأرض مثلا أمر حاصل سواء وجد الفرض والاعتبار أو لم يوجد وهو ليس أمرا عدميا لأن الشىء قد لا يكون فوقا ثم يصير فوقا ، فالفوقية التي حصلت بعد العدم لا تكون عدمية وإلا لكان نفى النفى عدميا وهو محال ، فالفوقية أمر ثبوتى وليست في نفس الذات، لأن الجسم من حيث إنه جسم غير مقول بالقياس إلى الغير ، ومن حيث إنه فوق مقول بالقياس إلى الغير، ولأن الشيء قد لا يكون فوق ثم يصير فوقا فالذات باقية فى الحالين والفوقية غير حاصلة في الحالين .
As for the philosophers, they argued for the existence of these relations by [saying] that the sky being above the earth, for example, is an obtainable matter whether assumption and consideration exist or not, and it is not a privative matter because a thing may not be above and then becomes above. So the above-ness that obtained after non-existence cannot be privative; otherwise the negation of negation would be privative, which is impossible. So above-ness is a positive matter and not in the essence itself, because a body insofar as it is a body is not predicated in relation to another, but insofar as it is above it is predicated in relation to another. And because a thing may not be above and then becomes above, while the essence remains in both states, and the above-ness is not obtained in both states.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (al-ḥukamā') proof for the existence of relative attributes (al-nisab). They use the example of the sky being above the earth. Their argument: the relation of above-ness is a real state (amr ḥāṣil) that exists whether or not someone conceives it. It is not a pure negation (amr ‘adamī), because the sky was not above and then became above; if it were merely privative, the change would be from one negation to another negation, which is impossible. Therefore, above-ness is a positive attribute (amr thubūtī). However, it is not in the essence of the substance itself, because the substance (sky) is not called 'above' in itself but only relative to another. The same substance persists while the relation changes, showing that the relation is an additional attribute. The proof follows a logical sequence: observation of change, rejection of privative explanation, affirmation of a positive attribute, and distinction from essential properties.

Key Terms
النسب
الفوقية
الأمر الثبوتي
الأمر العدمي
القياس إلى الغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Relative attributes are not real because they depend on the mind or are mere privations.
Correction: The philosophers argue that relative attributes are real and objective (ḥāṣil) independent of consideration, and they are positive (thubūtī) because they involve change from non-existence to existence, which privations cannot do.

Checkpoint
How do the philosophers prove that above-ness is a positive attribute rather than a privation?
A) By citing scripture.
B) By noting that a thing becomes above after not being above, which cannot be negation.
C) By general consensus.
D) By claiming it is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers prove that relative attributes are real positive states by showing they involve change from non-existence to existence, which cannot be mere privation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers argued that the sky being above the earth is a real relation, not just a mental construct. Consider a relationship in your life that you treat as merely conceptual or unimportant. How does it change your responsibility if you accept it as a real, objective state that exists before God? What would it mean to act on that reality today?
?
The passage shows that a relation can change while the essence remains the same. Is there a role or status in your life (like 'parent', 'spouse', 'student') that you have come to see as defining your very self? In light of this lesson, how can you hold that identity without letting it consume your essence? What must you return to God as your unchanging core?
?
If relative attributes are real but not in the essence, then actions like 'being a servant of God' are real but not your essence. Does your worship stem from a deep connection that goes beyond mere actions? What would it mean for your prayer to be a real relation that changes, yet your essence remains fixed on God? Are you willing to let go of your relational identity and stand before Him as pure essence?

Razi's Objection to Philosophers on Precedence
Razi objects to philosophers on precedence, using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the philosophers' view on temporal precedence leads to the absurd conclusion that an attribute (pastness) would have to subsist in a non-existent subject.

Core Idea
If 'pastness' is a real attribute, it would inhere in a non-existent day—impossible.

Imam Razi here presents a sharp critique of the philosophers' claim that relations like precedence are real attributes. He shows that such a view forces us to accept that a description like 'past' exists as an accident even after its subject has ceased to exist. This raises a fundamental problem: how can something exist in nothing? Let's walk through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the two distinct parts: the first short argument about simultaneity, and the longer second argument about the past day.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two separate objections. First, the simultaneity objection: if prior and posterior are real attributes, they would coexist. Second, the past-day argument: 'pastness' would be a real accident inhering in a non-existent subject.
The second argument is more detailed and involves the concept of 'positive attribute' (thubuti).
3
Understand the absurdity
Reflect on why an accident cannot subsist in a non-existent subject. This is a core principle in Islamic theology: accidents require an existent substrate.
This argument presupposes that 'pastness' is not a mere relation but a real quality—Razi is challenging that presupposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نقول : حجة الفلاسفة على إثبات النسب يقتضى كون المتقدم والمتأخر صفتين موجودتين وذلك محال ، لأن الإضافتين توجدان معا ومحلاهما يوجدان معا فالقبل موجود مع البعد هذا خلف، ولأنا نحكم على اليوم الماضى فى اليوم الحاضر بكونه ماضيا والمفهوم من كونه ماضيا ليس أمرا سلبيا لأنه صار ماضيا بعد ما لم يكن ماضيا فإذا هو ثبوتى ، وليس ثبوته في الذهن فقط ، فإنا لو فرضنا عدم الفرض والاعتبار فذلك اليوم ماض في نفسه وليس عبارة عن نفس ذلك اليوم ، لأنه حين كان حاضرا لم يكن ماضيا ، فيلزم أن يكون وصف كونه ماضيا عرضا حقيقيا قائما به حال عدمه فيكون الموجود قائما بالمعدوم وهو محال .
And we say: The philosophers' argument for the existence of relations requires that the prior (al-mutaqaddim) and the posterior (al-muta’akhkhir) be two existing attributes, and that is impossible, because the two relations exist together and their loci exist together, so the prior exists with the posterior—this is absurd (khulf). And because we judge the past day in the present day as being past, and the concept of its being past is not a negative matter because it became past after it was not past, so it is positive. And its positivity is not only in the mind, for if we assume the absence of assumption and consideration, that day is past in itself, and it is not identical with that day itself, because when it was present it was not past. So it follows that the description of its being past is a real accident subsisting in it at the time of its non-existence; thus the existent would subsist in the non-existent, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is the author's (Imam Razi's) own objection to the philosophers' argument for relative attributes, focusing on the case of temporal precedence and posterity. The argument has two parts: (1) If prior and posterior are real attributes, then they would both exist simultaneously (since the prior exists at the same time as the posterior?), leading to contradiction. (2) Take the example of a past day: we now judge it as past. The concept 'past' is not a mere negation because the day became past after not being past; hence it is a positive attribute. This attribute ('pastness') cannot be merely mental because even without anyone thinking about it, the day is past in itself. But if 'pastness' were a real accident, it would have to exist at the time when the day itself no longer exists (since the day is gone). That would mean an accident subsists in a non-existent subject, which is impossible. Therefore, the philosophers' position leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
المتقدم والمتأخر
السلب والإيجاب
العدم
الثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author denies temporal succession or that things are past.
Correction: The author does not deny that events are past; he denies that 'pastness' is a real accident that inheres in a non-existent subject. He affirms the common-sense notion of past events but rejects the philosophical reification of relations.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the philosophers' proof for precedence and posteriority (al-taqaddum wa al-ta’akhkhur) impossible?
A) Because it implies that God has temporal attributes.
B) Because it requires an accident to subsist in a non-existent subject.
C) Because it contradicts the Quran.
D) Because it leads to polytheism.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi argues that if temporal precedence were a real attribute, it would require an accident to subsist in a non-existent subject, which is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your own life: In what ways do you habitually attach reality to categories like 'past regret' or 'future anxiety', treating them as if they exist as separate entities? How does Razi's argument expose the illusion of thinking that the 'pastness' of your sins is a real attribute still clinging to you? Today, can you release that illusion and stand before Allah as if you are free of the past's supposed weight?
?
You regularly refer to 'the past day' as something fixed and real. But Razi insists that its description as 'past' cannot be a real accident inhering in it. What does this imply about your use of time? Are you living as if the past has an ontological grip on the present? How can you use this logical insight to make your present moment the only reality that matters for your obedience to Allah?
?
The philosophers argued for real attributes; Razi said this leads to absurdity. What 'attributes' have you been assuming exist in your own failings or strengths that might be mere judgments, not realities? Name one 'attribute' you have attached to yourself (e.g., 'I am a failure' or 'I am wise') that may not be a real accident but a relational judgment.
