Accidental Motion of a Contained Object
Discusses motion of contained object in moving container.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential and accidental motion in the context of a contained object in a moving container, and how different definitions of 'place' affect the judgment.

Core Idea
Motion can be essential or accidental; a contained object moves accidentally when its container moves.

This lesson examines a classic kalam dispute: does an object placed inside a moving container also move? The passage presents the strongest view that it moves accidentally, not essentially. The commentator clarifies how different conceptions of 'place' lead to different conclusions. Let's read the source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic claims.
Note that the text has an editorial comment in parentheses.
2
Identify the two positions
The main text states the closest view: the object moves accidentally. The commentator then explains two perspectives: one where the object does not move, and one where it moves accidentally.
3
Examine the definition of 'place'
The commentator distinguishes between place as the inner surface of the container (where the object does not leave its place) and place as a reference point for indication (where the object changes its relation).
This distinction is key to understanding the disagreement.
4
Reflect on essential vs accidental motion
Consider how this example illustrates the kalam concept of accidental motion: the object's motion depends on the container's motion, not its own essence.
This shows that motion can be attributed in two ways.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اختلفوا فى أن المحوى حال استقراره في الحاوى المتحرك) هل يكون متحركا، والأقرب أنه متحرك بالمرض لا بالذات . (أقول : إنه ليس بمتحرك عند من يجعل المكان المسطح الباطن من الحاوى لأنه لم يفارق مكانه ومتحرك باعتبار تعين الإشارة إليه بشيء واحد ، فلذلك قيل إنه متحرك بالعرض أنه يتبعه الخير لا بالذات من حيث لم يفارق مكانه)
Issue: They disagreed about whether the contained object, when it is settled in the moving container, is moving. The closest view is that it is moved incidentally ('cause'? likely 'accidentally') not essentially. (I say: It is not moving according to those who consider the place as the inner concave surface of the container, because it does not leave its place. It is moving with respect to the determination of indication to it by one thing; therefore it was said it is moved accidentally because it follows the other, not essentially, in that it does not leave its place.)
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute about the motion of an object (al-maḥwā) contained within a moving container (al-ḥāwī). The main text states the strongest view is that it moves accidentally (bi al-ʻaraḍ), not essentially (bi al-dhāt). The commentator then clarifies: For those who define place as the inner surface of the container, the object does not leave its place and thus does not move. However, with respect to a fixed reference point (taʻayyun al-ishārah), it can be considered moving accidentally because it follows the movement of the container, not because it leaves its own place essentially.

Correction: The passage shows that motion can be accidental, where the object's motion depends on the motion of its container.

B) Because it does not leave its place as defined by the inner surface of the container
C) Because motion requires a change of essence
D) Because it follows the container essentially

Key Takeaway
A contained object can be said to move accidentally when its container moves, depending on the definition of place.

?

Can One Knowledge Be of Two Knowns?
The author argues against one knowledge being of two knowns and responds to counterarguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that one knowledge cannot be of two knowns when knowledge is defined as relation, and how he refutes the opposing distinction and subsequent objections.

Core Idea
One knowledge can only relate to one known if knowledge is the relation itself.

This lesson presents a central debate in kalam epistemology: can a single act of knowledge encompass two objects? The author takes a nuanced position, distinguishing between knowledge as the relation itself and knowledge as something that necessitates relation. He then examines a proposed distinction by those who permit multiple knowns and refutes it, followed by a commentary discussing various scholarly views.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem and definitions
Read the opening where the author sets up the issue and defines two interpretations of knowledge: as the relation itself or as what necessitates relation.
Notice that the distinction is crucial for the argument.
2
Follow the author's argument
The author argues that if knowledge is the relation itself, one knowledge cannot be of two knowns because of the possibility of obliviousness.
Consider why obliviousness proves distinction in relation.
3
Examine the opponents' distinction
Some scholars distinguish between inseparable knowns and separable knowns, claiming that one knowledge can cover the former but not the latter.
The author refutes this with the example of blackness and whiteness.
4
Review the commentary and objections
The aqul section discusses views of various scholars and raises objections to the author's argument, including the issue of whole and parts.
Pay attention to how the objections are answered implicitly.

مسألة : اختلفوا فى أن العلم الواحد هل يكون علما بمعلومين
وعندى أنا إن فسرنا العلم بنفس التعلق لم يصح ذلك لأنه يصح أن يعقل كون الشيء عالما بأحد المعلومين مع الذهول عن كونه عالما بالآخر ولولا التغاير لا يصح ذلك ، وإن فسرناه بما يوجب التعلق لم يمتنع لأن العالم المتعلق بكون السواد مضاد البياض إن لم يكن هو بعينه متعلقاً بهما لم يكن متعلقاً بالمضادة التي بينهما مطلق المضادة،
وليس كلامنا في ذلك العلم بل فى العلم المتعلق بالمضادة المخصوصة ، وإن كان متعلقا بهما فهو المطلوب . ثم المجوزون منهم من فصل فقال كل معلومين يصح أن يعلم أحدهما مع الذهول عن الآخر امتنع تعلق العلم الواحد بهما ، وكل معلومين لا يصح العلم بأحدهما مع الذهول عن الآخر يجب أن يعلما بعلم واحد وهذا الفصل باطل عندى، لأن العلم بمضادة السواد والبياض لما ثبت أنه متعلق بالسواد والبياض مع أنه يصح أن يعلم السواد وحده مع الجهل بالبياض فقد تعلق ذلك العلم بأمرين يصح العلم بأحدهما مع الجهل بالآخر

أقول: العلم القديم عند أهل السنة يتعلق بمعلومات الله تعالى التي لا نهاية لها مع أنه واحد وهذا البحث يتعلق بالعلم المحدث، فقال أبو الحسن الباهلى أن العلم الواحد يجوز أن يتعلق بمعلومات كثيرة وحكى عن أبي الحسن الأشعرى ذلك وأنكره الأستاذ أبو إسحاق وقال إنه ذكره في الإلزام على من يقول العلم الواحد يتعلق بمعلومين وذهب الجبائي إلى جواز تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومين وأوجب ذلك من أهل السنة أبو منصور البغدادي، قال القاضي أبو بكر كل معلومين لا ينفك أحدهما عن الآخر في العقل يجوز أن يتعلق بهما علم واحد وكل ما يصح أن يعلم مع الذهول عن شيء آخر فلا يمكن أن يتعلق بهما علم واحد ويقال للمصنف إذا فسرت العلم بالتعلق جاز تعلق العلم بالمجموع ويكون الأجزاء داخلا فيه وحينئذ قد تعلق بأمرين وأنت حكمت بامتناع ذلك وأنت استدلت على الامتناع بصحة تعلق العلم بأحد المعلومين مع الذهول عن كونه عالما بالآخر وههنا لا يصح هذا الاستدلال وأيضا كان يجب أن يقول مع الذهول عن الآخر وأنت قلت مع الذهول عن كونه عالما بالآخر وذلك لأن المطلوب ههنا التعلق بمعلومين لا معلوم وبالعلم أيضا، وأيضا على تقدير تفسير العلم بما يوجب التعلق جعل العلم بمطلق المضادة غير متعلق بشيئين وذلك غير معقول فإن المضادة لا تعقل إلا بين شيئين بل يكون الشيئان شاملين ككل ما يقع عليه اسم الشيئية ، ولا فرق بين المضادة المطلقة والمضادة المخصوصة إلا بعدم التعيين ووجود التعيين فيما تتعلق المضادة بهما ، ولا يختلفان من حيث تعلقها معلومين ، وإبطال قول المجوزين بقوله العلم بالسواد والبياض تتعلق بأمرين يصح العلم بأحدهما مع الجهل وبالآخر غير صحيح لأن كلامهم في المضادة المتعلقة بهما وتصور السواد وحده غير تصور السواد المضاد للبياض ، فليس ما يصبح العلم مع الجهل بالآخر و أحد الشيئين اللذين يتعلق بهما معا
Issue: They disagreed about whether one knowledge can be knowledge of two knowns.
According to me, if we interpret knowledge as the relation itself, this is not correct because it is possible to conceive of a thing knowing one known while being oblivious to it being knowing the other; were it not for the difference (taghāyur), that would not be possible. And if we interpret it as what necessitates the relation, it is not impossible because the knowledge that relates to blackness being opposite of whiteness, if it is not itself related to both, then it is not related to the opposition between them absolutely. But our discussion is not about that knowledge, but about knowledge related to the particular opposition. If it is related to both, that is the desired conclusion. Then some of those who permit it made a distinction: any two knowns such that one can be known with obliviousness to the other, one knowledge cannot be attached to both; and any two knowns such that one cannot be known with obliviousness to the other, they must be known by one knowledge. This distinction is false according to me, because the knowledge of the opposition of blackness and whiteness, since it is established that it is related to blackness and whiteness while it is possible to know blackness alone with ignorance of whiteness, thus that knowledge is related to two things that can be known with one of them with ignorance of the other.

I say: Eternal knowledge (al-ʿilm al-qadīm) according to the Ahl al-Sunnah relates to the infinite objects of God's knowledge while being one, but this discussion concerns temporal knowledge (al-ʿilm al-muḥdath). Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili held that one knowledge can relate to many knowns, and that was narrated from Abu al-Hasan al-Ashʿari; but al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq denied it and said he mentioned it in al-Ilzam against those who say one knowledge relates to two knowns. Al-Jubba'i held the permissibility of one knowledge relating to two knowns, and among the Ahl al-Sunnah Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi affirmed it. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr said: any two knowns such that one cannot be separated from the other in the intellect, it is permissible that one knowledge relate to both; but anything that can be known with obliviousness to something else, one knowledge cannot relate to both. And it is said to the author: if you interpret knowledge as relation, then it is permissible for knowledge to relate to the whole, and the parts are included in it, and thus it has related to two things, while you ruled that impossible. You argued for impossibility based on the validity of knowledge relating to one of the two knowns with obliviousness to being knowing the other. But here this argument does not hold. Also, you should have said 'with obliviousness to the other', but you said 'with obliviousness to being knowing the other' because the required here is relation to two knowns, not to a known and to knowledge. Also, on the interpretation of what necessitates relation, you made the knowledge of absolute opposition not related to two things, which is unintelligible because opposition is only conceived between two things. Rather, the two things are inclusive like everything that falls under the name of 'thingness'. There is no difference between absolute opposition and particular opposition except by lack of determination and the presence of determination in what the opposition relates to. They do not differ in terms of being related to two knowns. And his refutation of those who permit it by saying 'knowledge of blackness and whiteness relates to two things such that knowledge of one is possible with ignorance of the other' is not correct because their discussion is about the opposition related to them, and the conception of blackness alone is not the conception of blackness as opposite of whiteness. So it is not what he thought, i.e., that knowledge is possible with ignorance of the other is one of the two things to which the knowledge relates.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This lesson is a detailed kalam argument (mas'ala) on whether a single act of knowledge (ʿilm wāḥid) can be knowledge of two known objects (maʿlūmayn). The author begins by stating the problem: scholars disagree on this. Then he gives his own position: if knowledge is defined as the relation (al-taʿalluq) itself, then one knowledge cannot be of two knowns because one can conceive of knowing one object while being oblivious to knowing the other; if there were no real distinction between the two relations, this would not be possible. If, however, knowledge is defined as something that necessitates the relation (mā yūjibu al-taʿalluq), then it is not impossible because the knowledge that relates to blackness being opposite of whiteness, if it is not the very same thing related to both, then it cannot be related to the opposition between them. But the author clarifies that the discussion is about knowledge related to a specific opposition (al-muḍādda al-makhṣūṣa), not about absolute opposition. Then he reports an attempted distinction by some who permit one knowledge for two knowns: they say that if two knowns can be known separately, then one knowledge cannot cover both; but if they cannot be known separately, then one knowledge must cover both. The author refutes this by example: knowledge of the opposition between blackness and whiteness is related to both, yet blackness can be known alone without whiteness. Thus the distinction fails.

The passage then includes a long comment (aqūl) that discusses various scholarly positions on this issue. The comment begins by distinguishing between God's eternal knowledge (al-ʿilm al-qadīm), which relates to infinite objects while being one, and temporal knowledge (al-ʿilm al-muḥdath), which is the subject of this discussion. It then cites several scholars: Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili and Abu al-Hasan al-Ashʿari (according to some) permitted one knowledge for many knowns; Abu Ishaq denied it; al-Jubba'i permitted it; Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi affirmed it; al-Qadi Abu Bakr made a conditional distinction. The comment then raises objections to the author's own argument. First, if knowledge is defined as relation, then the relation to a whole (majmūʿ) includes the parts, so it does relate to two things, contradicting the author's claim of impossibility. Second, the author's argument using obliviousness is flawed because in the case of a whole, one cannot be oblivious to the parts. Third, the author should have said 'with obliviousness to the other' rather than 'with obliviousness to being knowing the other' because the issue is relation to two knowns, not to one known and to knowledge itself. Fourth, absolute opposition is meaningless unless between two things, so the author's attempt to make absolute opposition not related to two things is incoherent. Finally, the author's refutation of those who permit it using the example of blackness and whiteness is incorrect because their discussion is about the specific opposition, not about blackness alone. The conception of blackness alone is not the same as the conception of blackness as opposed to whiteness.

Key Terms
علم واحد
معلومين
تعلق
التعليق
مضادة
الذهول
علم قديم
علم محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of one knowledge for two knowns applies equally to God's eternal knowledge.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that eternal knowledge relates to infinite objects while being one, and this discussion is confined to temporal knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for one knowledge (defined as relation) to be of two knowns?
A) Because knowledge is a simple attribute that cannot be divided.
B) Because one can know one object while being oblivious to knowing another.
C) Because the known objects are always multiple by nature.
D) Because the relation to two objects would require the knowledge to be composite.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of one knowledge covering two knowns depends on how knowledge is defined and whether the knowns are separable; the author holds it impossible when knowledge is the relation itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you honestly claim to have ever known two things with a single act of knowledge, or does each known object demand its own distinct awareness? How does this affect the way you trust your own understanding of God's unity?
?
When you consider God's knowledge—one yet encompassing everything without division—does it make you feel secure or distant? In your daily life, do you act as if God's knowledge of your smallest deeds is truly present, or do you forget? What would change if you remembered it constantly?
?
The distinction between temporal and eternal knowledge was noted: your knowledge is limited, God's is not. Does this humble you, or do you secretly wish your own knowledge could be like His? How can you turn this lesson into a real step toward accepting your limits and trusting His infinity?

General Knowledge: Known and Unknown Aspects
The author explains that general knowledge is partially known and partially unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why general knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ijmālī) is considered known in one aspect and unknown in another, and why it is not a separate type of knowledge.

Core Idea
General knowledge is not a separate type but a combination of known and unknown aspects.

A common misconception in Islamic epistemology is that general knowledge is a distinct type of knowledge, separate from detailed knowledge. In this passage, the author clarifies that general knowledge is actually a partial knowledge: it is known in one respect and unknown in another. The known aspect is clear and unambiguous, while the unknown aspect is entirely unknown. The comment that follows reinforces this distinction and connects it to earlier discussions. Understanding this point resolves the confusion and provides a solid foundation for further theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the author's main claim. Pay attention to the terms 'general knowledge' and 'detailed knowledge'.
2
Identify the two aspects
The author states that an object known generally has two aspects: a known aspect and an unknown aspect. These aspects are distinct. The known aspect has no ambiguity, while the unknown aspect is completely unknown.
The key here is that the two aspects are separate and do not mix.
3
Understand the misconception
The author points out that because both aspects are combined in one thing, people mistakenly think that general knowledge is a special type of knowledge. The correct view is that it is simply a partial knowledge, not a different category.
This error arises from conflating the object with the aspects.
4
Reflect on the commentator's remark
The commentator (the author speaking in the sharh) notes that the thing known-in-one-aspect is distinct from the two aspects themselves. This reinforces the idea that the object is not identical to either aspect.
This connects to earlier discussions about conceptualization (taṣawwur) not being acquired.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : المعلوم على سبيل الجملة معلوم من وجه مجهول من وجه
والوجهان متغايران ، فالوجه المعلوم لا إجمال فيه، والوجه المجهول غير معلوم البتة، لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد ظن أن العلم الجملى نوع يغاير العلم التفصيلي

أقول : اعترف ههنا بأن الشيء المعلوم من وجه والمجهول من وجه يغاير الوجهين وهذا ماذكرته في صدر الكتاب عند إبطال قوله التصور ليس مكتسب، ومطلوبه ههنا بيان تغاير الوجهين ، لكن حصل منه وجوب تغاير ما اجتمع فيه الوجهان والوجهين
Issue: The known in a general way is known in one aspect and unknown in another aspect. The two aspects are distinct. The known aspect has no ambiguity in it, and the unknown aspect is not known at all. But because they are combined in one thing, it was thought that general knowledge is a type distinct from detailed knowledge.

I say: He admits here that the thing known in one aspect and unknown in another aspect is different from the two aspects. This is what I mentioned at the beginning of the book when refuting his statement that conceptualization (taṣawwur) is not acquired. His goal here is to show the distinctness of the two aspects, but from it arises the necessity of the distinctness of that in which the two aspects and the aspects themselves are combined.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This lesson addresses a common misconception about general knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ijmālī). The author states that an object known in a general way is known in one respect (wajh) and unknown in another. The two respects are distinct (mutaghāyirān): the known respect has no ambiguity, while the unknown respect is entirely unknown. However, because both respects are combined in a single object, people erroneously think that general knowledge is a special type of knowledge different from detailed knowledge (al-ʿilm al-tafṣīlī). In reality, it is simply a partial knowledge. The comment (aqūl) adds that the author acknowledges that the object known-in-one-aspect is distinct from the two aspects themselves. The commentator refers to an earlier passage where he refuted the claim that conceptualization (taṣawwur) is not acquired. The purpose here is to show the distinctness of the two aspects, but the consequence is that the thing that combines both aspects is also distinct from the aspects.

Key Terms
معلوم
إجمال
وجه
علم جملى
علم تفصيلي
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: General knowledge is a distinct type of knowledge different from detailed knowledge.
Correction: General knowledge is not a separate type; it is simply knowledge that is known in one aspect and unknown in another. The known aspect has no ambiguity, and the unknown aspect is entirely unknown. The combination of both aspects in one object leads to the illusion of a distinct type.

Checkpoint
Why do some people mistakenly consider general knowledge a distinct type from detailed knowledge?
A) Because they think the known aspect has ambiguity.
B) Because both known and unknown aspects are combined in one thing.
C) Because general knowledge always precedes detailed knowledge.
D) Because the unknown aspect is impossible to know.

Key Takeaway
General knowledge is known in one aspect and unknown in another; it is not a distinct type of knowledge but a partial knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author says the unknown aspect is 'not known at all.' Is there a command of Allah that you have avoided learning about in detail, content with a vague sense of knowing? What step will you take today to move from ignorance to clarity?

All Sciences Are Necessary
Imam Razi asserts that all sciences are necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that all genuine knowledge must be absolutely certain, either immediately or through necessary entailment.

Core Idea
Certainty is the essence of knowledge; any possibility of error destroys it.

Imam Razi begins a foundational discussion on the nature of knowledge by asserting that all sciences (or knowledge in general) are necessary—meaning indubitable and certain. In this short but dense passage, he provides a logical proof based on the dichotomy of immediate versus derived knowledge, and the condition that for derived knowledge to be valid, the entailment must be absolutely necessary. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and clarifies what it means for knowledge to be 'necessary.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation to get the gist.
Notice the concise style; every word matters.
2
Identify the claim and its structure
The passage states a claim (all sciences are necessary) and gives a reason. Identify the two parts: the division into immediate and derived, and the proof by impossibility of non-entailment.
3
Analyze the logical condition
Focus on the phrase 'if there remains any possibility of non-entailment, it would not be knowledge.' Why does any possibility destroy certainty?
This is the core of the argument: certainty is all-or-nothing.
4
Connect to the broader lesson
Reflect on how this definition of knowledge influences the rest of Islamic theology and philosophy.
This passage sets a very high bar for what counts as knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : العلوم كلها ضرورية
لأنها إما ضرورية ابتداء أو لازمة عنها لزوما ضروريا فإنه إن بقى احتمال عدم اللزوم ولو على أبعد الوجوه لم يكن علم ، وإذا كان كذلك كانت بأثرها ضرورية (2)
Issue: All sciences are necessary. Because they are either originally necessary or necessarily entailed by them; for if there remains any possibility of non-entailment, even in the most remote manner, it would not be knowledge. And if so, they are all necessary (2).
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines all sciences (or knowledge) as necessary. The proof is a hypothetical syllogism: knowledge is either immediate (necessary in itself) or derived from immediate knowledge in a necessary way. If there were any possibility that the entailment might not hold, the derived knowledge would not be certain. Therefore, all knowledge is necessary, meaning indubitable and certain.

Key Terms
العلوم
ضرورية
ابتداء
لازمة
لزوم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some sciences are probabilistic or conjectural and still qualify as knowledge.
Correction: Imam Razi defines knowledge as absolutely certain; any element of probability or conjecture would disqualify it from being knowledge in the strict sense. The passage insists on necessary entailment without any possibility of failure.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if there remains a possibility that entailment fails?
A) Knowledge becomes probable
B) It would not be knowledge
C) It becomes speculative
D) It remains necessary

Key Takeaway
Genuine knowledge must be absolutely certain; any possibility of error, no matter how remote, disqualifies a claim from being knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any religious belief you hold that you have never examined for certainty—something you accepted because 'it sounds right' or 'most people believe it'? Write down one such belief and decide today to trace it back to either immediate knowledge or necessary entailment. Would you be willing to let it go if you find a possibility of error?
?
When you learn a new Islamic ruling or theological point, do you usually ask yourself 'Is this absolutely certain?' or do you settle for 'This is probably true'? If you find yourself in the latter category, what concrete step can you take this week to verify one such teaching until you know it necessarily?
?
Consider a time you were confident about something in your faith, but later discovered a possibility of doubt. How did that affect you? Now, based on this lesson, what would it mean for you to rebuild that knowledge on necessity? Commit to one idea you will seek to know with absolute certainty.

The Consistency of Knowledge Types: Principle and Branch
Imam Razi states an agreed rule about epistemic consistency between principle and branch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of a principle and its derived branch must share the same epistemic status (both necessary or both acquired) to avoid logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a principle and its branch cannot be of opposite natures—otherwise doubt destroys certainty.

This notice from Imam al-Razi highlights an agreed-upon logical rule in Islamic epistemology. When we have a foundational proposition (the principle) and a derived proposition (the branch), their epistemological status must match. If the principle is merely acquired (kasbī) while the branch is considered necessary (ḍarūrī), any doubt in the principle would undermine the branch, leading to contradiction. Let’s examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without getting caught up in every detail. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Identify the key terms
Make sure you understand what 'principle' (أصل), 'branch' (فرع), 'acquired' (كسبي), and 'necessary' (ضروري) mean in this context.
These terms are fundamental in Islamic epistemology.
3
Follow the logical argument
Trace the conditional chain: if the principle is acquired and the branch necessary, then doubt in the principle would cause doubt in the branch. This leads to the absurd conclusion that necessary knowledge becomes non-necessary.
The contradiction shows the assumption is false.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why must both be the same type? The argument shows that mixed types lead to logical inconsistency. This is a key principle in kalam and logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : اتفقوا على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون العلم بالأصل كسبيا وبالفرع ضروريا وإلا فعند وقوع الشك يحصل الشك في الفرع فيصير الضروری غير ضرورى هذا خلف (3)
Notice: They agreed that it is not permissible for knowledge of the principle to be acquired and knowledge of the branch to be necessary; otherwise, when doubt occurs, doubt would arise in the branch, and the necessary would become non-necessary, which is absurd (3).
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This is a logical notice (tanbih) about the consistency of knowledge types. The principle (aṣl) is the foundational knowledge, and the branch (farʿ) is dependent on it. If the principle is acquired (kasbī, not certain) while the branch is necessary (ḍarūrī, certain), then if doubt arises in the principle, it would propagate to the branch, making the necessary knowledge uncertain—a contradiction. Therefore, both must be of the same epistemic type.

Key Terms
الأصل
الفرع
كسبي
ضروري
شك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge can be acquired at the foundational level (principle) but certain at the derived level (branch), without any logical issue.
Correction: The passage shows this is impossible because any doubt in the principle propagates to the branch, undermining the certainty of the branch. Therefore both must share the same epistemic status.

Checkpoint
What happens if the principle is acquired and the branch is necessary?
A) The branch remains necessary
B) Doubt in the principle causes doubt in the branch
C) The branch becomes more certain
D) The principle becomes necessary

Key Takeaway
The epistemic status of a principle and its branch must be identical to avoid logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a branch of your faith or worldview that you hold as certain, yet the principle it rests on is something you have never truly examined? What would happen if you allowed yourself to doubt that principle even for a moment? Will you examine that principle today?
?
The passage shows that necessary knowledge cannot coexist with a doubtful principle. Where in your life have you assumed a derivative conclusion (like the validity of a practice or belief) is self-evident, while its foundation remains shaky? What concrete step will you take to either secure that foundation or let go of the certainty you don't actually have?
?
If every branch must share the epistemic status of its principle, then what does that mean for the certainties you claim about Allah, the afterlife, or the Quran? Have you built them on necessary knowledge or merely acquired opinions? Before you finish this reflection, commit to one theological principle you will strive to know with certainty, not just inherit.

The Impossibility of Holding Two Contradictory Beliefs
Imam Razi discusses whether the impossibility of believing two contradictories is intrinsic or due to a deterrent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossibility of believing two contradictories is intrinsic to the nature of certainty, not dependent on external deterrents.

Core Idea
Certainty leaves no room for its opposite — that is why contradiction is impossible.

Imagine being certain the sun is shining while also believing it is not. Is that impossible because of something in the beliefs themselves, or because something stops you? Imam Razi takes up this question and argues that the impossibility is built into what certainty means. This short passage provides a key insight into how Islamic theologians understand the logic of belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the plain meaning.
Notice the phrase 'الجزم بالثبوت' — certainty of affirmation.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage begins by stating a dispute. One view says impossibility is intrinsic; the other says it is due to a deterrent (ṣārif). Identify both.
3
Understand the author's argument
Razi says the closest view is that incompatibility is essential (dhātiyya). His proof: certainty that something is true requires that its opposite have no possibility. Therefore, simultaneous certainty in opposites is impossible.
The condition of 'no possibility' is key.
4
Reflect on the condition
Why does certainty require that the opposite have no possibility? Because if there is any possibility, the mind cannot be fully certain. This is a logical, not psychological, point.
5
Apply to theological certainty
Consider how this principle applies to core Islamic beliefs. For example, certainty in tawḥīd excludes any possibility of shirk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اختلفوا في أن اعتقاد الضدين يمتنع اجتماعهما لنفسهما أو الأمر يرجع إلى الصارف والأقرب أن المنافاة ذاتية لأن الجزم بالثبوت شرطه أن لا يكون لنقيضه احتمال فيستحيل تحققه دون هذا الشرط (4)
Issue: They disagreed about whether the simultaneous belief in two contradictories is impossible in itself or the matter refers to a deterrent. The closest (view) is that the incompatibility is essential, because certainty of affirmation (الجزم بالثبوت) requires that its contradictory have no possibility, so it is impossible for it to occur without this condition (4).
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute: is it impossible to hold two contradictory beliefs at once because of an intrinsic quality of the beliefs, or because some external factor (a deterrent, ṣārif) prevents it? The author states that the more correct view is that the incompatibility is essential (dhātiyya). The proof: certainty (jazzm) that something is true requires that its opposite have no possibility whatsoever. Therefore, if one is certain of a proposition, it is impossible for the opposite to be believed simultaneously. This impossibility is intrinsic to the nature of certainty.

Key Terms
اعتقاد الضدين
الصارف
المنافاة الذاتية
الجزم بالثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that holding contradictory beliefs is impossible due to psychological deterrents or human weakness.
Correction: The passage argues that the impossibility is logical and intrinsic to certainty itself—certainty by definition excludes any possibility for its opposite.

Checkpoint
What condition does certainty of affirmation (الجزم بالثبوت) require?
A) The contradictory must be possible
B) The contradictory must have no possibility
C) A deterrent must exist
D) The belief must be acquired

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of believing two contradictories is intrinsic to certainty because certainty excludes any possibility for its opposite.

?

In what area of your faith do you find it hardest to have certainty that excludes any possibility for the opposite? What specific step will you take today to strengthen that certainty?

The Non-Existent and Knowledge: A Kalam Debate
Imam Razi presents and critiques the claim that the non-existent cannot be known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether non-existent things can be known, including the use of syllogism, objection, and the concept of mental existence.

Core Idea
The non-existent is known through mental existence, but this raises deeper questions.

This passage presents a sophisticated debate in Islamic theology about the possibility of knowing non-existent things. The argument begins with a simple syllogism claiming that knowledge requires existence, which is then challenged by the need to conceive of the non-existent in order to deny it. The discussion leads to the notion of mental existence (al-thubūt al-dhihnī), which itself becomes problematic. By working through this debate, you will see how kalam thinkers refined their understanding of knowledge and existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall flow. Note that there are OCR corrections to keep in mind.
The word 'فمورض' should be read as 'فيعترض' (it is objected).
2
Identify the initial claim and its syllogism
Find the premises and conclusion that lead to 'the non-existent is not known.' Note the terms: known, distinct, established.
3
Trace the objection and response
Notice how the objection that judging the non-existent requires conception leads to the response about mental existence.
4
Analyze the problem of mental existence
Understand why saying 'God's partner is non-existent' creates a contradiction if the partner were present in the mind.
5
Follow the final resolution
See how the objector clarifies that it is the conception, not the essence, leading to the question of what that conception refers to.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : منهم من قال المعدوم غير معلوم لأن كل معلوم متميز وكل متميز ثابت فكل معلوم ثابت فما ليس ثابتا لا يكون معلوما ، فمورض بأن تخصيصه باللامعلومية يستدعى تصوره لأن مالا يتصور لا يصح الحكم عليه ، ثم أجابوا من كلام الأولين بأن المعدوم في الخارج ثابت في الذهن فقيل عليه الثابت في الذهن أخص من الثابت فيكون المعلوم ههنا ثابتا وليس كلامنا فيه وإنها الكلام في العلم بغير الثابت ، ولأن الثبوت الذهبى مشكل لانا إذا علمنا أن شريك الله تعالى معدوم لحضور الشريك في الذهن محال ، لأن الشريك هو الذى يجب وجوده لذاته والحاضر في الذهن لا يكون كذلك فإن قلت الحاضر في الذهن تصور الشريك لانفس الشريك ، قلت فقد عاد الإشكال لأن البحث إنما وقع عن متعلق هذا التصور فإنه إن كان نفيا محضا فكيف التحيز ، وإن كان ثابتا فثبوته إما في الذهن أو في الخارج والكلام فيه مامر (1)
Issue: Some said that the non-existent is not known, because everything known is distinct, and everything distinct is established, so everything known is established. Therefore, what is not established is not known. It is objected that specifying it as unknowable requires its conception, because what cannot be conceived cannot be judged. Then they answered from the speech of the earlier ones that the non-existent in the external world is established in the mind. So it was said that the established in the mind is more specific than the established, so 'known' here means established, and our discussion is not about that, but rather about knowledge of what is not established. And because mental establishment is problematic, for if we know that the partner of God is non-existent, then the presence of the partner in the mind is impossible, because the partner is that which necessarily exists in itself, and what is present in the mind is not like that. If you say: what is present in the mind is the conception of the partner, not the partner itself, I say: the problem returns, because the inquiry is about the object of this conception. For if it is pure negation, how is it determinate? And if it is established, its establishment is either in the mind or in the external world, and the discussion is as before (1).
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents a complex debate on whether non-existent things can be objects of knowledge. It starts with the claim that the non-existent is not known, based on a syllogism: knowledge requires distinction, distinction requires existence (thubūt), so what does not exist cannot be known. An objection is raised: to judge something as unknowable, one must first conceive it. The response invokes the concept of mental existence: the non-existent in the external world exists in the mind. But this is problematic because if we say 'God's partner is non-existent,' the partner cannot be in the mind since it would require the partner's essence to be present, which is impossible. The objector then clarifies that it is the conception (tasawwur) of the partner, not the partner itself. The author then points out that the problem recurs: what is the object of this conception? If it is a pure negation, it cannot be determinate; if it has some existence, it must be either mental or external, leading back to the same issue.

Key Terms
المعدوم
معلوم
ثابت
الثبوت الذهني
شريك الله
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existent things are simply nothing and cannot be thought about at all.
Correction: The debate shows that even to deny something, one must have a conception of it, which involves some form of mental existence. Thus, non-existent things can be objects of thought and knowledge through their mental representations.

Checkpoint
What is the initial syllogism used to claim that the non-existent is not known?
A) All known things are necessary; the non-existent is not necessary
B) Everything known is distinct and established; the non-existent is not established
C) The non-existent cannot be conceived; therefore it is not known
D) Knowledge requires evidence; the non-existent has no evidence

Key Takeaway
The debate shows that even denying something requires a conception that has some form of existence, either mental or otherwise, leading to deeper epistemological questions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that even denying the existence of God's partner requires a conception that has some form of existence. Think of a belief you hold that involves denying something—like 'there is no injustice in God.' Does your mind give that denial a shape? Are you comfortable that your denial is not pure nothing but a mental 'thing'? How does that affect your humility before God's unknowable essence?
?
Imam Razi shows that mental existence is problematic: the partner of God cannot truly be present in the mind. When you try to conceive of something impossible—like another god alongside Allah—do you realize you are still creating a mental image? Is that image, even if false, potentially dangerous? How will you discipline your thoughts to avoid giving existence to what should have none?
?
The debate leaves a tension: knowledge of non-existence is possible yet leads to paradox. You rely on this knowledge every time you declare 'there is no God but Allah.' But this lesson shows the epistemological difficulty. Will you now recite the shahada with more caution, or more awe, knowing that your mind's grasp of 'no god' is not as simple as you thought? What will you do with this intellectual unease?

Willing a Thing Is Not Hating Its Opposite: A Refinement by Imam Razi
Imam Razi refutes the unconditional identity of willing and hating its opposite, and provides a conditional correction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why willing a thing is not equivalent to hating its opposite, and how the condition of attentiveness refines the relationship.

Core Idea
Willing something does not automatically mean hating its opposite unless you are aware of it.

Imam Razi examines a claim that equates willing with hating the opposite. Using the example of heedlessness, he shows this equation fails. He then offers a refined formulation: willing entails hatred of the opposite only if the opposite is consciously considered.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the original claim and refutation
The passage begins with a claim: 'willing a thing is hating its opposite.' Then Razi declares it false and gives a reason. Find the reason.
The reason involves a state of heedlessness (ghafla).
3
Understand the counterexample
In a state of heedlessness, a person wills something without being aware of its opposite. Thus, they do not hate the opposite at that moment. This refutes the unconditional identity.
4
Note the corrected formulation
The footnote (or author's comment) provides the correct view: willing necessitates hating the opposite, but only if one is attentive to the opposite.
This shows the original claim was too strong.
5
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why this distinction matters for understanding human will and moral responsibility.
Willing and hating are separate psychological states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : منهم من قال إرادة الشيء كراهة ضده وهو باطل ) لأنه قد يراد الشيء حالة الغفلة حصلت ضدو غان (۱) (۱) أقول : الصواب أن يقال إرادة الشيء يلزمها كرامة ضده بشرط التفطن للضد .
Issue: Some of them said that willing a thing is hating its opposite, and it is false, because a thing may be willed in a state of heedlessness (ghafla) its opposite [?] (1). (1) I say: The correct thing is to say that willing a thing entails hating its opposite, with the condition of being attentive to the opposite.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage rejects the equation of willing a thing with hating its opposite. The proof is that a person can will something while being unaware (ghafla) of its opposite, so willing does not automatically imply hatred of the opposite. The footnote (1) corrects this: the correct relationship is that willing a thing necessitates (yalzamuha) hating its opposite, but only if one is attentive to the opposite. Thus the original claim is false as an unconditional identity.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irada)
الكراهة (al-karaha)
الضد (al-didd)
الغفلة (al-ghafla)
التفطن (al-tafattun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If I will something, I automatically hate its opposite.
Correction: No, you must be aware of the opposite for hatred to arise. Willing itself does not guarantee hatred.

Checkpoint

A) Because a person can will something without thinking of its opposite.
B) Because hatred is a stronger emotion than will.
C) Because opposites are not real.
D) Because God wills things without hatred.

Key Takeaway
Willing and hating are distinct; hatred of the opposite only follows from willing when the opposite is consciously considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you willed something (wealth, status, a relationship) while ignoring the spiritual harm it might cause. Were you truly heedless, or did you suppress awareness? How does this lesson confront that choice?
?

?
You claim to love Allah and His commands, but do you hate the disobedience that opposes them? Or is there heedlessness in your heart that allows both to coexist? What would it take for you to truly feel the hatred of sin as a consequence of your will for righteousness?

Intrinsic vs. Contextual Conflict of Wills
Razi frames a debate on the intrinsic vs. contextual incompatibility of two wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the incompatibility between two opposing wills is intrinsic or due to external considerations, and the two main positions on this issue.

Core Idea
The conflict of wills may be inherent or circumstantial—a foundational question for divine attributes.

This short passage from Imam al-Razi presents a nuanced theological debate: Is the incompatibility between two wills (e.g., to move and to rest) a matter of their intrinsic natures, or does it arise from other factors? The text first frames the question, then outlines two opposing views, referencing an earlier discussion on belief for analogy. Let's examine how the author structures this fundamental issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation to understand the meaning.
Pay attention to the reference to the previous chapter on belief (i'tiqad).
2
Identify the two views
Notice that the passage presents two distinct positions. The first says the wills themselves are intrinsically opposite. The second says one will diverts the agent from the other.
The second view treats incompatibility as a matter of redirection rather than inherent opposition.
3
Connect to the earlier reference
The text says 'the discussion on it is similar to what has passed.' Reflect on how the earlier discussion on conflicting beliefs might parallel this issue.
This shows the author's method of using analogical reasoning across topics.

مسألة المنافاة بين إرادتي العدين ذاتية أو للمصارف ) فيه ما تقدم في باب الاعتقاد (۳) (۳) أقول : قيل إرادة الحركة ترجيح صدورها وإرادة السكون ترجيح صدوره وكما أنهما متقابلان لذاتيهما كذلك إرادتهما ، وقوم أخر قالوا إرادة الحركة تصرف الفاعل عن إرادة السكون والكلام فيه مثل ما مر .

Commentary
This passage addresses whether the incompatibility between two opposing wills (e.g., will for movement and will for rest) is intrinsically necessary or due to external considerations. The author refers to the earlier discussion on belief (i'tiqad) for parallels. The commentary (أقول) presents two views: one that the wills themselves are intrinsically opposite like their objects, and another that the will for movement simply diverts the agent from the contrary will. The discussion mirrors the earlier treatment of conflicting beliefs.

Refuting the Claim That Will Is Hatred of the Opposite
Razi states and refutes a claim about will and hatred, then offers a corrected view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that willing a thing is identical to hating its opposite is false, and what the correct relationship between will and hatred is.

Core Idea
Will is not hatred; it only entails hatred when you are aware of the opposite.

This passage from Razi's Muhassal addresses a subtle but important distinction in the psychology of volition. Some thinkers claimed that willing something and hating its opposite are the same thing. Razi shows that this cannot be, because a person can will something without even thinking about its opposite. Instead, the correct view is that willing something implies hatred of the opposite, but only if the person is attentive to that opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the claim and its refutation
Find the statement that some people said willing a thing is hating its opposite. Notice how Razi rejects it as false and gives a counterexample involving heedlessness (ghafla).
3
Understand the correction
Razi then provides the correct formulation: willing a thing entails hating its opposite, but only on condition of being attentive (tafattun) to the opposite. This is a conditional relationship, not an identity.
Contrast this with the original claim which omitted the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

I say: The correct statement is that willing a thing entails hating its opposite on condition of attention to the opposite.

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific claim about the relationship between will and hatred. The claim is that willing something is identical to hating its opposite. Razi states it is false, giving the example that someone can will something while in a state of heedlessness (ghafla), unaware of the opposite, so the will occurs without hatred of the opposite. Then Razi (or the commentator) offers a correction: the correct view is that willing a thing entails (yalzamu) hatred of its opposite, but only on condition that the person is attentive (tafattun) to the opposite. Thus, the original claim is false because it omits the condition of awareness.

Are Two Opposing Wills Naturally Opposite?
Razi discusses whether the opposition between two wills (e.g., to move and to be still) is essential or due to something else.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether the opposition between two wills (e.g., to move and to be still) is essential or due to diverting factors, and the two views presented by Razi.

Core Idea
The opposition between wills may stem from the nature of the acts or from a change in the agent's intention.

In this passage, Razi introduces a subtle debate: is the conflict between two wills—like the will to move and the will to be still—intrinsic to the wills themselves, or does it arise from external diverting factors? This question touches on how we understand divine and human agency. The passage presents two opposing views and references an earlier discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully along with its literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms 'ترجيح' and 'تصرف'.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two views
The passage first gives one view: that wills are opposite because their objects are opposite. Then it gives a second view: that the will of motion is a turning away from the will of rest. Notice how each view defines will differently.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this debate matters for theology. If opposition is essential, it affects how we understand God's will and action. If it is due to diverting factors, it might allow for simultaneous wills in some sense.
This connects to broader discussions in kalam about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة المنافاة بين إرادتي الضدين ذاتية أو للمصارف فيه ما تقدم في باب الاعتقاد . أقول : قيل إرادة الحركة ترجيح صدورها وإرادة السكون ترجيح صدوره وكما أنهما متقابلان لذاتيهما كذلك إرادتهما ، وقوم أخر قالوا إرادة الحركة تصرف الفاعل عن إرادة السكون والكلام فيه مثل ما مر .
Issue: The opposition between the two wills of opposites is essential or due to diverting factors? In it, what has preceded in the chapter on belief. I say: It was said: Will of motion is preponderating its occurrence, and will of rest is preponderating its occurrence, and just as they are opposites in themselves, so are their wills. And another group said: Will of motion is the agent's turning away from will of rest, and the discussion about it is like what has passed.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage addresses a question: is the opposition between two wills of opposites (e.g., will to move vs will to be still) intrinsic to the wills themselves, or does it come from external diverting factors? Razi reports two views. First view: will of motion is the preponderance (tarjih) of motion's occurrence, and will of rest is preponderance of rest's occurrence; since motion and rest are opposites in essence, their wills are also opposites. Second view: will of motion is the agent's turning away (tasarruf) from will of rest, implying the opposition is by way of change. Razi notes the discussion is like what preceded (in the chapter on belief), indicating this is a familiar debate.

Key Terms
منافاة
إرادة
ضدين
ذاتية
تصرف
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the opposition between wills is merely a matter of preference, but it is rooted in the opposition of the acts themselves.
Correction: The passage shows that the first view links the opposition of wills to the essential opposition of their objects (motion and rest), not to subjective preference.

Checkpoint

A) They are opposite because the agent turns away from one to the other
B) They are opposite in themselves just as motion and rest are
C) They are not actually opposite
D) Their opposition is due to external factors

Key Takeaway
The opposition between wills can be understood either as essential (because the objects are opposite) or as a result of the agent's turning away from one will to another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself torn between two desires, do you attribute the conflict to the objects themselves or to a change in your own heart? Which view does your experience support?
?
Have you ever 'turned away' from a will to do good? What does that say about the nature of your will and its relationship to your actions?
?
If the opposition of wills is merely a matter of preference, what does that imply about your accountability for the choices you make?

On the Nature of Wish and the Example of the Incapable
Razi discusses the condition for a wish and gives an example of an incapable person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the example of an incapable person to clarify the conditions under which a wish (shawq) arises, and the distinction between mere belief and desire.

Core Idea
A wish can exist even when attainment is impossible, as in longing for an unreachable beloved.

In this passage, Imam Razi continues his analysis of the nature of wish (shawq). He examines a specific scenario: a person who is incapable of obtaining something yet still experiences desire. This example helps to refine our understanding of what constitutes a genuine wish and how it relates to power (qudra) and belief (i'tiqad). Let's explore the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
The Arabic is fragmented, but the translation captures the core idea.
2
Identify the key terms
Note the terms: ميل (mayl, desire), شوق (shawq, wish), قدرة (qudra, power), اعتقاد (i'tiqad, belief). These are central to the argument.
3
Understand the example of the incapable person
Focus on the example: a person who cannot obtain something (e.g., a beloved) but still feels desire. This shows that desire does not require possibility of attainment.
The example is given as 'yearning for a beloved by one who cannot reach him.'
4
Contrast with the capable person
For the one with complete power, the passage says 'the mentioned belief is sufficient.' Compare the two cases to see the difference.
5
Reflect on the philosophical implication
Consider: Why does Razi emphasize that desire can exist without possibility? This relates to the nature of wish as a psychological state distinct from objective possibility.
This connects to broader discussions in kalam about human agency and divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمكن وصولها إليه أو إلى ذلك الغير من غير مانع من تعب أو معارضة ثم فى وجود ميل يترتب على هذا الاعتبار مغاير له نظر ، قالوا هذا المثل عدث بمن لا يقدر على تحصيل ذلك الشيء وقدرة تامة فيحصل له ميل إلى شيء يريد حصوله ولا يحصل بحسب ما يتمناه ، وذلك مثل الشوق إلى المحبوب ممن لا يصل إليه أما في القادر التام القدرة فيكفي الاعتقاد المذكور
It is possible for it to reach him or that other without an obstacle of trouble or opposition, then regarding the existence of a desire dependent on this consideration, there is a different consideration. They said: This example is counted for one who is unable to obtain that thing, and with complete power, a desire occurs for something whose occurrence he wants, but it does not occur according to what he wishes. That is like yearning for a beloved by one who cannot reach him. As for the one with complete power, the mentioned belief is sufficient.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition for a wish (mash'q or shawq) to arise. It states that a wish can exist when there is no obstacle preventing attainment. However, there is a specific case: a person who is unable (la yaqdiru) to obtain something but has complete power (qudra tamma) may still have a desire (mayl) for something he wants to happen, but it does not happen as he wishes. The example is yearning for a distant beloved who cannot be reached. In contrast, for one who has complete power, mere belief (i'tiqad) in the possibility is sufficient for the wish to be meaningful. The passage likely aims to distinguish between genuine desire and mere wishful thinking.

Key Takeaway
Wish (shawq) can arise even when the object of desire is unattainable, as illustrated by the example of yearning for a distant beloved.

Perception Is Not Necessitated by Physical Conditions
Razi argues perception is not necessitated by physical conditions, against Mu'tazila and philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that perception is not a necessary consequence of physical conditions, and how he uses the example of seeing a large object from a distance to support his view.

Core Idea
Perception is not forced by conditions; it follows Allah's custom, not logical necessity.

In this passage from the Muhassal, Imam Razi takes on a central debate in kalam: is perception automatically produced when all physical conditions are met? The Mu'tazila and philosophers say yes, but Razi disagrees. He presents a subtle argument based on our everyday experience of seeing large objects as small, and then responds to a powerful objection. Let's unpack his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation side by side. Notice the structure: claim, proof, objection, answer.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify Razi's main claim
Find where he states that perception is 'not necessary' (ghayr wajib). This is the core thesis.
3
Work through the proof
Razi gives two reasons: the size perception example and the independence of seeing parts. Make sure you see how these support the claim.
He avoids circularity.
4
Analyze the objection and Razi's reply
The opponents raise a reductio ad absurdum. Razi's answer appeals to 'al-adiyat' (divine custom). Why is this sufficient?
This distinguishes metaphysical necessity from customary regularity.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this argument preserves divine omnipotence: God is free to create or withhold perception even when conditions are met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الإدراك عند سلامة الحاسة وحصول المبصر وسائر الشرائط المشهورة غير واجب عندنا خلافا للمعتزلة والفلاسفة. إنا نرى الكبير من البعيد صغيرا وما ذاك إلا لأنا نرى بعض أجزائه دون البعض مع استوائها في أثرها في كل الشرائط، ولأنا لما رأينا الجسم الكبير فقد رأينا كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء، وليست رؤية كل جزء مشروطة برؤية الجزء الآخر وإلا وقع الدور، فرؤية كل واحد منها غنية عن رؤية الآخر، واحتجوا بأنه لولم يجب ذلك لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا شموس وجبال ونحن لا نراها. الجواب: أنه معارض بجميع العاديات.
Issue: Perception given soundness of the sense-organ, presence of the visible object, and all the well-known conditions is not necessary according to us, contrary to the Mu'tazila and the philosophers. Indeed, we see the large from afar as small, and this is only because we see some of its parts without others, while they are equal in their effect in all conditions; and because when we see the large body, we have seen each one of those parts, and the seeing of each part is not conditioned upon the seeing of the other part, otherwise circularity would occur; so the seeing of each one of them is independent of seeing the other. And they objected: if that were not necessary, then it would be possible that there be suns and mountains present with us and we do not see them. The answer: it is countered by all the habitual [customs].
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a position-statement and proof on the nature of perception. Razi (the author) asserts that perception (al-idrak) is not a necessary result of the presence of physical conditions such as a healthy sense-organ and the visible object, contrary to the Mu'tazila and philosophers who hold that perception is necessitated under such conditions. He provides a proof: we see a large object from a distance as small, which shows that we see only some parts and not others, even though all parts are equally present and visible. This indicates that seeing one part does not necessitate seeing another; otherwise there would be circularity. Therefore, each part's perception is independent. The opponents object: if perception were not necessary, then we could have suns and mountains before us and not see them, which is absurd. Razi counters by saying this objection is refuted by appealing to 'all the habitual ways' (al-'adiyat), meaning that God's custom (al-'adah) ordinarily brings about perception under these conditions, but it is not logically necessary. The argument distinguishes between customary regularity and metaphysical necessity.

Key Terms
الإدراك (al-idrak)
المبصر (al-mubsar)
العاديات (al-'adiyat)
العادة (al-'adah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that perception is automatically necessary once all physical conditions are met.
Correction: Razi argues that perception only occurs by God's custom, not by logical necessity. The example of seeing large objects as small shows that even when conditions are present, perception can be partial, and the objection is countered by noting that God's customary action is reliable but not necessitated.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's main claim about perception in this passage?
A) Perception is logically necessary when conditions are met.
B) Perception is not necessary; it is a matter of divine custom.
C) Perception is impossible under any conditions.
D) Perception depends only on the sense-organ, not the object.

Key Takeaway
Razi establishes that perception depends on divine custom, not logical necessity, preserving God's freedom to create or withhold perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now understand that your sight is not a mechanical certainty but a gift that Allah could withdraw at any moment. When you looked in the mirror this morning, did you thank Him for that moment of perception? How can you make that gratitude a daily reality?
?
Think of a time when you assumed something would happen because all the conditions were right—and it didn't. How does Razi's argument that outcomes are not necessitated by conditions change the way you view that disappointment? Are you willing to trust Allah's custom more than your own expectations?
?
If perception is a divine custom, then every time you see, Allah is directly creating that experience for you. Does this truth make you more conscious of His constant involvement in your life? What would it take for you to live with that awareness every second?

Defense of the Impression Theory of Vision
Razi defends Aristotle's theory of impression against the objection of size mismatch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends Aristotle's impression theory of vision against the objection of size mismatch by clarifying that only a phantom (shabah) is impressed, not the object itself.

Core Idea
Impression theory needs only a phantom, not the full object.

In this passage, Razi defends the Aristotelian theory of vision known as impression (al-intiba') against a common objection: how can a large object be seen when the eye is small? He explains that the theory does not claim the object itself enters the eye, but rather its phantom. The passage uses analogies to mirrors and paintings to illustrate the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: objection, response, and two analogies.
Pay attention to the terms 'shabah' (phantom) and 'intiba'' (impression).
2
Identify the objection and response
What is the objection? How does Razi respond? He says the objection is invalid because impression of the whole is not required.
The key move is distinguishing between the object itself and its phantom.
3
Consider the analogies
Reflect on the mirror analogy (reflecting half the sky) and the painting analogy (creating depth on a flat surface). How do they support Razi's defense?
These analogies show that a small impression can convey largeness or depth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: إنما قال بالانطباع أرسطاطاليس وأصحابه وبينوا السبب في رؤية العظيم من بعيد صغيرا وإبطاله بامتناع انطباع العظيم في الصغير غير صحيح لأنهم لا يشترطون فيه انطباع العظيم نفسه أو مقداره بل قالوا بانطباع شبح منه ولعل مقدار الشبح على صغر محله يقتضي إدراك ذي الشبح على عظمه وذلك كما ينطبع في المرآة نصف السماء والأجرام التي فيه وأما رؤية القريب على قربه والبعيد على بعده يعني الأبعاد فلعل المنطبع في العين يكون على هيئة يفيد إدراك الأبعاد ونحن لما تعذر علينا أن نعبر عنه استبعدناه مع أنا نرى النقاشين ينقشون صور الأجسام على السطوح على وجه يدرك الناظر فيها أعماق تلك الأجسام وأبعاد ما بينها.
I say: Only Aristotle and his followers held the theory of impression, and they explained the reason for seeing the large from afar as small; and invalidating it by the impossibility of the impression of the large in the small is not correct, because they do not require in it the impression of the large itself or its size, but rather they said it is an impression of a phantom of it, and perhaps the size of the phantom, despite the smallness of its place, necessitates perception of the possessor of the phantom in its largeness, similar to what is impressed in a mirror: half the sky and the celestial bodies in it. As for seeing the near at its nearness and the far at its farness, i.e., distances, perhaps what is impressed in the eye is in a form that provides perception of distances; and when we were unable to express it, we considered it remote, even though we see painters depict images of bodies on surfaces in a way that the viewer perceives the depths of those bodies and the distances between them.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the theory of vision known as 'al-intiba'' (impression) attributed to Aristotle and his school. The author (likely Razi in an editorial comment) responds to an objection: if a large object is seen from far as small, it cannot be that the entire object impresses itself in the eye, because the eye is too small. The defense is that proponents do not require the impression of the object itself or its quantity, but only an image (shabah) of it. The small size of the image does not preclude perceiving the object's largeness, just as a mirror can reflect half the sky. Regarding depth perception, the impression in the eye may have a form that conveys distance, analogous to how painters create an illusion of depth on a flat surface. The author concedes that the precise mechanism is hard to express, but the phenomenon is observable.

Key Terms
الانطباع (al-intiba')
الشبح (al-shabah)
الأبعاد (al-ab'ad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impression theory requires the entire object to fit in the eye.
Correction: The theory only requires a phantom (shabah) of the object, not the object itself.

Checkpoint
What do Aristotle and his followers claim is impressed in the eye?
A) The entire object itself.
B) The exact size of the object.
C) A phantom (shabah) of the object.
D) Nothing; they reject impression.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends impression theory by clarifying that only a phantom of the object is impressed, not the object itself, using analogies from mirrors and paintings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine you are looking at something far away, like a mountain or a person. Your eyes only capture a small image. Yet you know the mountain is huge. This lesson shows that the small image (phantom) is enough for you to perceive the true size. Now, ask yourself: In your own life, how often do you judge a situation, a person, or yourself based on a 'small' first impression, without allowing the reality—the 'largeness' of the truth—to come through? Can you commit today to pause and seek the larger picture before drawing conclusions?
?
Razi uses the analogy of a painter creating depth on a flat surface to explain how the eye's impression can convey distance. This means that your perception of depth is not a direct encounter with space but a constructed representation. Have you ever been deceived by an optical illusion or trusted your senses only to realize they misled you? What does this tell you about the reliability of your own perceptions in spiritual matters? Will you let this humble your certainty about what you think you see in your life's path?
?
The passage concedes that the precise mechanism of how the eye perceives depth is hard to express ('عبر عنه استبعدناه') but observable. This shows that not everything can be fully explained, yet it still functions. Are there areas of your faith or life where you demand complete explanation before trusting? Is there a truth you have rejected because you couldn't fully articulate it? Today, reflect on one such area and ask Allah to guide you to accept what is true even if you cannot fully grasp it.

Hearing Does Not Require Air Reaching the Ear
Razi argues against philosophers and Nazzam that hearing does not require air carrying sound to reach the ear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that hearing does not require air carrying sound to reach the ear, and the two analogies he uses to support his position.

Core Idea
Hearing works through solids; air transmission is not a necessary condition.

In this concise argument, Razi takes on the philosophers and al-Nazzam by challenging a common assumption about hearing. He presents two clever analogies—one involving a solid wall, the other involving the sense of touch—to show that air reaching the ear is not a requirement for hearing. Let's read his reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: a statement of the issue, the author's position, and two proofs.
The passage is short but packed with argument.
2
Identify the two proofs
Look for the two reasons Razi gives: one about hearing through a solid wall, and one about perceiving the direction of sound. Mark them in the text.
3
Understand the wall analogy
Razi argues that if air were necessary, we could not hear speech from behind a wall because the air's shape is lost. Think about why that would be a problem for the opposing view.
This shows sound can travel through solids.
4
Understand the direction analogy
Razi compares hearing to touch: we only feel something when it contacts us, but we cannot tell its direction by touch alone. Since we can perceive the direction of sound, air reaching the ear cannot be a condition.
This is a powerful analogy from common experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اختلفوا في أنه هل يعتبر في السمع وصول الهواء الحامل للصوت إلى الصماخ؟ فعندنا أنه غير واجب خلافا للفلاسفة والنظام. لنا أنه لو كان كما قالوا لما سمعنا كلام من يجول بيننا وبينه من جدار صلب، لأن الهواء النافذ في مسام ذلك الجدار لا يبقى على الشكل الأول الذي باعتباره كان حاملا للحروف، ولأنه كان يجب أن لا يدرك جهات الصوت، كما أنا لا نلمس الشيء إلا حال وصوله إلينا، فلأجل ذلك لا ندرك بمجرد اللمس جهة وصوله.
Issue: They differed on whether reaching the ear of the air carrying sound is considered necessary in hearing. According to us it is not necessary, contrary to the philosophers and al-Nazzam. Our proof: if it were as they said, we would not hear speech of one who is beyond a solid wall from us, because the air penetrating the pores of that wall does not remain in the first state by virtue of which it was a carrier of letters; and because it would then be necessary that we not perceive the directions of sound, just as we do not touch a thing except at the moment of its reaching us, and therefore we do not perceive by mere touch the direction of its reaching.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a brief argument (mas'ala) on the nature of hearing. The author states that the question is whether the arrival of air carrying sound to the ear is a condition for hearing. The Ash'ari position is that it is not necessary, contrary to the philosophers and al-Nazzam (the Mu'tazili). The proof is that if it were necessary, we would not hear speech from behind a solid wall, because the air passing through the wall's pores loses the shape that carried the sound. Additionally, if hearing required air reaching the ear, we would not be able to perceive the direction of sound, analogous to touch: we only feel something when it touches us, but we cannot tell its direction by touch alone. Since we do perceive sound direction, the condition is false.

Key Terms
السمع
الهواء الحامل للصوت
الصماخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hearing always requires air transmission to the ear.
Correction: Sound can travel through solids; the Ash'ari position is that air reaching the ear is not a necessary condition for hearing.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that hearing does not require air reaching the ear?
A) Because we can hear through a solid wall.
B) Because the ear does not need air.
C) Because sound is created directly in the ear.
D) Because philosophers agreed with him.

Key Takeaway
Hearing does not require air reaching the ear; the Ash'ari position is supported by two analogous proofs from common experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that hearing does not require air transmission. Razi uses a simple analogy from touch to hearing. When you hear someone speaking through a wall, do you ever pause to recognize the power of Allah in creating sound and perception? Or has the familiarity of such experiences made you forget the divine wisdom behind them? What will you do today to cultivate awe?
?
This argument challenges a philosophical assumption using empirical evidence. Are there beliefs you hold in your faith or daily life that you have never examined against your own experience? Will you set aside time this week to test one such belief against what you directly observe and feel, trusting Allah’s signs over human conjecture?
