Wave Theory of Sound Transmission
Razi explains wave theory and refutes objections about shape and medium.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's explanation of the wave theory of sound, including the key concepts of quality (kayfiyya) and ringing (ranin), and how he refutes common objections.

Core Idea
Sound transmission is a wave of quality, not a visible shape, and direction is perceived through a remaining ringing.

Imam Razi presents and defends the wave theory of sound, clarifying common misconceptions. He explains that the wave is not a visible shape but a quality spreading through the medium, and that even in bodies without pores, sound travels via waves. He also addresses how we perceive the direction of sound through a ringing effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the author's own words and structure come through before interpretation.
2
Identify key components of the wave theory
Notice that the wave (tamawwuj) is described as a quality (kayfiyya) spreading, not a visible shape. The example of water waves is about the quality in the depth, not the surface shape.
Also note that the theory does not require air to remain in a specific shape.
3
Understand the refutation of objections
See how Razi extends the wave to non-air bodies like brass vessels, and how sound can be heard even from a solid body without pores, via wave transmission through the body to the air.
This refutes the objection that waves only occur in air or water.
4
Grasp the explanation of direction perception
Understand that direction is perceived via a 'ringing' (ranin) that remains in the air, as stated. Also note Abu al-Barakat's analogy of the soul following the struck air.
5
Reflect on the rejection of analogy to touch
Razi concludes that analogizing hearing to touch is futile. Think about why this analogy might be misleading and how the wave theory offers a better explanation.
This step ties together the lesson's key points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: القائلون بالتموج لا يشترطون فيه بقاء الهواء على شكل والذي يتمثلون به من تموج الماء ليس المراد منه حدوث الشكل المرئي فيه بل الكيفية الحاصلة في نفس جزئه بسبب القرع وانبساط تلك الكيفية في الماء الذي بلى موضع القرع، فإن الشكل يختص بالسطح الظاهر والموج يحصل في عمق الماء والهواء، وأيضاً لا يقولون بامتناع وجود التموج في جسم غير الماء والهواء بل يجوزونه في غير ذلك كالأواني الصفرية وارتعاشها زمانا بسبب القرع وإحداثها الصوت بعد القرع زمانا طويلا، وأيضاً إذا حدث القرع على جسم لا مسام له أصلا فإن السامع يسمع الصوت من غير أن يصل من موضع القرع هواء إلى صماخه بل يتأدى التموج من ذلك الجسم إلى الهواء الذي يجاوره ومن الهواء إلى الصماخ، وإدراك الجهات بسبب رنين يبقى في الهواء يفيد الإحساس بجهة القرع، وقال أبو البركات البغدادي كأن النفس تتبع الهواء المقروع في جهة القارع حتى يحس بذلك، وقياس السمع على اللمس لا يجد بطائل.
I say: The proponents of wave theory do not require the air to remain in a shape in it; and the example they give of a wave in water is not meant to be the occurrence of the visible shape in it, but rather the quality (kayfiyya) occurring in the part itself due to the strike and the spreading of that quality in the water adjacent to the place of the strike. For the shape is specific to the apparent surface, whereas the wave occurs in the depth of water and air. Moreover, they do not claim the impossibility of the existence of a wave in a body other than water and air; rather they allow it in other things such as brass vessels and their vibration for a time due to the strike, and their producing sound after the strike for a long time. Also, if a strike occurs on a body that has no pores at all, the hearer hears the sound without air from the place of the strike reaching his ear, but rather the wave transmits from that body to the air adjacent to it, and from the air to the ear; and the perception of directions is due to a ringing (ranin) that remains in the air that conveys the sensation of the direction of the strike. And Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi said: it is as if the soul follows the struck air in the direction of the striker until it senses that. And analogizing hearing to touch is of no avail.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note on the wave theory (tamawwuj) of sound transmission. The author clarifies that proponents of wave theory do not require the air to preserve a specific shape; the example of water waves is about a quality (kayfiyya) spreading through the medium, not a visible shape. Waves occur in the depth, not just the surface. They also allow waves in non-air/water bodies like brass vessels, which vibrate and produce sound. Even if a body has no pores, sound is heard because the wave passes through the body to the adjacent air and then to the ear. Direction perception is explained by a 'ringing' (ranin) that remains in the air. He quotes Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi who likens it to the soul tracking the struck air. The author concludes that analogizing hearing to touch is futile.

Key Terms
التموج (al-tamawwuj)
الرنين (al-ranin)
الكيفية (al-kayfiyya)
القرع (al-qar')
الصماخ (al-simakh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wave theory requires air to preserve a visible shape and only applies to water and air.
Correction: The wave is a quality spreading, not a visible shape; shape is only surface-deep, and waves can occur in other bodies like brass.

Checkpoint
According to the wave theory, what is responsible for the perception of sound direction?
A) A visible shape in the air
B) A remaining ringing (ranin) in the air
C) The arrival of air to the ear
D) The size of the sound waves

Key Takeaway
Sound transmission is a wave of quality, not a visible shape, and direction is perceived via a remaining ringing (ranin) in the air.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the adhan, do you ever pause to consider that the sound reaching your ears is not just a shape of waves but a quality that could travel even through solid walls? How does this awareness affect your readiness to respond?
?
The passage explains that direction is perceived through a 'ringing' that remains. In your spiritual life, what 'ringing' remains after you hear a Qur'an verse or a reminder? Do you let that ringing guide you toward the Source, or do you let it fade without response?
?
Imam Razi defends the wave theory against the objection that it requires a visible shape. How often do you judge your own worship or deeds by their outward appearance rather than the inner quality (kayfiyya) of your heart? What one change can you make today to focus more on the reality behind the form?

Three Modes of Smell Perception
Razi outlines three possible modes of smell perception and comments on their strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proposed mechanisms for how smell perception works and why the third is considered the weakest.

Core Idea
Smell perception is explained by matter contact, not by soul projection.

How do we perceive smells? Is it by particles entering the nose, or by some other means? Imam Razi presents three possible explanations: one involving air modification, one involving fine particles, and one involving the perceiving power reaching out to the fragrance. He then judges each possibility, explaining why the third is far-fetched. This passage shows how early Muslim theologians reasoned about sensory perception using physical principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text first without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the literal meaning. Pay attention to the three possibilities listed.
2
Identify the three modes
The passage lists three ways smell perception might occur. Identify each one and note how they differ: air modification, particle separation, and direct attachment of the perceiving power.
The third mode involves the soul's power leaving its locus, which Razi finds problematic.
3
Understand Razi's evaluation
Razi comments on each mode. The first two are supported by observable phenomena (lingering smell, evaporation). The third is dismissed because the perceiving power cannot detach from its locus. Notice the reasoning based on the nature of the soul and its faculties.
This reflects a commitment to the soul's immanence in the body.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
Consider why the third mode is rejected: it assumes the soul can act at a distance without a medium. This is a key principle in kalam: the soul's faculties are bound to the body. How does this contrast with modern theories of perception?
This connects to broader debates about the nature of the soul and its powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: إدراك الشم قد يكون بتكيف الهواء المتصل بالخيشوم بكيفية ذي الرائحة وقد يكون لانفصال أجزاء لطيفة منها ووصولها إلى خيشومنا كما في التبخرات، وقد يكون تعلق القوة المدركة بالرائحة وهي هناك وهذا أضعف الاحتمالات. أقول: الوجهان الأولان موجودان في أشياء لا تنقض باحتباس رائحة وفي التبخيرات. والوجه الثالث بعيد فإن القوة لا تتعلق بغير محلها ولا تنتقل من محلها.
Issue: The perception of smell may be by the air connected to the nose becoming qualified with a quality of the fragrant thing, or by the separation of fine particles from it and their reaching our nose as in evaporations, or by the attachment of the perceiving power to the fragrance while it is there; and this is the weakest of the possibilities. I say: The first two aspects are found in things not invalidated by retention of smell and in evaporations. The third aspect is far-fetched, for the power does not attach to other than its locus and does not transfer from its locus.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents three possible explanations for the sense of smell. (1) The air around the nose becomes modified by the quality (kayfiyya) of the fragrant object. (2) Fine particles of the fragrant object detach and reach the nose, as in vapors. (3) The perceiving power attaches to the fragrance while it is still in its original place (i.e., perception at a distance). The author notes that the third is the weakest. In the comment (أقول), the first two modes are confirmed by instances where smell lingers or in evaporations, while the third is unlikely because the perceiving power (the soul or sensory faculty) does not leave its locus (the body) and cannot attach to something not present in it.

Key Terms
الشم (al-sham)
الكيفية (al-kayfiyya)
القوة المدركة (al-quwwa al-mudrika)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That smell always requires particles to reach the nose.
Correction: The first mode (air modification) also explains smell without particle transfer, as when a scent lingers in a room.

Checkpoint
Which mode of smell perception does Razi consider the weakest?
A) Air being qualified by the fragrance
B) Fine particles reaching the nose
C) The perceiving power attaching to the distant fragrance
D) All are equally strong

Key Takeaway
Razi explains smell perception through two plausible mechanisms (air modification and particle transfer) and rejects the idea that the soul's power can directly attach to distant objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were absolutely certain about something based on your senses—perhaps a smell you thought came from a certain direction. After learning that perception involves indirect mechanisms like air modification or particle transfer, how does that affect your trust in your own senses? Are you willing to acknowledge that your perceptions may be limited or mediated, and what does that humility demand of you in your daily judgments?
?
This lesson rejects the idea that the soul can directly perceive or influence distant objects without a physical medium. Have you ever acted as if your thoughts, emotions, or desires could directly change your circumstances without any effort or means? Reflect on a specific situation where you expected a result without taking concrete action. How does this lesson expose the arrogance of such an assumption, and what will you do differently?
?
Razi states that the perceiving power 'does not attach to other than its locus and does not transfer from its locus.' Your soul is anchored to your body. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes seek experiences that are disembodied or detached from your physical reality? How can you better honor the fact that your faith is lived through your body and its actions, not through detached spiritual claims?

Impossibility of Transference of Accidents
Razi proves that accidents cannot transfer from one substance to another, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for why accidents cannot transfer from one substance to another, including the key dichotomy and the resolution of an objection.

Core Idea
Accidents are ontologically tied to a specific locus and cannot move.

This lesson presents a foundational argument in Islamic metaphysics: the impossibility of accidents transferring. Razi builds a logical dichotomy, stripping an accident of all attributes to analyze its need for a locus. Understanding this proof clarifies the nature of accidental properties and their dependence on substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's logical structure come through.
Notice the concise style of the proof.
2
Understand the two branches
The proof begins with a stripped accident: either it needs a locus or not. Trace each branch to its conclusion.
The first branch is dismissed quickly; the second leads to the desired conclusion.
3
Examine the objection and answer
Read the objection that challenges the first branch. See how Razi distinguishes between 'necessarily not in a locus' and 'not necessarily in a locus'.
This distinction is subtle but crucial.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters for theology and natural philosophy. How does it relate to the nature of substances and accidents?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اتفق المتكلمون والفلاسفة على امتناع انتقال الأعراض. لأن الانتقال عبارة عن الحصول في حين بعد الحصول في حيز آخر وذلك إنما يعقل في المتحيز. والعمدة المشهورة أنا لو قدرنا العرض خاليا عن جميع الأوصاف اللازمة فإما أن لا يحتاج حينئذ إلى المحل أو يحتاج؛ والأول باطل لأنه حينئذ يكون غنيا بذاته عن المحل، والغنى بذاته عن المحل يستحيل أن يعرض له ما يحوجه إليه، لأن ما بالذات لا يزول بما بالعرض. وإن احتاج فإما أن يحتاج إلى محل مبهم وهو محال لأن مقتضى الوجود في الخارج موجود في الخارج والمبهم من حيث هو كذلك غير موجود في الخارج، أو إلى محل معين فيلزم استحالة مفارقته عنه وهو المطلوب. ولقائل أن يقول: لم لا يجوز أن لا يحتاج إليه قوله لأن الغنى بذاته عن المحل لا يعرض له ما يحوجه إليه. قلنا: العرض عندنا لا يصدق عليه أنه يجب أن لا يكون في المحل حتى يكون ذلك منافيا لحصوله في المحل، بل يصدق عليه أنه نظرا إلى ذاته لا يجب أن يكون في المحل، وهذا لا ينافيه الحصول في المحل بسبب منفصل.
Issue: The theologians and philosophers agreed on the impossibility of transference of accidents. Because transference is obtaining at a time after obtaining in another space, and that is only conceivable for that which is space-occupying (mutahayyiz). And the well-known proof is: if we suppose the accident stripped of all necessary attributes, then either it does not then need a locus, or it does need. The first is false, because then it would be in itself independent of a locus, and that which is in itself independent of a locus is impossible that what necessitates it should occur to it, because what is intrinsic does not vanish by what is accidental. And if it needs, then either it needs an indeterminate locus, which is impossible because the implication of existence in the external is existent in the external, and the indeterminate as such is not existent in the external; or it needs a determinate locus, and then the impossibility of its separation from it follows, which is the desired conclusion. And one might say: why not allow that it does not need it, saying that independence by essence from locus is not subject to what necessitates it? We say: the accident, according to us, is not described as necessarily not being in a locus such that this would conflict with its occurrence in a locus; rather it is described as, in respect of its essence, not necessarily being in a locus, and this does not conflict with its occurrence in a locus due to an external cause.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the consensus that accidents cannot transfer from one substance to another. The proof is a dichotomy: consider an accident stripped of all necessary attributes. Either it needs a locus (mahall) or it does not. If it does not need a locus, it is self-sufficient, and something self-sufficient cannot acquire a need; this is false because accidents are dependent. If it needs a locus, either the locus is indeterminate or determinate. An indeterminate locus is impossible because existence requires determination. So the locus must be determinate, meaning the accident is inseparable from that specific locus; hence transference is impossible. The objector says: why not allow that it does not need a locus, since self-sufficiency prevents acquisition of need? The answer draws a distinction: the accident is not said to be necessarily not in a locus (which would oppose its being in one); rather, it is said that in its essence it does not necessitate existence in a locus. This does not prevent it from being in a locus due to an external cause (like God creating it in a locus). So the accident's intrinsic attribute is not self-sufficiency, but rather lack of necessity for a locus; it can still be given a locus by divine action.

Key Terms
انتقال الأعراض (intiqal al-a'rad)
المحل (al-mahall)
الغنى بالذات (al-ghina bi'l-dhat)
المبهم (al-mubham)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents can transfer from one substance to another, like heat moving from one object to another.
Correction: Accidents are not independent entities; they inhere in a specific locus and cannot separate from it, as proven by the logical necessity of a determinate locus.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the proof about accidents?
A) Accidents can transfer from one locus to another.
B) Accidents are self-sufficient and need no locus.
C) Accidents require a determinate locus and cannot separate from it.
D) Accidents need an indeterminate locus.

Key Takeaway
Accidents are essentially dependent on a determinate locus and cannot transfer, as shown by a logical dichotomy that leads to the conclusion of inseparability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every quality you have is an accident inseparably tied to its locus (your body, your soul), what does that mean for your attachment to your own attributes? Are they truly yours, or are they constantly sustained by God?
?
The proof says an accident cannot acquire a need if it is self-sufficient. In your spiritual life, what 'self-sufficiency' are you clinging to that prevents you from recognising your utter need for God in every moment?
?
The response clarifies that an accident can be given a locus by an external cause. How does this truth about God's constant re-creation of your qualities change the way you view your own successes, failures, and identity?

8 min
The Core Proof: Accident Cannot Transfer Substrate
Razi presents the initial proof and first objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that an accident, stripped of attributes, cannot transfer because it must either need a substrate (leading to determination) or be self-sufficient (impossible for an accident).

Core Idea
An accident's existence depends on a specific substrate; it cannot be self-sufficient.

This lesson unpacks the opening of Razi's proof. He begins by stating the consensus that accidents cannot transfer. He defines transmission and then presents a thought experiment: if an accident were stripped of all essential attributes, it would either need a substrate or not. Each option leads to a conclusion that prevents transfer. We will follow this reasoning and examine an initial objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of transmission
Notice that Razi defines transmission as 'obtaining at a time after obtaining in another location,' which immediately restricts it to things that occupy space.
This definition is crucial because accidents are not spatial entities.
2
Understand the supposition
Razi asks us to imagine an accident stripped of all essential attributes. This thought experiment isolates the accident's relationship to a substrate.
3
Analyze the two options
Either the accident does not need a substrate (self-sufficient) or it does. The first is false because then it would be independent, and later needing a substrate would be a change in essence.
The argument uses the principle that what is essential cannot be changed by accident.
4
Examine the objection
An objector suggests that perhaps the accident does not need a substrate at all. Razi responds by clarifying that accidents are not described as 'must not be in a substrate', but rather 'considering its essence, it does not have to be in a substrate'—this leaves room for external dependence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اتفق المتكلمون والفلاسفة على امتناع انتقال الأعراض) لأن الانتقال عبارة عن الحصول في حين بعد الحصول في حيز آخر وذلك إنما يعقل في المتحيز والعمدة المشهورة أنا لو قدرنا العرض خاليا عن جميع الأوصاف اللازمة فإما أن لا يحتاج حينئذ إلى المحل أو يحتاج والأول باطل لأنه حينئذ يكون غنيا بذاته عن المحل والغنى بذاته عن المحل يستحيل أن يعرض له ما يحوجه إليه ، لان ما بالذات لا يزول بما بالعرض ، وإن احتاج فإما أن يحتاج إلى محل مبهم وهو محال لأن مقتضى الوجود في الخارج موجود في الخارج والمبهم من حيث هو كذلك غير موجود في الخارج ، أو إلى محل معين فيلزم استحالة مفارقته عنه وهو المطلوب . ولقائل أن يقول : لم لا يجوز أن لا يحتاج إليه قوله لأن الغنى بذانه عن المحل لا يعرض له ما يحوجه إليه . قلنا : العرض عندنا لا يصدق عليه أنه يجب أن لا يكون في المحل حتى يكون ذلك منافيا لحصوله في المحل ، بل يصدق عليه أنه نظرا إلى ذاته لا يجب أن يكون في المحل ، وهذا لا ينافيه الحصول في المحل بسبب منفصل. سلمنا أنه يحتاج إلى المحل لكن لم يحتاج إلى معين ، وما ذكروه منقوض باحتياج الجسم المعين إلى مكان غير معين ، ولأن الواحد بالنوع معين ، فاحتياج الواحد بالشخص إلى المحل الواحد بالنوع لا يوجب تعين المحل الواحد بالشخص (۱). أقول فى الانتقال بمعنى الحصول في حيز بعد الحصول في غيره من الأحياز عن الأعراض لا يحتاج إلى بيان ، فإن العاقل لا يمكن أن يتخيله فضلا أن يدعيه والمطلوب ههنا هو في الانتقال عنها بمعنى الحصول في محل بعد الحصول في محل غير ذلك المحل ، وهو لم يتعرض لذلك أصلا وما أورده من الحجة مزيف بما ذكره . والبرهان عليه : أن العرض هو الموجود الذى لا يتحقق وجوده الشخصي إلا بما يحل فيه والشيء المحتاج في وجوده الشخصي إلى علة لا يمكن أن يحتاج إلى علة مبهمة ، لأن المهم لا يكون من حيث هو مبهم موجوداً في الخارج وما لا يكون موجوداً في الخارج لا يفيد وجوداً في الخارج بالبديهة ، فالعرض إذا لا يتحقق وجوده إلا بمحل بعينه يتحقق وجوده الشخصي ويبطل بتبدله ذلك الموجود ولذلك يمتنع انتقاله عنه ، وأما الشيء المحتاج في صفة غير الوجود وإلى غيره من حيث طبيعة ذلك الغير كالجسم المحتاج في التحيز لا في الوجود إلى يمتنع أن ينتقل من حيز بعينه إلى حيز آخر يساوي الحيز الأول في معنى الحيز ، وهكذا إذا تعين مكان الواحد بالنوع كان الواحد بالشخص من جملة ذلك النوع محتاجاً إلى أحد أجزاء حيز ذلك النوع لا بعينه ، ولذلك أمكن انتقاله إلى حيز آخر ، وأيضاً الوجوب الشخصي الحاصل من سبب موجود معه يمكن أن تختلف شرائطه بحسب أزمنة مختلفة كالهيولى المحتاج إلى الصورة لا بعينها ، وذلك غير ما نحن فيه .
Issue: The theologians and philosophers agreed on the impossibility of the transmission of accidents. Because transmission is obtaining at a time after obtaining in another location, and that is only conceivable for a thing occupying space. The well-known proof is that if we suppose an accident stripped of all essential attributes, then either it does not need a substrate at that time, or it does. The first is false because then it would be self-sufficient without a substrate, and what is self-sufficient without a substrate cannot possibly acquire what would make it need it, since what is essential does not cease by what is accidental. And if it needs, then either it needs an indeterminate substrate – which is impossible because the requisite of existence in the outside world exists outside, and the indeterminate as such does not exist outside – or it needs a determinate substrate, so the impossibility of its separation from it follows, which is what is sought. An objector may say: Why is it not permissible that it does not need it – his statement: because what is self-sufficient without a substrate does not acquire what would make it need it. We say: According to us, it is not true of the accident that it must not be in a substrate such that that would be contrary to its being in a substrate; rather, it is true of it that, considering its essence, it does not have to be in a substrate, and this does not contradict its being in a substrate due to an external cause. We grant that it needs a substrate, but it does not need a determinate one. And what they mentioned is refuted by the need of a particular body for an indeterminate place, and because the one in species is determinate, so the need of the one in person for a substrate that is one in species does not necessitate the determination of a substrate that is one in person. (I say: Regarding transmission in the sense of obtaining in a location after obtaining in another of the locations – it does not need clarification, for a rational person cannot imagine it, let alone claim it. What is sought here is transmission from it in the sense of obtaining in a place after obtaining in a place other than that place, and he did not address that at all, and the argument he presented is invalidated by what he mentioned. The proof for it is that an accident is an existent whose individual existence is only realized by what it inheres in, and a thing that needs in its individual existence a cause cannot need an indeterminate cause, because the indeterminate as such does not exist outside, and what does not exist outside cannot bring about existence outside – this is self-evident. So an accident's existence is only realized by a specific substrate that realizes its individual existence, and that existence is nullified by its change, therefore its transmission from it is impossible. As for a thing that needs in a quality other than existence, and needs others in respect of the nature of that other, like a body that needs a location (tahayyuz) but not in existence – it is impossible for it to transfer from a specific location to another location equal to the first location in the meaning of location. And likewise, if the place of the one in species is determined, then the one in person, being one of that species, needs one of the parts of the location of that species not determinately, and therefore it is possible for it to transfer to another location. Also, the personal necessity arising from a cause that exists with it can have its conditions differ according to different times, like matter (hayula) which needs a form not determinately, and that is different from our subject.)
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the impossibility of an accident transferring from one substrate to another. Razi states that theologians and philosophers agree on this. He first explains that transmission means to exist in a place after existing in another place, which is only conceivable for bodies. The proof assumes an accident stripped of all essential qualities. Two possibilities: either it does not need a substrate (impossible because then it would be self-sufficient and could not later need one) or it needs a substrate. If it needs, either indeterminate (impossible because indeterminate does not exist outside) or determinate (so it cannot leave). An objection: why can't it not need a substrate at all? Razi answers that the accident is not described as 'must not be in a substrate' but rather 'considering its essence, it does not have to be in a substrate'; this does not prevent it from being in a substrate due to an external reason. A second objection: granted it needs a substrate, but not a specific one; analogy with a body needing a place. Razi responds in a note that the real issue is transmission from one specific substrate to another, not from one location to another. He then provides a full proof: an accident's individual existence depends on a particular substrate; it cannot need an indeterminate cause; hence it cannot leave its specific substrate. He distinguishes this from a body's need for a place (not for existence but for a quality), and from matter's need for a form, which is not determinate. Thus the impossibility stands.

Key Terms
انتقال الأعراض
المحل
الغنى بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An accident might be self-sufficient and not need a substrate.
Correction: The proof shows that an accident cannot be self-sufficient; even if it seemed independent, it still needs a substrate because its existence is not essential.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's proof, what is the consequence if an accident does not need a substrate?
A) It would be self-sufficient and then later need a substrate
B) It would be impossible for it to exist at all
C) It would be independent but then cannot acquire a need
D) It would be able to transfer freely

Key Takeaway
The core of the proof is that an accident must have a determinate substrate, making transfer impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that an accident cannot be self-sufficient without a substrate. In your daily life, when do you act as if you are self-sufficient, not recognizing your total dependence on Allah? What would it take for you to internalize that, like an accident, your very existence depends on a Sustainer?
?
The proof shows that what is essential cannot change by what is accidental. Reflect on your own character: Are there essential traits (e.g., faith, humility) that you allow accidental circumstances to erode? How can you safeguard these essentials?
?
If an accident cannot transfer from one substrate to another, how does this remind you that your relationship with Allah cannot be 'transferred' to any other being? Are there moments when you seek security or purpose in something other than Allah? Commit to turning back to Him alone.

Indeterminate Substrate and Razi's Final Proof
Razi refutes the indeterminate substrate objection and presents his own proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection about indeterminate substrate and Razi's rigorous proof that an accident's individual existence requires a specific substrate.

Core Idea
An accident's individual existence is tied to a particular substrate; no indeterminate cause suffices.

This lesson continues Razi's discussion by addressing a further objection: even if the accident needs a substrate, why can't it be indeterminate? Razi responds with an analogy and then provides his own deeper proof. He distinguishes the accident's dependence from that of bodies and matter, showing that accidents are unique in needing a specific substrate for their very existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector grants that the accident needs a substrate but argues it does not need a specifically determined one, similar to a body needing any location.
2
Analyze the refutation
Razi points out that the analogy fails because bodies need a location for a quality (tahayyuz), not for existence itself. Accidents need a substrate for their individual existence.
3
Read Razi's own proof
Razi defines an accident as that whose individual existence is only realized by a substrate. He then argues that it cannot need an indeterminate cause because the indeterminate does not exist externally.
This is a more precise argument based on existence itself.
4
Compare with body and matter
He distinguishes the accident's case from a body's need for location (which is for a property, not existence) and from matter's need for form (which is not determinate).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اتفق المتكلمون والفلاسفة على امتناع انتقال الأعراض) لأن الانتقال عبارة عن الحصول في حين بعد الحصول في حيز آخر وذلك إنما يعقل في المتحيز والعمدة المشهورة أنا لو قدرنا العرض خاليا عن جميع الأوصاف اللازمة فإما أن لا يحتاج حينئذ إلى المحل أو يحتاج والأول باطل لأنه حينئذ يكون غنيا بذاته عن المحل والغنى بذاته عن المحل يستحيل أن يعرض له ما يحوجه إليه ، لان ما بالذات لا يزول بما بالعرض ، وإن احتاج فإما أن يحتاج إلى محل مبهم وهو محال لأن مقتضى الوجود في الخارج موجود في الخارج والمبهم من حيث هو كذلك غير موجود في الخارج ، أو إلى محل معين فيلزم استحالة مفارقته عنه وهو المطلوب . ولقائل أن يقول : لم لا يجوز أن لا يحتاج إليه قوله لأن الغنى بذانه عن المحل لا يعرض له ما يحوجه إليه . قلنا : العرض عندنا لا يصدق عليه أنه يجب أن لا يكون في المحل حتى يكون ذلك منافيا لحصوله في المحل ، بل يصدق عليه أنه نظرا إلى ذاته لا يجب أن يكون في المحل ، وهذا لا ينافيه الحصول في المحل بسبب منفصل. سلمنا أنه يحتاج إلى المحل لكن لم يحتاج إلى معين ، وما ذكروه منقوض باحتياج الجسم المعين إلى مكان غير معين ، ولأن الواحد بالنوع معين ، فاحتياج الواحد بالشخص إلى المحل الواحد بالنوع لا يوجب تعين المحل الواحد بالشخص (۱). أقول فى الانتقال بمعنى الحصول في حيز بعد الحصول في غيره من الأحياز عن الأعراض لا يحتاج إلى بيان ، فإن العاقل لا يمكن أن يتخيله فضلا أن يدعيه والمطلوب ههنا هو في الانتقال عنها بمعنى الحصول في محل بعد الحصول في محل غير ذلك المحل ، وهو لم يتعرض لذلك أصلا وما أورده من الحجة مزيف بما ذكره . والبرهان عليه : أن العرض هو الموجود الذى لا يتحقق وجوده الشخصي إلا بما يحل فيه والشيء المحتاج في وجوده الشخصي إلى علة لا يمكن أن يحتاج إلى علة مبهمة ، لأن المهم لا يكون من حيث هو مبهم موجوداً في الخارج وما لا يكون موجوداً في الخارج لا يفيد وجوداً في الخارج بالبديهة ، فالعرض إذا لا يتحقق وجوده إلا بمحل بعينه يتحقق وجوده الشخصي ويبطل بتبدله ذلك الموجود ولذلك يمتنع انتقاله عنه ، وأما الشيء المحتاج في صفة غير الوجود وإلى غيره من حيث طبيعة ذلك الغير كالجسم المحتاج في التحيز لا في الوجود إلى يمتنع أن ينتقل من حيز بعينه إلى حيز آخر يساوي الحيز الأول في معنى الحيز ، وهكذا إذا تعين مكان الواحد بالنوع كان الواحد بالشخص من جملة ذلك النوع محتاجاً إلى أحد أجزاء حيز ذلك النوع لا بعينه ، ولذلك أمكن انتقاله إلى حيز آخر ، وأيضاً الوجوب الشخصي الحاصل من سبب موجود معه يمكن أن تختلف شرائطه بحسب أزمنة مختلفة كالهيولى المحتاج إلى الصورة لا بعينها ، وذلك غير ما نحن فيه .
Issue: The theologians and philosophers agreed on the impossibility of the transmission of accidents. Because transmission is obtaining at a time after obtaining in another location, and that is only conceivable for a thing occupying space. The well-known proof is that if we suppose an accident stripped of all essential attributes, then either it does not need a substrate at that time, or it does. The first is false because then it would be self-sufficient without a substrate, and what is self-sufficient without a substrate cannot possibly acquire what would make it need it, since what is essential does not cease by what is accidental. And if it needs, then either it needs an indeterminate substrate – which is impossible because the requisite of existence in the outside world exists outside, and the indeterminate as such does not exist outside – or it needs a determinate substrate, so the impossibility of its separation from it follows, which is what is sought. An objector may say: Why is it not permissible that it does not need it – his statement: because what is self-sufficient without a substrate does not acquire what would make it need it. We say: According to us, it is not true of the accident that it must not be in a substrate such that that would be contrary to its being in a substrate; rather, it is true of it that, considering its essence, it does not have to be in a substrate, and this does not contradict its being in a substrate due to an external cause. We grant that it needs a substrate, but it does not need a determinate one. And what they mentioned is refuted by the need of a particular body for an indeterminate place, and because the one in species is determinate, so the need of the one in person for a substrate that is one in species does not necessitate the determination of a substrate that is one in person. (I say: Regarding transmission in the sense of obtaining in a location after obtaining in another of the locations – it does not need clarification, for a rational person cannot imagine it, let alone claim it. What is sought here is transmission from it in the sense of obtaining in a place after obtaining in a place other than that place, and he did not address that at all, and the argument he presented is invalidated by what he mentioned. The proof for it is that an accident is an existent whose individual existence is only realized by what it inheres in, and a thing that needs in its individual existence a cause cannot need an indeterminate cause, because the indeterminate as such does not exist outside, and what does not exist outside cannot bring about existence outside – this is self-evident. So an accident's existence is only realized by a specific substrate that realizes its individual existence, and that existence is nullified by its change, therefore its transmission from it is impossible. As for a thing that needs in a quality other than existence, and needs others in respect of the nature of that other, like a body that needs a location (tahayyuz) but not in existence – it is impossible for it to transfer from a specific location to another location equal to the first location in the meaning of location. And likewise, if the place of the one in species is determined, then the one in person, being one of that species, needs one of the parts of the location of that species not determinately, and therefore it is possible for it to transfer to another location. Also, the personal necessity arising from a cause that exists with it can have its conditions differ according to different times, like matter (hayula) which needs a form not determinately, and that is different from our subject.)
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the impossibility of an accident transferring from one substrate to another. Razi states that theologians and philosophers agree on this. He first explains that transmission means to exist in a place after existing in another place, which is only conceivable for bodies. The proof assumes an accident stripped of all essential qualities. Two possibilities: either it does not need a substrate (impossible because then it would be self-sufficient and could not later need one) or it needs a substrate. If it needs, either indeterminate (impossible because indeterminate does not exist outside) or determinate (so it cannot leave). An objection: why can't it not need a substrate at all? Razi answers that the accident is not described as 'must not be in a substrate' but rather 'considering its essence, it does not have to be in a substrate'; this does not prevent it from being in a substrate due to an external reason. A second objection: granted it needs a substrate, but not a specific one; analogy with a body needing a place. Razi responds in a note that the real issue is transmission from one specific substrate to another, not from one location to another. He then provides a full proof: an accident's individual existence depends on a particular substrate; it cannot need an indeterminate cause; hence it cannot leave its specific substrate. He distinguishes this from a body's need for a place (not for existence but for a quality), and from matter's need for a form, which is not determinate. Thus the impossibility stands.

Key Terms
المحل المبهم
الواحد بالنوع
الواحد بالشخص
الوجود الشخصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An accident could transfer to a different substrate of the same type, like a body moves to a different location.
Correction: Accidents depend on their specific substrate for existence, unlike bodies which depend on location only for a quality.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's own proof, why can't an accident need an indeterminate substrate?
A) Because the indeterminate does not exist in the external world
B) Because accidents prefer determinate things
C) Because the substrate must be larger than the accident
D) Because the accident has no location

Key Takeaway
Razi's final proof establishes that an accident's individual existence requires a specific substrate, making transfer logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi proves that an indeterminate cause cannot bring about existence. When you pray, do you turn to a vague 'higher power' or to Allah, the Specific, the Determinate? What would change in your salah if you truly believed that only the Specific One can grant existence?
?
Your very existence as a unique person depends on a specific cause from your Lord. Are you living as if your individuality is a gift from that Specific Cause, or do you sometimes act as if it is self-generated? How can you acknowledge this dependence today?
?
Consider an attribute like your knowledge. According to this lesson, it is an accident that requires a specific substrate (your soul/body). If that substrate changes (death), your knowledge would not transfer. How does this urgency affect your intention to seek knowledge? Are you seeking knowledge that will remain with you through its specific cause, or that which perishes?

The Two Core Proofs for the Impossibility of Accident Permanence
Imam Razi's first two proofs for accident immortality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari reasoning that an accident cannot persist because permanence would be an accident subsisting by another accident, and because permanence would make its non-existence impossible.

Core Idea
If an accident could persist, it would either require an accident upon accident or make its own disappearance impossible.

Imam Razi begins by stating the Ash'ari consensus on the impossibility of accident permanence. He then provides two foundational proofs: one based on the nature of permanence as an attribute, and another based on the logical consequences of permanence for the accident's ceasing to exist. This lesson unpacks these two arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the first line about the Ash'ari consensus. Note that the topic is the permanence of accidents (بقاء العرض).
The Ash'ari position is that accidents do not last; they are renewed each moment.
2
Examine the first proof
The first proof says permanence is an attribute, so if an accident remained, it would mean an attribute (permanence) subsisting by an attribute (the accident), i.e., accident upon accident, which is rejected.
This is a quick argument based on the definition of permanence as a property.
3
Study the second proof's structure
The second proof is longer. It assumes permanence and then examines why non-existence would become impossible. It breaks down possible causes of non-existence into existent or non-existent, and then into necessitating or willful.
Notice the careful classification of causes; this is a typical kalam analytical method.
4
Follow the conclusion
The proof concludes that if permanence were valid, non-existence would be impossible. But accidents do cease, so permanence is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: permanence leads to a contradiction with observed reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اتفقت الأشاعرة على امتناع بقاء العرض وهو لأن البقاء صفة فلو بقى العرض لزم قيام العرض بالعرض ولأنه لو صح بقاء العرض لامتناع عدمه ، لأن عدمه بعد البقاء لا يجوز أن يكون واجباً وإلا لانقلب الشيء من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع ، بل يكون جائز أوله سبب وهو إما وجودى أو عدمى ، أما الوجود فإما الموجب كما يقال إنه يفنى الطريان الصد محال ، لأن طريان الضد على المحل مشروط بعدم الضد الأول عنه ، فلو عمل ذلك العدم به ازم الدور ، وإما المختار كما يقال الله تعالى بعدمه وهو محال لأن المعدم هند الإعدام إما أن يكون قد صدر عنه أمر أو لم يصدر ، فإن صدر عنه أمر فتأثير فى تحصيل أمر وجودى فهذا يكون إيجاداً لا إعداماً ، وإن لم يصدر عنه أمر فهو محال لأن القادر لا بد له من أثر ، وأما المدمى فإنه ينتفى لاتفاء شرطه لكن شرطه الجوهر وهو باق والكلام في كيفية عدمه كالكلام فى كيفية عدم العرض فثبت أنه لو صح بقاؤه لامتنع عدمه ، لكنه قد يعدم لامتنع بقاؤه فقيل على الأول لا نسلم أن البقاء عرض سلمناه لكن لم لا يجوز قيام مثل هذا العرض بالعرض ، وعلى الثانى لم لا يجوز أن يجب عدمه بعد بقائه في زمان معين ، وهذا لأن العرض عندكم كان جائز الوجود فى الزمان الأول ثم انقلب ممتنعا ثم الزمان الثانى فلم لا يجوز أن يبقى أزمنة كثيرة ثم يلتهى إلى زمان يصير فيه ممتنع الوجود بعينه وحينئذ يفنى لا لسبب سلمنا أنه لابد له من سبب لكن لم لا يجوز أن ينتفى لانتفاء الشرط وهو أن تكون الأعراض الباقية مشروطة بأعراض لا تبقى فعند انقطاعها يفنى الباقى ، ولا يبقى في دفع هذا الاحتمال إلا الاستقراء الذى لا يفيد إلا الغان، ثم حتجوا على جواز بقائها بأنها لو كانت مسكنة الوجود فى الزمان الأول وتكون كذلك فى الزمان الثانى إذ لو جاز أن ينقلب الممكن لذاته في زمان ممتنعا في زمان آخر الجاز أن ينقلب الممتنع في زمان واجبا في زمان آخر ، وعلى هذا يجوز أن يكون العالم قبل وجوده ممتنعا الوجود لعينه ثم انقلب واجبا لعينه ، وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم نفى الصانع تعالى عنه علوا كبيرا (1) .
Issue: The Ash'arites agreed on the impossibility of the permanence of the accident. This is because permanence is an attribute; so if the accident remained, it would entail the subsistence of an accident by an accident. And because if the permanence of the accident were valid, its non-existence would be impossible, for its non-existence after permanence cannot be necessary, otherwise a thing would change from essential possibility to impossibility. Rather, it must be contingent and have a cause, which is either existent or non-existent. As for the existent, it is either necessitating, as when it is said that it annihilates by the occurrence of the opposite? That is impossible, because the occurrence of the opposite on the locus is conditioned by the absence of the first opposite, so if that absence acted upon it, there would be circularity. Or it is willful, as when it is said that God annihilates it by His will, and that is impossible because the annihilator, when he annihilates, either issues an act or not. If an act issues from him, then it is an influence in the attainment of an existent thing, and this would be bringing into existence, not annihilation. If no act issues from him, that is impossible because the capable must have an effect. As for the non-existent, it disappears by the non-existence of its condition, but its condition is the substance which remains, and the discourse about how it ceases is like the discourse about how the accident ceases. So it is established that if its permanence were valid, its non-existence would be impossible. But it does cease to exist, so its permanence is impossible. Then it was said regarding the first: we do not grant that permanence is an accident; if we grant it, why is it not permissible for such an accident to subsist by another accident? And regarding the second: why is it not permissible for its non-existence to become necessary after its permanence in a specific time? For the accident, according to you, was contingently existent in the first time, then became impossible in the second time; so why is it not allowed that it remains for many times and then reaches a time in which it becomes impossible in itself and then perishes without a cause? We grant that it must have a cause, but why is it not allowed that it be negated by the negation of a condition, namely that persistent accidents are conditioned by accidents that do not persist, so when they cease, the persistent one ceases? There remains no way to refute this probability except through induction, which only yields supposition. Then they argued for the possibility of its permanence by saying: if it were possible in the first time and likewise in the second time, because if it were permissible for a possible in itself to become impossible in another time, then it would be permissible for the impossible to become necessary in another time. And on this basis, it would be permissible that the world, before its existence, was impossible in itself and then became necessary in itself, and on this assumption, the denial of the Creator follows—highly exalted is He above that.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a complex proof and refutation. Imam Razi begins by stating the Ash'ari consensus on the impossibility of accident permanence. Two proofs are given: first, permanence is an attribute, so if an accident remained, it would mean an attribute subsisting by another attribute (accident upon accident), which is rejected. Second, if the accident remained, its non-existence would become impossible, because it would have to have a cause. The proof then examines possible causes: a necessitating cause (like an opposite) leads to circularity; a willful cause (God annihilating) would either be creation or nothing; non-existence by removal of condition fails because the condition (substance) remains. Thus if permanence were possible, non-existence would be impossible. But accidents do cease, so permanence is impossible. Then objections are raised: (1) permanence might not be an accident, and (2) perhaps the accident could become impossible after a time. The author answers these: the first by noting that even if it were, the objection remains; the second by arguing that contingency cannot change to impossibility, and granting causes, they ask why it cannot cease by removal of condition. Induction is the only way, but it only gives probability. Then a counter-argument is presented: if contingency could change, then impossibility could become necessity, leading to denial of the Creator. Thus the opinion is refuted.

Key Terms
بقاء العرض
قيام العرض بالعرض
الإمكان الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes accidents cannot have causes at all.
Correction: The argument does not deny accidents have causes; it shows that if an accident persisted, no coherent cause could explain its cessation.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why is a necessitating cause (like an opposite) impossible for making a persistent accident cease?
A) Because the opposite is also an accident and cannot act.
B) Because the occurrence of the opposite requires the absence of the first opposite, leading to circularity.
C) Because God does not use necessitating causes.
D) Because the opposite would itself need to persist.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position that accidents cannot persist is supported by two proofs: the accident-upon-accident argument and the impossibility of accounting for its cessation if it persisted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every accident in your body and your life is renewed each moment by God, how does that change your attachment to your health, your wealth, or your relationships? Are you holding on to something that literally does not last for two consecutive moments? Reflect on one specific thing you fear losing, and ask yourself: Is my fear based on the illusion of permanence that this passage destroys? What would it mean to truly live knowing that everything is being recreated every instant?
?
Imam Razi argues that if an accident persisted, its non-existence would be impossible, but we see things vanish. What does this say about your own sense of control? Do you assume that your own actions or intentions persist? The proof shows that even your own thoughts (accidents) cannot stay; they are constantly created and annihilated by God. How should this reshape your humility before Allah? In your next prayer, will you remember that even your concentration is an accident that God gives and takes away each moment?
?
The proof demonstrates that only impossible things lead to contradiction. If you recognise that the world is constantly changing and that nothing you experience persists, how does that affect your longing for permanence? Does your soul yearn for something that truly lasts? How does this logical argument point you toward the necessity of the Eternal (Al-Baqi) as the only true reality? What is one step you can take today to shift your attachment from the transient to the Eternal?

Objections and Responses to the Impossibility of Accident Permanence
Objections and responses to the Ash'ari proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main objections raised against the Ash'ari proof and how Imam Razi responds, including the final counter-argument that denying accident permanence leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Objections to the proof are met with logical responses, and a counter-argument shows that denying accident permanence preserves divine power.

After presenting the proof, Imam Razi addresses potential objections. Opponents may question whether permanence is an accident, why accident-upon-accident is impossible, and why non-existence after permanence cannot become necessary in a later time. The refutations are followed by a powerful counter-argument: if contingency could change to impossibility within time, then the world could have changed from impossible to necessary, denying the Creator. This lesson unpacks these dialectical moves.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection and response
The objector says: 'We do not grant that permanence is an accident.' Then even if it were, why can't an accident subsist by another accident? Notice the response is implied as not granting the objection's force.
The response seems to challenge the objector's premise directly.
2
Examine the second objection and response
The objector asks: 'Why can't the accident become impossible after persisting for some time?' The response uses a reductio: if contingency can change to impossibility, then impossibility could change to necessity, which would make the world necessarily existent from possible, denying the Creator.
This is a strong theological argument tying the position to divine existence.
3
Understand the objection about conditional cessation
The objector suggests that persistent accidents might depend on other non-persistent accidents, so when those cease, the persistent one also ceases. Razi says this can only be refuted by induction, which gives only probability.
This shows the limits of empirical reasoning in such matters.
4
Note the final argument
The passage ends with the counter-argument from the opponents that if accident permanence is impossible, then nothing persists, which is absurd. But the Ash'ari response is that accidents are renewed, substances persist.
This sets up the broader debate about change and persistence.

Source Text

مسألة : اتفقت الأشاعرة على امتناع بقاء العرض وهو لأن البقاء صفة فلو بقى العرض لزم قيام العرض بالعرض ولأنه لو صح بقاء العرض لامتناع عدمه ، لأن عدمه بعد البقاء لا يجوز أن يكون واجباً وإلا لانقلب الشيء من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع ، بل يكون جائز أوله سبب وهو إما وجودى أو عدمى ، أما الوجود فإما الموجب كما يقال إنه يفنى الطريان الصد محال ، لأن طريان الضد على المحل مشروط بعدم الضد الأول عنه ، فلو عمل ذلك العدم به ازم الدور ، وإما المختار كما يقال الله تعالى بعدمه وهو محال لأن المعدم هند الإعدام إما أن يكون قد صدر عنه أمر أو لم يصدر ، فإن صدر عنه أمر فتأثير فى تحصيل أمر وجودى فهذا يكون إيجاداً لا إعداماً ، وإن لم يصدر عنه أمر فهو محال لأن القادر لا بد له من أثر ، وأما المدمى فإنه ينتفى لاتفاء شرطه لكن شرطه الجوهر وهو باق والكلام في كيفية عدمه كالكلام فى كيفية عدم العرض فثبت أنه لو صح بقاؤه لامتنع عدمه ، لكنه قد يعدم لامتنع بقاؤه فقيل على الأول لا نسلم أن البقاء عرض سلمناه لكن لم لا يجوز قيام مثل هذا العرض بالعرض ، وعلى الثانى لم لا يجوز أن يجب عدمه بعد بقائه في زمان معين ، وهذا لأن العرض عندكم كان جائز الوجود فى الزمان الأول ثم انقلب ممتنعا ثم الزمان الثانى فلم لا يجوز أن يبقى أزمنة كثيرة ثم يلتهى إلى زمان يصير فيه ممتنع الوجود بعينه وحينئذ يفنى لا لسبب سلمنا أنه لابد له من سبب لكن لم لا يجوز أن ينتفى لانتفاء الشرط وهو أن تكون الأعراض الباقية مشروطة بأعراض لا تبقى فعند انقطاعها يفنى الباقى ، ولا يبقى في دفع هذا الاحتمال إلا الاستقراء الذى لا يفيد إلا الغان، ثم حتجوا على جواز بقائها بأنها لو كانت مسكنة الوجود فى الزمان الأول وتكون كذلك فى الزمان الثانى إذ لو جاز أن ينقلب الممكن لذاته في زمان ممتنعا في زمان آخر الجاز أن ينقلب الممتنع في زمان واجبا في زمان آخر ، وعلى هذا يجوز أن يكون العالم قبل وجوده ممتنعا الوجود لعينه ثم انقلب واجبا لعينه ، وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم نفى الصانع تعالى عنه علوا كبيرا (1) .
Issue: The Ash'arites agreed on the impossibility of the permanence of the accident. This is because permanence is an attribute; so if the accident remained, it would entail the subsistence of an accident by an accident. And because if the permanence of the accident were valid, its non-existence would be impossible, for its non-existence after permanence cannot be necessary, otherwise a thing would change from essential possibility to impossibility. Rather, it must be contingent and have a cause, which is either existent or non-existent. As for the existent, it is either necessitating, as when it is said that it annihilates by the occurrence of the opposite? That is impossible, because the occurrence of the opposite on the locus is conditioned by the absence of the first opposite, so if that absence acted upon it, there would be circularity. Or it is willful, as when it is said that God annihilates it by His will, and that is impossible because the annihilator, when he annihilates, either issues an act or not. If an act issues from him, then it is an influence in the attainment of an existent thing, and this would be bringing into existence, not annihilation. If no act issues from him, that is impossible because the capable must have an effect. As for the non-existent, it disappears by the non-existence of its condition, but its condition is the substance which remains, and the discourse about how it ceases is like the discourse about how the accident ceases. So it is established that if its permanence were valid, its non-existence would be impossible. But it does cease to exist, so its permanence is impossible. Then it was said regarding the first: we do not grant that permanence is an accident; if we grant it, why is it not permissible for such an accident to subsist by another accident? And regarding the second: why is it not permissible for its non-existence to become necessary after its permanence in a specific time? For the accident, according to you, was contingently existent in the first time, then became impossible in the second time; so why is it not allowed that it remains for many times and then reaches a time in which it becomes impossible in itself and then perishes without a cause? We grant that it must have a cause, but why is it not allowed that it be negated by the negation of a condition, namely that persistent accidents are conditioned by accidents that do not persist, so when they cease, the persistent one ceases? There remains no way to refute this probability except through induction, which only yields supposition. Then they argued for the possibility of its permanence by saying: if it were possible in the first time and likewise in the second time, because if it were permissible for a possible in itself to become impossible in another time, then it would be permissible for the impossible to become necessary in another time. And on this basis, it would be permissible that the world, before its existence, was impossible in itself and then became necessary in itself, and on this assumption, the denial of the Creator follows—highly exalted is He above that.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a complex proof and refutation. Imam Razi begins by stating the Ash'ari consensus on the impossibility of accident permanence. Two proofs are given: first, permanence is an attribute, so if an accident remained, it would mean an attribute subsisting by another attribute (accident upon accident), which is rejected. Second, if the accident remained, its non-existence would become impossible, because it would have to have a cause. The proof then examines possible causes: a necessitating cause (like an opposite) leads to circularity; a willful cause (God annihilating) would either be creation or nothing; non-existence by removal of condition fails because the condition (substance) remains. Thus if permanence were possible, non-existence would be impossible. But accidents do cease, so permanence is impossible. Then objections are raised: (1) permanence might not be an accident, and (2) perhaps the accident could become impossible after a time. The author answers these: the first by noting that even if it were, the objection remains; the second by arguing that contingency cannot change to impossibility, and granting causes, they ask why it cannot cease by removal of condition. Induction is the only way, but it only gives probability. Then a counter-argument is presented: if contingency could change, then impossibility could become necessity, leading to denial of the Creator. Thus the opinion is refuted.

Key Terms
طريان الضد
المعدِم
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari proof is easily refuted by pointing out that accidents could become impossible after some time.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that such a change would imply that possibility and necessity are time-dependent, leading to the denial of the Creator, which is unacceptable.

Checkpoint
What consequence does Imam Razi claim follows if we allow an accident to change from possibly existent to impossible in a later time?
A) It would prove that accidents can persist.
B) It would imply that the impossible could become necessary, leading to denial of the Creator.
C) It would show that induction is necessary.
D) It would make God's will irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The objections to the Ash'ari proof are refuted by logical arguments, and a counter-argument shows that denying accident permanence safeguards the doctrine of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you rely on induction (empirical observation) rather than logical certainty in matters of faith, how does that affect your confidence in divine truths? This lesson shows induction only yields supposition. Are there areas of your belief where you are basing them on mere probability rather than divine revelation? What will you do to secure certainty?
?
The reductio argument shows that denying accident permanence leads to denying the Creator. Have you ever held a belief that, if traced logically, would undermine your faith in Allah's sole power of creation? How can you examine your assumptions to ensure they align with Tawheed, especially in how you think about cause and effect?
?
This passage demonstrates that Allah's power is not limited by the persistence of created attributes. Do you live as though your own strengths and weaknesses are permanent or as though only Allah's continuous will sustains them? What would change in your daily reliance on Allah if you truly believed that every attribute you have is renewed moment by moment?

Refuting Claims of Necessary Knowledge
Imam Razi refutes Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's claim that knowledge of accident permanence is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's critique of the claim that knowledge of accident permanence is necessary, and why he considers the supporting arguments as unsubstantiated assertions.

Core Idea
Necessity in knowledge requires proof; bare assertions are insufficient.

Imam Razi addresses a specific claim made by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri regarding the necessary knowledge of accident permanence. He dissects the reasoning, showing that the alleged conditions and consequences are merely asserted without evidence. This passage illustrates the rigorous standard of proof required in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time to understand the two arguments.
2
Identify Abu al-Husayn's two claims
The passage mentions two claims: (1) the condition about the opposite, and (2) the nature of the annihilator's act. Identify each in the text.
3
Understand Razi's counter-arguments
Razi refutes each claim, calling them 'bare claims' (دعوى مجردة) and demanding evidence. Note why he rejects them.
Observe Razi's method: he does not accept assertions without proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : أبو الحسين البصرى يدعى أن العلم ببقاء الأعراض كالسواد والبياض ضرورى . وقوله : بأن طريان الضد على المحل مشروط بعدم الضد الأول دعوى مجردة لا يقبلها القائل بأن الضد ينتفى عند طريان ضده ، بل يقول عدم الضد الأول معلل بطريان الضد على محله وترجيح أحد القولين على الآخر محتاج إلى دليل . وقوله : المعدم إن صدر عنه أمر فتأثيره في تحصيل أمر وجودى أيضا غير مسلم عنده ، فإنه يقول تأثيره أمر متجدد وذلك الأمر ليس إيجاد معدوم بل هو إعدام موجود ، مالدليل على أن الأول ممكن وحده دونه
I say: Abu al-Husayn al-Basri claims that knowledge of the permanence of accidents like blackness and whiteness is necessary. His statement that the occurrence of the opposite on the locus is conditioned by the absence of the first opposite is a bare claim not accepted by the one who says that the opposite ceases at the occurrence of its opposite; rather, he says the absence of the first opposite is caused by the occurrence of the opposite on its locus, and preferring one of the two statements over the other requires evidence. And his statement that the annihilator, if an act issues from him, his influence is in the attainment of an existent thing is also not conceded by him, for he says his influence is a renewing act, and that act is not bringing a non-existent into existence but rather annihilating an existent. What is the evidence that the first is possible to the exclusion of the latter?
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
In this marginal note, Imam Razi responds to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's claim that we have necessary knowledge that accidents persist (like blackness). Abu al-Husayn had argued that the occurrence of an opposite is conditioned by the absence of the first opposite, and that the annihilator's act is to create a new accident. Imam Razi refutes each: the statement about the condition is a mere assertion not accepted by those who hold that the opposite disappears automatically; there is no proof for one view over the other. Regarding the annihilator, if God creates a new accident, that is a new creation, not annihilation. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
ضرورة العلم
طريان الضد
دعوى مجردة
إعدام موجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge that accidents persist is necessary and requires no proof.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that both arguments for necessity are mere assertions without evidence, so the necessity claim is unsupported.

Checkpoint

B) It is a bare claim that requires evidence.
C) It is proven by induction.
D) It is widely accepted.

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge claims must be supported by evidence; otherwise they remain unsubstantiated assertions.

The Refutation of a Single Accident in Two Loci
Imam Razi states the issue, lists dissenters, and gives a rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that a single accident cannot inhere in two distinct loci, based on the analogy with a body in two places.

Core Idea
If an accident could be in two loci, a body could be in two places — an absurdity.

This passage introduces a central theological debate: can one accident exist in two different substances? Imam Razi states the near-consensus on its impossibility, notes the exceptions of Abu Hashim and the philosophers, and provides a logical proof. The proof uses an analogy: if an accident could be in two loci, then by the same logic a body could be in two places, which is impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the overall flow.
Notice the structure: issue, dissenters, proof, additional argument, conclusion.
2
Identify the two dissenting views
The passage mentions Abu Hashim (composition) and ancient philosophers (relations). Understand why they are exceptions.
Abu Hashim limits his view to two loci; philosophers only for relations.
3
Understand the analogy proof
The proof argues that if an accident could inhere in two loci, then by analogy a body could be in two places, which is rationally impossible.
This is a qiyas (analogical) argument based on rational impossibility.
4
Consider the argument about three loci
Since Abu Hashim agrees that inherence in three loci is impossible, there is an inconsistency. The author demands a distinction.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author states that referring the difficulty of disengagement to the free agent (God) is better than accepting the absurdity.
This concludes the first proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اتفقوا على أن العرض الواحد لا يحل في محلين إلا أبا هاشم فإنه قال : التأليف عرض واحد حال فى محلين ووافقنا على أنه يستحيل قيامه بأكثر من محلين، وجمع من قدماء الفلاسفة زعموا أن الإضافة عرض واحد قائم بمحلين كالجوار والقرب . لنا لو جاز في العقل أن يكون الحال في هذا المحل عين الحال فى ذلك الجاز أن يكون الحاصل في هذا المكان هو الحاصل في ذلك فيكون الجسم الواحد حاصلا في مكانين ، ولأنه وافق على امتناع الحلول فى الثلاثة فنطالبه بالفرق وإحالة صعوبة التفكيك على الفاعل المختار أولى من التزام هذا المحال (۱)
أقول : يفهم من كون العرض الواحد حالا في محلين معنيان أحدهما أن المرض الواحد الحال في محل هو بعينه حال فى الآخر ، والثاني أن العرض الواحد حال في مجموع شيئين صارا باجتماعهما محلا واحدا له. والأول باطل بما قاله ، فإنه قاس العرض على الجسم الممتنع كونه في مكانين ولو ذلك الفيل يمتنع اجتماع عرضين في محل واحد قياسا على امتناع الجسمين في مكان واحد ، السكن اجتماع الأعراض الكثيرة في محل واحد كالسواد والحركة والتأليف والحياة بما لا يدفعه أحد ، والدليل على بطلانه أن المعرض محتاج في وجوده إلى المحل الذى هو فيه ولو أمكن حلوله في محلين ثبت استغناء كل واحد منهما عن الآخر فيكون محتاجا إلى كل واحد منهما ومستغنيا عنه معا وهو باطل . والثانى لم تقم حجة على امتناعه والفلاسفة يقولون بقيام العرض الواحد بمحل ينقسم إلى أجزاء كثيرة كالوحدة بالعشرة الواحدة ، والتثليث مجموع الأضلاع المثلثة المحيطة سطح، والحياة بنية متجزئة إلى أعضاء .
وأبو هاشم إنما قال بقيام تأليف واحد مجوهرين لأن عدم انفكاك المؤلف عنهما دون المتجاورين محتاج إلى علة ، ولو قام بكل واحد منهما ذلك للملة يتعذر انفكاكهما ، ولم يقل بقيامه بما فوق الاثنين ، الان التأليف لو قام مثلا بثلاثة جواهر ثم أزيل واحد منها من الاجتماع بالباقيين وجب انعدام التأليف لانعدام محله فلا يبقى الباقيان ، والفين وذلك بخلاف ما عليه الوجود .
وقال بعد إبطال قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين في جواب أبي هاشم : إن إحالة صعوبة التفكيك على الفاعل المختار بأن يلتصق أحدهما بالآخر أولى من النزام جوال حلول العرض في محلين .
Issue: They agreed that a single accident does not inhere in two loci except Abu Hashim, for he said: composition (ta'lif) is a single accident inhering in two loci, and he agreed with us that it is impossible for it to subsist in more than two loci. A group of ancient philosophers claimed that relation (idafa) is a single accident subsisting in two loci, like adjacency and proximity. Our proof: If it were rationally permissible for the inherer in this locus to be the same as the inherer in that locus, then it would be permissible for the obtained in this place to be the same as the obtained in that place, and thus a single body would be obtained in two places. And because he agreed on the impossibility of inherence in three, we demand the difference. And referring the difficulty of disengagement to the free agent is preferable to committing this absurdity. I say: From the concept of a single accident inhering in two loci, two meanings are understood: first, that the single accident inhering in a locus is the same as the one inhering in another; second, that the single accident inheres in the aggregate of two things that become one locus by their combination. The first is invalid by what he said, for he analogized the accident to the body, which cannot be in two places. And if that were the case, it would be impossible for two accidents to gather in a single locus, by analogy to the impossibility of two bodies in one place. But the gathering of many accidents in one locus, like blackness, motion, composition, and life, is undeniable. And the proof of its invalidity is that the accident is needful in its existence of the locus in which it is; if it could inhere in two loci, then it would be established that each is independent of the other, so it would be needful of each and independent of each simultaneously—that is false. As for the second, no proof has been established for its impossibility, and the philosophers say that a single accident can subsist in a locus that divides into many parts, like unity with the ten, and triangularity with the set of triangular sides enclosing a surface, and life with a structure divided into limbs. Abu Hashim only said that a single composition subsists in two substances because the non-disengagement of the composed from them, unlike the adjacent, needs a cause; if it subsisted in each of them, their disengagement would be impossible. And he did not say it subsists in more than two, because if composition existed in three substances and then one was removed from the assembly, composition would cease due to the cessation of its locus, and the remaining two would not remain, contrary to what is observed. And after refuting the subsistence of a single accident in two loci, in response to Abu Hashim, he said: referring the difficulty of disengagement to the free agent, by attaching one to the other, is better than committing to the inherence of an accident in two loci.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage deals with the question of whether a single accident can inhere in two distinct loci. Imam Razi states the consensus except for Abu Hashim (who considered composition as a single accident in two substances) and the philosophers (who considered relations like adjacency as such). The proof: if a single accident could be in two loci, then by analogy a single body could be in two places, which is absurd. Also, since Abu Hashim agrees that it cannot be in three, he must explain the difference. The author then explains two meanings of 'single accident in two loci' and rejects the first (the accident being exactly the same in each locus) because that would imply independence and dependence together, and also because many accidents can coexist in one locus, so the analogy with bodies fails. The second meaning (inhering in a composite locus) is not proven impossible; in fact, philosophers accept it (e.g., unity in ten things). Abu Hashim's view is explained: he only allows it for composition because the two substances become like one locus, and disengagement is impossible without a cause. The author concludes that referring the difficulty to God's action is better than accepting the absurdity of inherence in two loci.

Key Terms
العرض الواحد
الحلول في محلين
التأليف
الإضافة
الفاعل المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against a single accident inhering in two loci?
A) If it were possible, two accidents could not coexist.
B) If it were possible, a body could be in two places.
C) The Qur'an prohibits it.
D) Accidents cannot be shared.

Key Takeaway
A single accident cannot inhere in two distinct loci because that would entail a body being in two places, which is rationally impossible.

Reflect on a belief you hold that you have not examined for logical consistency. How might this lesson's method of testing by analogy help you refine your understanding of God's attributes?

Two Meanings of a Single Accident in Two Loci
Imam Razi distinguishes two interpretations of a single accident in two loci, refutes the first, and discusses the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between a single accident being exactly the same in two loci versus inhering in a composite locus, and why the first is impossible while the second is possible.

Core Idea
One accident cannot be the same in two separate loci, but can inhere in a combined locus.

Imam Razi clarifies that the phrase 'a single accident in two loci' can be understood in two ways: either the very same accident exists in two distinct loci, or the accident inheres in the aggregate of two things as one locus. He then refutes the first meaning using both analogy and a proof from need, and shows that the second meaning has no proof against it, supported by philosophers' examples like unity in ten. This lesson explores these two interpretations and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two meanings
The passage explicitly states two interpretations. Note them clearly.
First meaning: same accident in two separate loci. Second meaning: accident in a composite locus.
2
Understand why the first meaning is invalid
The passage gives two proofs: an analogy with bodies and an argument about the accident's need for its locus.
The second proof shows logical contradiction: accident would be dependent and independent simultaneously.
3
Examine the second meaning
The passage states no proof has been established against it, and philosophers give examples like unity, triangularity, and life.
These examples show a single accident can be present in multiple parts of a composite whole.
4
Consider Abu Hashim's view on composition
Abu Hashim allows a single composition in two substances because the two become like one locus, and disengagement would need a cause.
He does not extend it to three because removing one substance would annihilate the composition.
5
Reflect on the final conclusion
The author reiterates that referring the difficulty to God is better than accepting the absurdity of the first meaning.
This concludes the discussion.
