مسألة : اتفقوا على أن العرض الواحد لا يحل في محلين إلا أبا هاشم فإنه قال : التأليف عرض واحد حال فى محلين ووافقنا على أنه يستحيل قيامه بأكثر من محلين، وجمع من قدماء الفلاسفة زعموا أن الإضافة عرض واحد قائم بمحلين كالجوار والقرب . لنا لو جاز في العقل أن يكون الحال في هذا المحل عين الحال فى ذلك الجاز أن يكون الحاصل في هذا المكان هو الحاصل في ذلك فيكون الجسم الواحد حاصلا في مكانين ، ولأنه وافق على امتناع الحلول فى الثلاثة فنطالبه بالفرق وإحالة صعوبة التفكيك على الفاعل المختار أولى من التزام هذا المحال (۱)
أقول : يفهم من كون العرض الواحد حالا في محلين معنيان أحدهما أن المرض الواحد الحال في محل هو بعينه حال فى الآخر ، والثاني أن العرض الواحد حال في مجموع شيئين صارا باجتماعهما محلا واحدا له. والأول باطل بما قاله ، فإنه قاس العرض على الجسم الممتنع كونه في مكانين ولو ذلك الفيل يمتنع اجتماع عرضين في محل واحد قياسا على امتناع الجسمين في مكان واحد ، السكن اجتماع الأعراض الكثيرة في محل واحد كالسواد والحركة والتأليف والحياة بما لا يدفعه أحد ، والدليل على بطلانه أن المعرض محتاج في وجوده إلى المحل الذى هو فيه ولو أمكن حلوله في محلين ثبت استغناء كل واحد منهما عن الآخر فيكون محتاجا إلى كل واحد منهما ومستغنيا عنه معا وهو باطل . والثانى لم تقم حجة على امتناعه والفلاسفة يقولون بقيام العرض الواحد بمحل ينقسم إلى أجزاء كثيرة كالوحدة بالعشرة الواحدة ، والتثليث مجموع الأضلاع المثلثة المحيطة سطح، والحياة بنية متجزئة إلى أعضاء .
وأبو هاشم إنما قال بقيام تأليف واحد مجوهرين لأن عدم انفكاك المؤلف عنهما دون المتجاورين محتاج إلى علة ، ولو قام بكل واحد منهما ذلك للملة يتعذر انفكاكهما ، ولم يقل بقيامه بما فوق الاثنين ، الان التأليف لو قام مثلا بثلاثة جواهر ثم أزيل واحد منها من الاجتماع بالباقيين وجب انعدام التأليف لانعدام محله فلا يبقى الباقيان ، والفين وذلك بخلاف ما عليه الوجود .
وقال بعد إبطال قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين في جواب أبي هاشم : إن إحالة صعوبة التفكيك على الفاعل المختار بأن يلتصق أحدهما بالآخر أولى من النزام جوال حلول العرض في محلين .
Issue: They agreed that a single accident does not inhere in two loci except Abu Hashim, for he said: composition (ta'lif) is a single accident inhering in two loci, and he agreed with us that it is impossible for it to subsist in more than two loci. A group of ancient philosophers claimed that relation (idafa) is a single accident subsisting in two loci, like adjacency and proximity. Our proof: If it were rationally permissible for the inherer in this locus to be the same as the inherer in that locus, then it would be permissible for the obtained in this place to be the same as the obtained in that place, and thus a single body would be obtained in two places. And because he agreed on the impossibility of inherence in three, we demand the difference. And referring the difficulty of disengagement to the free agent is preferable to committing this absurdity. I say: From the concept of a single accident inhering in two loci, two meanings are understood: first, that the single accident inhering in a locus is the same as the one inhering in another; second, that the single accident inheres in the aggregate of two things that become one locus by their combination. The first is invalid by what he said, for he analogized the accident to the body, which cannot be in two places. And if that were the case, it would be impossible for two accidents to gather in a single locus, by analogy to the impossibility of two bodies in one place. But the gathering of many accidents in one locus, like blackness, motion, composition, and life, is undeniable. And the proof of its invalidity is that the accident is needful in its existence of the locus in which it is; if it could inhere in two loci, then it would be established that each is independent of the other, so it would be needful of each and independent of each simultaneously—that is false. As for the second, no proof has been established for its impossibility, and the philosophers say that a single accident can subsist in a locus that divides into many parts, like unity with the ten, and triangularity with the set of triangular sides enclosing a surface, and life with a structure divided into limbs. Abu Hashim only said that a single composition subsists in two substances because the non-disengagement of the composed from them, unlike the adjacent, needs a cause; if it subsisted in each of them, their disengagement would be impossible. And he did not say it subsists in more than two, because if composition existed in three substances and then one was removed from the assembly, composition would cease due to the cessation of its locus, and the remaining two would not remain, contrary to what is observed. And after refuting the subsistence of a single accident in two loci, in response to Abu Hashim, he said: referring the difficulty of disengagement to the free agent, by attaching one to the other, is better than committing to the inherence of an accident in two loci.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage deals with the question of whether a single accident can inhere in two distinct loci. Imam Razi states the consensus except for Abu Hashim (who considered composition as a single accident in two substances) and the philosophers (who considered relations like adjacency as such). The proof: if a single accident could be in two loci, then by analogy a single body could be in two places, which is absurd. Also, since Abu Hashim agrees that it cannot be in three, he must explain the difference. The author then explains two meanings of 'single accident in two loci' and rejects the first (the accident being exactly the same in each locus) because that would imply independence and dependence together, and also because many accidents can coexist in one locus, so the analogy with bodies fails. The second meaning (inhering in a composite locus) is not proven impossible; in fact, philosophers accept it (e.g., unity in ten things). Abu Hashim's view is explained: he only allows it for composition because the two substances become like one locus, and disengagement is impossible without a cause. The author concludes that referring the difficulty to God's action is better than accepting the absurdity of inherence in two loci.

Key Terms
العرض الواحد
المعنيان
الاستغناء
التركيب
الفاعل المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof against a single accident in two loci rejects all cases of one accident in multiple loci.
Correction: It only rejects the first meaning (same accident in separate loci); the second meaning (accident in a composite locus) is possible and accepted by philosophers.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what is the status of the second meaning of a single accident in two loci?
A) It is impossible.
B) It is possible and accepted by philosophers.
C) It is only possible for composition.
D) It is the same as the first meaning.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of a single accident in two loci only applies to the exact same accident in separate loci; an accident can inhere in a composite locus, as shown by examples from the philosophers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that an accident cannot be the same in two separate loci because it would be needful and independent of each simultaneously. Reflect: Are there areas in your life where you try to serve two masters—like seeking approval from both Allah and people—as if you could be dependent on both independently? How does this logical contradiction expose the impossibility of a divided heart? What will you do this week to unify your dependence solely on Allah?
?
The second meaning shows that a single accident can inhere in a composite whole, like life in all parts of the body. Consider your own body: every limb depends on the same life. But your soul's life—your iman—is it unified across all your actions, or do some parts of your life feel disconnected from faith? What composite locus of your daily affairs needs to be recollected under one divine purpose?
?
Imam Razi concludes that attributing the difficulty of disengagement to the free agent is better than accepting the absurdity of the first meaning. When you face difficulties in your life—when things seem stuck or disengaged—do you first assume a flawed understanding of how Allah works, or do you humbly refer the matter to His free will? How can you train yourself to see His wisdom in the complexity of your circumstances?

Principle of Possibility and Preponderator
Principle of possibility requiring preponderator

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational principle that when a possible thing has a preponderator for one side, that side becomes necessary, and the exception when both sides are equally balanced.

Core Idea
Possibility requires a preponderator to tip the scale.

Imam al-Razi establishes a foundational principle in kalam: for any contingent being, if one side is favored by a preponderator, that side must occur. But if both sides are equally related to the essence, no necessity arises. This sets the stage for discussions on causation and free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the conditional wording: 'when there is preponderance... except when equal.'
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) preponderator present for one side, (2) both sides equal in relation to essence. Compare the outcomes.
Only in case 1 does necessity occur.
3
Reflect on the exception
Ask yourself: In what situation is the possible not necessitated? When both sides are equal. This means possibility alone does not guarantee existence.
This distinction is crucial for later arguments about God's will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني، بل الممكن إذا حصل مع ترجيح أحد الطرفين وجب حصول ذلك الطرف وجودا كان أو عدما، إلا ما كان الطرفان متساويين في النسبة إلى ماهيته. وقوله: شرط الجوهر محتاج إلى انحصار الشرائط فيه، فإن الجوهر قابل للعرض فقط وربما يحتاج فاعله إلى وجود شرط آخر، فإن الشمس فاعلة لإضاءة وجه الأرض، وشرطه المحاذاة فإنها إن زالت صار وجه الأرض غير مضيء وإن كان القابل والفاعل موجودين، وباقي الكلام ظاهر.
Second: Rather, the possible, when there is preponderance of one of the two sides, that side must occur, whether existence or non-existence, except when the two sides are equal in relation to its essence. And his statement: The condition of the substance requires that the conditions be contained in it, for the substance is only receptive to accidents, and its agent may need the existence of another condition. For the sun is the agent of illumination of the earth's surface, and its condition is alignment; if it disappears, the earth's surface becomes dark even though the receptacle and the agent are present. And the rest of the speech is clear.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents a theological principle about possibility. It states that when a possible thing is accompanied by a preponderance of one of its two sides (existence or non-existence), that side must occur. The exception is when the two sides are equal with respect to the thing's essence—meaning no preponderance, so neither side is necessitated. Then it discusses the condition of a substance (jawhar). The condition of a substance is said to be limited to its receptivity to accidents; nothing else is required from the substance itself. However, the agent that brings the substance into existence may need an additional condition beyond the substance's receptivity. An example is given: the sun is the agent of illumination on the earth's surface, but the condition for illumination is alignment (the sun's rays striking the earth directly). If alignment disappears, the earth's surface becomes dark, even though the receptive body (the earth) and the agent (the sun) still exist. Thus, the agent requires external conditions, not just the inherent receptivity of the substance.

Key Terms
الممكن
الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think any possible thing must come into existence if it has a cause of any kind.
Correction: This passage clarifies that the cause must be a preponderator that tips the scale; if both sides are equal, no necessity arises.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when do both sides of a possible thing remain equally possible?
A) When a preponderator is present
B) When they are equal in relation to its essence
C) When the possible thing is a substance
D) When the agent is present

Key Takeaway
A possible thing only becomes necessary if one side is preponderated; without preponderance, both sides remain equally possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a major decision in your life that felt equally balanced. According to this principle, without a preponderator, neither side would tip. What or who served as the preponderator that pushed you to choose? Was it based on evidence, desire, or something else? How does this principle challenge you to identify the preponderators in your spiritual choices?
?
The passage says that if both sides are equal in relation to the essence, no determination occurs. Are there areas of your faith where you remain undecided or lukewarm—where you see both sides as equally possible? What preponderator are you missing that would tip you toward certainty or action? Will you seek it today?

Condition of a Substance: Receptivity and Agent's Conditions
Clarification of a substance's condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the condition of a substance (jawhar) is that it is receptive to accidents, while the agent may require additional conditions beyond the substance's receptivity.

Core Idea
A substance's condition is its receptivity; the agent may need extra conditions.

The text now addresses a specific objection: what is the condition of a substance? Al-Razi clarifies that a substance only requires the condition of being receptive to accidents; no further conditions are needed from the substance itself. However, the agent that acts upon the substance may require additional conditions, as illustrated by the sun and alignment example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation together. Note the distinction between the condition of the substance and the condition of the agent.
2
Identify the condition of the substance
The passage states: 'the substance is only receptive to accidents' – this is its sole condition. Nothing else is required from the substance itself.
3
Understand the agent's additional condition
The sun example shows that even if the substance (earth) and agent (sun) exist, a further condition (alignment) is needed for illumination. This demonstrates that the agent may require external conditions.
Thus, the condition of the substance does not encompass all conditions for action.

?

Are you willing to accept that some outcomes are beyond your control, and how does that change what you do next?
?

?
The sun and earth example demonstrates that existence alone is not sufficient for an effect. How does this reorient your understanding of Allah's power and your dependence on Him for even the simplest outcomes? Will you intentionally pause today to remind yourself that nothing happens without the fulfillment of conditions only He can orchestrate?

Razi's Proof of the Indivisible Moment and the Atom
Razi proves the indivisibility of the present moment of motion and the existence of the atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical proof that the present moment of motion must be indivisible and how this leads to the existence of the atom.

Core Idea
The present moment of motion must be indivisible; otherwise motion itself would be impossible.

Razi presents a logical proof that motion and space are composed of discrete indivisible units. He begins by analyzing the present moment of motion. If it were divisible, parts would have to be in temporal succession, contradicting the simultaneous existence required for the present. He then extends the reasoning to the distance traversed, concluding that there must be indivisible parts of space, which he identifies as the atom (al-jawhar). This passage is a classic argument for atomism in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read through the Arabic and English to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the use of conditional reasoning.
2
Identify the main contradiction
Find where Razi says 'this is a contradiction' (خلف) and understand why the divisibility leads to a logical impossibility.
3
Trace the extension to distance
Razi applies the same reasoning to the distance traversed. See how he argues that the part of distance must be indivisible, otherwise the motion would be divisible.
This part introduces the atom.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that motion is composed of indivisible moments and that the atom (جوهر) is the indivisible unit of distance.
This is a key proof for atomism in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في زمان حاضر، والمستقبل هو الذي يتوقع صيرورته كذلك، وما يمتنع حضوره لا يصير ماضيا ولا مستقبلا، ثم ذلك الحاضر إما منقسم وإلا لزم أن يكون بعض أجزائه قبل البعض، فعند حضور أحد الصفين لا يكون الصف الآخر موجودا، فلا يكون الموجود موجودا، هذا خلف. فإذا الجزء الحاضر من الحركة غير منقسم، وعند قيامه يحصل جزء آخر غير منقسم، فالحركة مركبة من أمور كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة. ثم نقول: القدر المقطوع من المسافة كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء التي لا تتجزى، إن كان منقسما كانت الحركة إلى نصفه نصف تلك الحركة، فتلك الحركة منقسمة، هذا خلف، وإن لم يكن منقسما فهو الجوهر (١).
In the present time, and the future is that which is expected to become such, and what is impossible to occur does not become past or future. Then that present is either divisible, otherwise it would necessitate that some of its parts be before others. So when one of the two rows is present, the other row does not exist, so the existent would not exist—this is a contradiction. Thus the present part of motion is indivisible, and upon its completion another indivisible part occurs. So motion is composed of things each of which is indivisible. Then we say: the determined extent of the distance is each one of those indivisible parts. If it were divisible, then motion to its half would be half of that motion, so that motion would be divisible—this is a contradiction. And if it is not divisible, then it is the atom (al-jawhar).
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of indivisible parts (atoms) in motion and distance. Razi starts by analyzing the present moment. He argues that if the present moment were divisible, one part would precede another, leading to a contradiction because two parts cannot coexist in the present. Therefore the present moment of motion is indivisible. Successive indivisible moments compose motion. Then he extends this to distance: the distance traversed in each indivisible moment is itself indivisible. If such a part of distance were divisible, then the motion over half of it would be half the original motion, making the motion divisible—a contradiction. Hence that part is indivisible, and that is the atom (جوهر فرد). Note: The argument relies on the premise that motion is composed of discrete moments, a view consistent with atomism.

Key Terms
الجوهر
الحركة
الحاضر
الماضي
المستقبل
الانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what contradiction arises if the present moment of motion were divisible?
A) Motion would not occur.
B) One part of the present would be absent while another is present, so the present would not fully exist.
C) The atom would be divisible.
D) The future would become past.

Key Takeaway
Razi uses a proof by contradiction to establish that the present moment of motion and the units of distance are indivisible, forming the basis for atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that the present moment must be indivisible, otherwise it would not fully exist. Think of a moment in your life that felt fully present—where your entire being was absorbed in that instant. Was that moment truly undivided? How does your awareness of time's indivisibility change the way you experience the present? Will you now strive to make each moment a complete act of worship and presence before Allah?
?
If motion is composed of indivisible parts, then every action you take is a series of distinct, atomic moments. Which of your habits or sins are made up of repeated small choices? Can you identify the indivisible moment of decision where you either obey or disobey? Will you guard that moment more carefully?
?
Razi's proof shows that the atom is necessary for motion and existence. Consider how your own existence is built from moments granted by Allah each instant. Are you living as if each moment is a fresh creation from Him, or do you take them for granted? What would change if you truly believed that your next breath is an atom of His mercy?

The Present is Not a Time: Critique of Motion in the Present Moment
A commentator argues that the present is not a time and thus cannot contain motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the present moment cannot contain motion because it is a boundary, not a duration.

Core Idea
The present is a boundary between past and future, not a time.

In this footnote, the commentator responds to an argument that motion exists only in past and future. He clarifies that the present is not a time, but a dividing line. Since motion requires temporal extension, the indivisible present cannot be a locus for motion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Read the first line: the opponent says motion has no existence except in past or future. Note that this sets up the debate.
The opponent is likely a philosopher or theologian who denies motion in the present.
2
Understand the definition of the present
The commentator defines the present (al-ḥāl) as the end of the past and beginning of the future, not a time (zamān). This is crucial.
In Arabic, 'ḥāl' can mean state or moment; here it is the present instant.
3
Recognize the reasoning about motion and time
The commentator argues: since motion requires time, and the present is not time, motion cannot be in the present.
This is a syllogism: every motion is in time; the present is not time; therefore no motion in present.
4
Examine the commentator's conclusion
The commentator concludes that the present is not motion, and addresses a finer point about common sections and the opponent's alternative view of motion as partly past and future.
The second half about 'common sections' is less clear due to OCR issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول: مخالفه يقول الحركة لا وجود لها إلا في الماضي أو في المستقبل، وأما الحال فهو نهاية الماضي وبداية المستقبل وليس بزمان وما ليس بزمان لا يكون فيه حركة لأن كل حركة في زمان، وكذلك سير الفصول المشتركة للقادران جزء ليست بأجزاء لها إذ لو كانت الفصول المشتركة أجزاء المقادير التي هي فصولها لكانت القسمة إلى قسمين إلى ثلاثة أقسام قسمة إلى خمسة أقسام هذا خلف، فإذا الحاضر ليس بحركة، وهو أدهى أنه هو الحركة وبنى عليه بيانه، والمخالف لا يسلم أن الماضي من الحركة كان موجوداً إلى أن صار حاضراً إنما يقول هو الذي كان بعضه بالقياس إلى أن الحال مستقبلا وقبل بعضه ماضيا وصار في الحال كله ماضي او هكذا في المستقبل وفي الآن الفاصل بين الماضي والمستقبل لا يمكن أن يتحرك فإن الحركة إنما تقع في زمان وليس شيء من الزمان محاضر لأنه غير قار الذات.
I say: An opponent says that motion has no existence except in the past or in the future. As for the present, it is the end of the past and the beginning of the future, and it is not a time, and what is not a time cannot contain motion because every motion is in time. Likewise, the traversal of common sections for [؟] is not parts of them, because if the common sections were parts of the quantities that are their sections, then division into two, three, five would be a contradiction. Therefore the present is not motion, and it is more [؟] that it is motion upon which he built his proof. The opponent does not concede that the past of motion existed until it became present; he rather says that it was something that was partly, in relation to the present, future, and partly past, and in the present it all becomes past, and similarly in the future. And in the instant (al-ān) separating past and future, motion cannot occur, because motion only takes place in time, and nothing of time is present because it is not a fixed essence.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This footnote is a critical response to Razi's argument about the present moment. The commentator (perhaps a later scholar) presents an opponent's view: motion exists only in past and future, not in the present because the present is a boundary, not a duration. Since motion requires time, the indivisible present cannot contain motion. The commentator also addresses a potential confusion about common sections and argues that the opponent does not accept Razi's premise that the past of motion was existent until it became present. Thus, the present is not a moment of motion but a boundary.

Key Terms
الحال
الزمان
الآن
الحركة
الفصل المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the present is a time (duration) that can contain events like motion.
Correction: The footnote clarifies that the present is a boundary, not a duration, and thus cannot contain motion which requires temporal extension.

Checkpoint
According to the footnote, why cannot motion occur in the present?
A) Because the present is divisible.
B) Because the present is not a time.
C) Because motion requires past and future only.
D) Because the present is a fixed point.

Key Takeaway
The present is a boundary, not a duration, so motion cannot take place in it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that the present is not a time but a boundary between past and future. If the present has no duration, then when do you actually perform your actions? Reflect on the fact that your entire life is a succession of boundaries, none of which you can grasp. Does this change how you view the urgency of obedience to Allah? What can you do today to act as if this moment is your only chance?
?
The source states that motion cannot occur in the present because every motion is in time. Think about your own daily movements—walking, talking, even thinking. Are you ever truly 'in the present'? Or are you always recollecting the past or anticipating the future? How might this knowledge help you to live each instant in full awareness of Allah's presence? Commit to a specific dhikr or pause that brings your attention to the present moment as a gift from Allah.

The Impossibility of Infinite Actual Parts
Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum against infinite actual parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a body cannot be composed of infinite actual parts and how the objection of potential divisibility is addressed.

Core Idea
If a body had infinite actual parts, crossing it would take infinite time—an absurdity.

Razi begins his refutation by presenting a classic Zeno-style argument: if a body contains infinitely many actual parts, motion across the body would require traversing an infinite number of halves, which is impossible. He then anticipates and dismisses the objection that these parts are only potential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement followed by an objection.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the logical structure
Follow the conditional: if infinite parts (premise), then infinite halves, then infinite time, then contradiction. The conclusion: the premise is false.
3
Examine the objection
Razi anticipates the objection that the parts are only potential, not actual. He promises to refute this later.
This objection sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرد الثالث : لو تركب الجسم من أجزاء غير متناهية لامتنع الوصول من أوله إلى آخره بالحركة إلا بعد الوصول إلى نصفه ولامتنع الوصول إلى نصفه إلا بعد الوصول إلى ربعه، فإذا كانت المفاصل غير متناهية وجب أن لا يصل المتحرك إلى آخر المسافة إلا في زمان غير متناه وفساد اللازم فساد الملزوم. لا يقال: هذا إنما يلزم على من يقول الأجزاء التي لا نهاية لها حاصلة بالفعل، ونحن لا نقول به بل الجسم عندنا واحد قابل لانقسامات غير متناهية. لانا نقول القول بوحدة ما يقبل القسمة باطل لوجوه. أحدها: أن وحدته إن كانت نفس الذات أو من لوازمها امتنعت إزالتها إلا عند عدم الذات وإن كان من العوارض الزائلة فهو محال، لأن القائم بما يقبل الانقسام قابل للانقسام فالوحدة في نفسها قابلة للانقسام فإن قامت بها وحدة أخرى لزم التسلسل وإن لم تقم بها وحدة أخرى كانت تلك الوحدة منقسمة بالفعل فالموصوف بها كذلك، فالجسم منقسم بالفعل. وثانيهما: أنا إذا جعلنا الماء الواحد مائين فالماءان الحاصلان إن قلنا إنهما كانا موجودين قبل ذلك فمن المعلوم بالضرورة أن أحدهما ما كان عين الذات فكان مغايرا له فالجزءان كانا موجودين بالفعل وإن قلنا إنهما ما كانا موجودين قبل ذلك كان ذلك إحداثا لهذين المائين وإعداما للماء الأول وهو باطل بالبديهة. وثالثها: أن كل مره يمكن فرضه في الجسم فهو موصوف بخاصية غير حاصلة في الجزء الآخر، لأن مقطع موصوف بالنصفية ولا يتصف بها إلا مورد القسمة وكذا مقطع الثلث والربع، وإذا كان لكل واحد من المقاطع الممكنة خاصة بالفعل وعندهم أن الاختصاص بالخواص المختلفة يوجب حصول الانقسام بالفعل.
The third individual: If the body were composed of infinite parts, then reaching its end by motion would be impossible except after reaching its half, and reaching its half would be impossible except after reaching its quarter. So if the articulations are infinite, then the mover would not reach the end of the distance except in infinite time—and the corruption of the necessary implies the corruption of that which necessitates. It should not be said: This only applies to those who hold that infinite parts are actually existent, and we do not say that; rather, for us the body is a unity capable of infinite divisions. For we say: The statement that what is capable of division is a unity is false for reasons. First: If its unity is the essence itself or its necessary concomitant, its removal would be impossible except upon non-existence of the essence; and if it is among transient accidents, it is impossible because that which subsists in what is divisible is itself divisible. So unity itself is divisible: if another unity subsists in it, infinite regress follows; if not, that unity is actually divided, and thus the described is divided, so the body is actually divided. Second: If we make one water into two waters, then the two resulting waters: if we say they existed before, then it is known necessarily that one of them was not identical to the essence, so it was other than it, so the two parts existed actually; and if we say they did not exist before, then that would be a creation of these two waters and an annihilation of the first water, which is self-evidently false. Third: Every point that can be assumed in the body is characterized by a property not present in another part, because the halfway point is characterized by half-ness and is not so characterized except at the place of division, similarly the quarter and third points. And if each of the possible points has an actual property, and according to them the specification of different properties necessitates the occurrence of actual division.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that a body is a unity with infinite potential divisions, and a proof that it is actually divided. Razi first presents a proof: if a body had infinite actual parts, crossing it would take infinite time (since one must traverse infinitely many halves), which is absurd; therefore the body cannot have infinite parts. He then addresses an objection that infinite parts are not actual but potential. His answer contains three arguments. First: If the body's unity is essential, it cannot be removed; but we see bodies divide, so the unity must be an accidental property. However, an accidental property subsisting in a divisible thing is itself divisible, leading to either infinite regress or actual division. Second: When water divides, the two parts either existed before or not. If they existed, then the whole was already multiple; if not, then division creates new substances, which is absurd. Third: Points like the midpoint have distinct properties (e.g., half-ness) that are not shared, implying actual division.

Key Terms
أجزاء غير متناهية
المفاصل
زمان غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Potential divisibility avoids the absurdity of infinite parts.
Correction: Razi will show that even potential divisibility implies actual division.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude about a body with infinite actual parts?
A) It would take finite time to cross.
B) It would take infinite time to cross, which is absurd.
C) It is not composed of parts at all.
D) It is a unity with potential parts.

Key Takeaway
A body cannot have infinite actual parts because motion across it would require infinite time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's argument shows that assuming infinite parts makes actual motion impossible. Reflect on your own spiritual journey: Are you assuming you have 'infinite time' to repent and draw closer to Allah? What finite, concrete step will you take today to move towards Him, knowing that your time is limited?
?
If every physical action requires finite steps, what does this teach you about the urgency of your deeds? Is there an area in your life where you are delaying obedience, treating it as if you have endless chances? Commit to one specific act of worship before the day ends.
?
The body is a unity capable of division but not actually divided. How does this truth affect your understanding of your own existence? You are a single soul with finite responsibilities—not an infinite collection of tomorrows. What one thing will you complete today that you have been putting off, knowing that the path requires actual steps now?

Three Proofs for Actual Division in a Body
Razi enumerates three arguments for actual division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments Razi provides to prove that a body is actually divided into parts.

Core Idea
Unity in a divisible body is impossible; the body must be actually divided.

Having shown that infinite actual parts are impossible, Razi now proves that the body cannot even be a true unity. He presents three independent proofs: from the nature of unity, from the transformation of water, and from the distinct properties of points.

Guided Path
1
Read the three proofs
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice each proof begins with a number (أحدها, وثانيهما, وثالثها).
These are three separate arguments for the same conclusion.
2
Analyze the first proof
Understand the dilemma: unity is either essential or accidental. If essential, it cannot be removed; if accidental, it is divisible. Either way, the body must be actually divided.
This is a logical disjunction.
3
Analyze the second proof
Consider the example of water dividing into two. The two parts either existed before or not. Both lead to actual parts.
This argument uses common experience.
4
Analyze the third proof
Recognize that points like the midpoint have unique properties (e.g., half-ness) that are not shared, implying actual division at those points.
Different properties require distinct parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third individual: If the body were composed of infinite parts, then reaching its end by motion would be impossible except after reaching its half, and reaching its half would be impossible except after reaching its quarter. So if the articulations are infinite, then the mover would not reach the end of the distance except in infinite time—and the corruption of the necessary implies the corruption of that which necessitates. It should not be said: This only applies to those who hold that infinite parts are actually existent, and we do not say that; rather, for us the body is a unity capable of infinite divisions. For we say: The statement that what is capable of division is a unity is false for reasons. First: If its unity is the essence itself or its necessary concomitant, its removal would be impossible except upon non-existence of the essence; and if it is among transient accidents, it is impossible because that which subsists in what is divisible is itself divisible. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In the second proof, Razi says that if the two waters did not exist before, their creation from the first water would be absurd. When you change a habit or a belief, do you assume the new you was already there, or do you create yourself from scratch? Which assumption is more honest about your own experience? And what does Razi’s reasoning say about the necessity of actual parts in your own character?
?

Is that unity essential or accidental? If it were accidental, what would it mean for it to be divided? Can you identify a time when it became divided, and how did you (or did you) restore it?

Atomism Arguments and Razi's Answer
Proponents present three arguments for indivisible parts; Razi responds by distinguishing direction from division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments put forward for atomism and how Razi refutes them by distinguishing between directional aspects and substantial division.

Core Idea
Directional differences do not prove substantial division.

In this passage, opponents of Razi present three arguments intended to prove that matter is composed of indivisible parts—atoms. Each argument tries to show that any extended thing must be divisible. Razi's response is clever: he accepts that parts have different directional faces but denies that this implies actual division in the substance, using the analogy of a circle's center.

Guided Path
1
Read the arguments carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the three objections. Note that each argument uses a different scenario: the first involves right/left faces, the second stacked surfaces, the third moving parts.
Try to see the pattern: all infer division from distinct aspects.
2
Identify the common flaw
Notice that all three arguments infer division from having different parts (faces, visibility, contact points). Ask yourself: do these necessarily imply physical separation?
3
Understand Razi's counter
Razi says these only show different directions (جهات), not division in essence. The circle center example illustrates that an indivisible point can relate to multiple directions.
The center of a circle faces all circumferences yet remains one point.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does this distinction matter for theology? It defends the possibility of indivisible entities, important for concepts like the soul or divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا أحدها : أن كل متحيز يفرض فإن الوجه الذي منه يلاقي ما على عينه غير الذي منه يلاقي ما على يساره فيكون منقسما. وثانيها: أنا إذا ركبنا سطحا فوق آخر لا يتجزى ثم نظرنا رأينا أحد وجهيه دون الثاني والوجه المرئي غير الذي ليس بمرئي فيكون منقسما. وثالثها: أنا لو ركبنا خطا من ستة أجزاء ووضعنا فوق طرفه الأيمن جزءا وتحت طرفه الأيسر جزءا ثم تحركا إلى أن يصل كل واحد منهما إلى آخر المسافة فلابد أن يمر كل واحد منهما بالآخر ولا يمكن ذلك إلا بعد أن يتحاذيا وموضع التحاذي متصل الثالث والرابع، وإذا وقع الجزء على ذلك الموضع فقد ماس لكل واحد من نصفيه نصف كل واحد منهما فيلزم التجزئة. الجواب أن ما ذكرتموه يدل على تغاير جهات الجزء وذلك لا يوجب القسمة في الذات، فإن مركز الدائرة يحاذي جملة أجزاء الدائرة مع أن المركز نقطة غير منقسمة (٢).
They argued: First: Every extended thing that is assumed has a face by which it meets what is on its right different from the face by which it meets what is on its left, so it is divisible. Second: If we place an indivisible surface over another then look, we see one of its two faces without the second, and the visible face is other than the non-visible one, so it is divisible. Third: If we compose a line of six parts and place a part above its right end and a part below its left end, then they move until each reaches the end of the distance, each must pass the other, and that is only possible after they become aligned, and the place of alignment is connected to the third and fourth. When the part falls on that place, it contacts with each of its halves half of each of the other, so division follows. The answer: What you have mentioned indicates difference of directions of the part, but that does not necessitate division in the essence, for the center of a circle aligns with all parts of the circle while the center is an indivisible point.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents three objections from opponents who argue for indivisible atoms, followed by Razi's response. The first argument: any extended body has different faces for different directions, so it must be divisible. The second: if two indivisible surfaces are stacked, we see only one face, implying division. The third: a line of six parts with two parts moving past each other requires contact at half-points, forcing division. Razi's answer: these arguments only prove different directional aspects (جهات), not division in the substance itself. He gives the example of a circle's center: it aligns with all circumferences yet remains indivisible. Thus, the objections are invalid.

Key Terms
المتحيز
الجهات
المركز
النقطة
التماس
التجزئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Having different directional aspects or relations means the thing must be physically divided.
Correction: Razi clarifies that directionality (جهات) does not entail division in the essence, as shown by the indivisible circle center that relates to all parts.

Checkpoint
What is the core of Razi's response to the three arguments?
A) The arguments prove atoms do not exist.
B) The arguments confuse direction with division in the essence.
C) The arguments are valid only for surfaces, not points.
D) The arguments require infinite time to work.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that having different directions does not imply division in essence; thus the arguments for atomism fail.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know you face multiple demands in life—from family, work, and faith. Do you ever feel torn, as if these demands split your soul into pieces? How does Razi's lesson that multiple directions do not divide the essence challenge you to see yourself as a unified whole even when pulled in different ways? What concrete step will you take this week to act from your core rather than from fragmented reactions?
?
Consider a time when you assumed that because you felt two different emotions (like love and anger) toward the same person, your heart must be divided. After reading that a single point can align with an entire circle, are you ready to see your heart as capable of holding seeming opposites without being broken? What relationship in your life needs this reframing most urgently?
?
The opponents thought that multiple contacts proved division. In your own life, when you interact with many people or hold many duties, do you feel you lose yourself? Razi's answer points to an indivisible center. What is the 'center' of your existence—your deepest identity that remains unchanged regardless of how many directions you face? How will you protect and return to that center today?

Time Divides Like Distance: Infinite Divisibility
Analogy between distance and time divisibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that time is divisible into parts without limit, just like distance, and that this division is only accidental.

Core Idea
Time and distance share the same infinite accidental divisibility.

The commentator establishes a crucial analogy: just as distance can be divided indefinitely, so too the time required to traverse it. This principle underpins the subsequent critique of Razi's proofs, which assume actual division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the commentator's point about time and distance being alike in divisibility.
Notice the term 'accidentally' (bil-‘araḍ) – division is potential, not actual.
2
Understand the analogy
The commentator compares distance dividing without an ultimate part to time of motion dividing similarly. The 'king traversing distance' illustrates this.
This is a key premise for the refutations later.
3
Reflect on accidental division
Ask yourself: why does the commentator emphasize 'accidental' division? It distinguishes potential divisibility from actual atomic division.
This aligns with the Ash'ari view that bodies are not actually divided into atoms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول: كما أن المسافة تنقسم إلى أجزاء لا إلى حد يقف عندها كذلك زمان الحركة، والمفاصل غير متناهية إلا بالعرض كذلك الزمان الذي تقطع فيه الملك المسافة يكون في العرض قابلا لأجزاء كأجزاء المسافة بعينها، فإن كانت المسافة ذات مفاصل غير متناهية كان زمان قطعها مثلها. قوله في إبطال وحدة ما يقبل القسمة أن القائم بما يقبل الانقسام قابل للانقسام باطل لم لمر؟ وقيام الوحدة بالوحدة ممكن في النقل. وفي الوجه الثاني ادعاء الضرورة بأن أحد المائين الموجودين قبل القسمة ليس هو غير الثاني مشتمل على دهوى في القسمة مع فرضها ولذلك لزم المحال. ولا يلزم من كونهما غير موجودين قبل القسمة عدم شيء بعد القسمة غير الاتصال وحدوث شيء غير الاتصال وذلك محسوس فضلا عن أن يكون باطلا بالبديهة. وفي الوجه الثالث أن الأجزاء المفروضة تستقبح الخواص ليس بشيء لأن تغاير الخواص اللازمة من الغرض لا يقتضي الانقسام الموجود بالفعل مع عدم الغرض.
I say: Just as distance divides into parts without a limit at which it stops, so too the time of motion; the articulations are infinite only accidentally, similarly the time in which the king traverses the distance is accidentally divisible into parts like the parts of the distance itself. If the distance has infinite articulations, then the time of its traversal would be like them. His statement in refuting the unity of what is divisible: that what subsists in what is divisible is itself divisible – this is false because [!] and the subsistence of unity in unity is possible in transfer. In the second proof, the claim of necessity that one of the two waters existing before division is not other than the second involves a claim about division with its assumption, and therefore the absurdity follows. And it does not follow from their non-existence before division that after division nothing is lost except continuity and that something other than continuity occurs; that is perceptible, let alone being self-evidently false. In the third proof, that the supposed parts entail properties is nothing, because the difference of properties consequent upon the intent does not necessitate actual division in the absence of the intent.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This footnote critiques Razi's three proofs for actual division. The commentator first establishes that time, like distance, is infinitely divisible accidentally. Then he attacks each proof. Against the first proof (unity of divisible is not possible), he says it is false because unity can subsist in unity (e.g., one accident in another). Against the second (the two waters), he argues that the claim that one water is not the other before division is a petitio principii, and that the emergence of two waters after division does not involve annihilation of the first water but only loss of continuity—which is perceptible, not absurd. Against the third (different properties of points), he says that different properties do not entail actual division unless there is a purpose for division, but here there is none.

Key Terms
المسافة
الزمان
الاتصال
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think time is indivisible or composed of indivisible instants.
Correction: The commentator affirms that time is divisible without limit, just like distance, though only accidentally.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, how is the division of time characterized?
A) Actual division into finite parts
B) Accidental division without limit
C) Essential division into atoms
D) No division at all

Key Takeaway
Time and distance share the property of infinite accidental divisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that time, like distance, is only accidentally divisible—it has no real separate parts in itself. Does this change how you view the continuity of your life under Allah's gaze? Are you tempted to break your life into separate chapters (good and bad, obedient and sinful), forgetting that it is all one continuous journey for which you will be held accountable? How can you start living today as if every moment is inseparably linked?
?
The passage uses the example of a king crossing a distance. Imagine that king is you, and the distance is your life span. Do you see the 'parts' of your life as mere accidents, or do you treat some parts as more real than others? If you truly believed that every moment is infinitely divisible in potential, would you be more careful with how you spend even the smallest unit of your time?
?
The commentator insists that the division of time is only accidental, not actual. This means that, in reality, your past sins are not separate 'packets' that you can isolate and forget; they are part of a continuous whole. How does this perspective affect your approach to seeking forgiveness and changing your behavior? Are you willing to see your entire life as one unbroken thread that must be rewoven in repentance?

Refutation of Razi's Proofs for Actual Division
Refutation of three proofs for actual division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's refutations of three proofs that aim to show actual division in bodies.

Core Idea
Razi's proofs for actual division rely on flawed assumptions or circular reasoning.

After establishing that time and distance are only accidentally divisible, the commentator systematically dismantles Razi's three arguments for actual division. Each refutation exposes a logical flaw, from begging the question to misinterpreting what happens when continuity is lost.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first proof's refutation
The commentator rejects the claim that what subsists in divisible must be divisible. Unity can subsist in unity, e.g., one accident in another.
The phrase 'لم لمر' likely means 'not an issue' or similar.
2
Analyze the second proof's refutation
The claim that two waters are distinct before division begs the question. After division, only continuity is lost, not the water's existence.
The commentator says this is perceptible, not absurd.
3
Consider the third proof's refutation
Different properties of points do not force actual division unless there is a purpose (al-gharaḍ). In absence of intent, no division is necessitated.
The word 'تستقبح' may be a misreading of 'تستتبع' (entail).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول: كما أن المسافة تنقسم إلى أجزاء لا إلى حد يقف عندها كذلك زمان الحركة، والمفاصل غير متناهية إلا بالعرض كذلك الزمان الذي تقطع فيه الملك المسافة يكون في العرض قابلا لأجزاء كأجزاء المسافة بعينها، فإن كانت المسافة ذات مفاصل غير متناهية كان زمان قطعها مثلها. قوله في إبطال وحدة ما يقبل القسمة أن القائم بما يقبل الانقسام قابل للانقسام باطل لم لمر؟ وقيام الوحدة بالوحدة ممكن في النقل. وفي الوجه الثاني ادعاء الضرورة بأن أحد المائين الموجودين قبل القسمة ليس هو غير الثاني مشتمل على دهوى في القسمة مع فرضها ولذلك لزم المحال. ولا يلزم من كونهما غير موجودين قبل القسمة عدم شيء بعد القسمة غير الاتصال وحدوث شيء غير الاتصال وذلك محسوس فضلا عن أن يكون باطلا بالبديهة. وفي الوجه الثالث أن الأجزاء المفروضة تستقبح الخواص ليس بشيء لأن تغاير الخواص اللازمة من الغرض لا يقتضي الانقسام الموجود بالفعل مع عدم الغرض.
I say: Just as distance divides into parts without a limit at which it stops, so too the time of motion; the articulations are infinite only accidentally, similarly the time in which the king traverses the distance is accidentally divisible into parts like the parts of the distance itself. If the distance has infinite articulations, then the time of its traversal would be like them. His statement in refuting the unity of what is divisible: that what subsists in what is divisible is itself divisible – this is false because [!] and the subsistence of unity in unity is possible in transfer. In the second proof, the claim of necessity that one of the two waters existing before division is not other than the second involves a claim about division with its assumption, and therefore the absurdity follows. And it does not follow from their non-existence before division that after division nothing is lost except continuity and that something other than continuity occurs; that is perceptible, let alone being self-evidently false. In the third proof, that the supposed parts entail properties is nothing, because the difference of properties consequent upon the intent does not necessitate actual division in the absence of the intent.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This footnote critiques Razi's three proofs for actual division. The commentator first establishes that time, like distance, is infinitely divisible accidentally. Then he attacks each proof. Against the first proof (unity of divisible is not possible), he says it is false because unity can subsist in unity (e.g., one accident in another). Against the second (the two waters), he argues that the claim that one water is not the other before division is a petitio principii, and that the emergence of two waters after division does not involve annihilation of the first water but only loss of continuity—which is perceptible, not absurd. Against the third (different properties of points), he says that different properties do not entail actual division unless there is a purpose for division, but here there is none.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the emergence of two waters after division proves they were actually distinct before division.
Correction: The commentator says they were not distinct before; only continuity is lost, and the new state is simply a result of division assumed.

Checkpoint
In the second proof, what does the commentator say is assumed incorrectly?
A) That the two waters are distinct before division
B) That division is possible
C) That continuity is lost after division
D) That the waters are the same after division

Key Takeaway
Razi's proofs for actual division all falter due to flawed premises or circular reasoning; only accidental divisibility stands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The commentator shows that even a brilliant thinker like Razi can fall into this trap. What assumptions in your own faith or worldview might you be holding without scrutiny? Will you honestly check them today?
?
The commentator argues that differing properties do not necessitate actual division unless there is an intent. In your relationships, do you often assume that differences in opinion or behavior indicate a fundamental separation? Could it be that you have failed to see the unifying intent behind those differences? Are you willing to seek that intent rather than assume division?
?

When did you last feel the limits of your own reasoning? How does that realization drive you to rely more on divine guidance than on your own conclusions?

Critique of Directions and the Center Example
Commentator critiques Razi's response to atomism by analyzing directions and the center example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's logical critique of Razi's response to atomism, focusing on the nature of directions and the failure of the center-of-the-circle analogy.

Core Idea
Directions cannot be both non-existent and cause division.

This footnote is a critical examination of Razi's earlier response to atomist arguments. Razi had denied the reality of surfaces and points, but his response here seems to rely on them. The commentator systematically tests the nature of directions—are they non-existent, substances, or accidents?—and shows each option leads to a problem. He also challenges the center-of-the-circle analogy, arguing it does not prove uniformity. Follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and the English translation. Notice the dense, argumentative style. The commentator is responding to a previous claim by Razi.
The passage uses technical terms like 'جهات' (directions) and 'تماس' (contact).
2
Analyze the four possibilities for directions
The commentator lists four possible natures for differing directions: (1) non-existent (عدمية), (2) existent substances, (3) accidents subsisting in other substances, (4) accidents subsisting in the atoms themselves. For each, he shows a contradiction or problem.
Focus on why each option fails to support Razi's position.
3
Understand the center example's failure
The commentator argues that the center of a circle aligns uniformly with all parts, but contacts in atomism are multiple and distinct, so the analogy does not apply.
This step shows the commentator's careful distinction between uniform alignment and multiple distinct contacts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢) أقول: إنما حكم فيما مضى بنفي السطوح والنقعة وأجاب ههنا بما هو مبنى على ثبوتها وعلى تغاير الجهات، ولقائل أن يقول: الجهات المتغايرة إن كانت عدمية فلا تمايز بينها على قولك، وإن كانت وجودية وكانت جواهر عاد الكلام فها كما كان في الأول، وإن كانت أعراضا وكانت حالة في غير تلك الجواهر لم تكن مقتضية لتغاير التماس فيها، وإن كانت حالة فيها أوجب تغايرها انقسام الجواهر لتميزها. وكون المركز محاذيا لجملة أجزاء الدائرة لا يفيده في هذا الموضع السكون ما يتعلق به تلك المحاذيات المنكرة واحدا، وكون ما يتعلق به التماسات غير واحد، فإن تماس ما يماسه من جهة لا تقع على موضع تماس ما يماسه من جهة أخرى، ولذلك يلزم التغاير ههنا ولم يلزم في المركز.
I say: He previously ruled by denying surfaces and points, and here he answers based on their affirmation and on the difference of directions. And someone may say: The differing directions: if they are privative, then there is no distinction among them according to your statement; if they are existent and are substances, then the argument returns the same as before; if they are accidents and are subsisting in other than those substances, they do not necessitate difference of contact in them; and if they subsist in them, their difference necessitates division of the substances due to their distinction. And that the center aligns with all parts of the circle does not benefit here the stillness [؟] related to those unrecognized alignments as one, and that what relates to contacts is not one, for the contact of what it contacts from one direction does not fall on the place of contact from another direction, and therefore the difference is necessitated here and not in the center.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This footnote critiques Razi's response to the atomists. Razi said that the three arguments only prove difference of directions, not division. The commentator points out a contradiction: earlier Razi denied the existence of surfaces and points (presumably he rejected atomism in some forms), but here he uses the example of a center point (point) and surfaces (the circle parts). Then he analyzes directions: if directions are non-existent (عدم), they are not distinct; if existent and are substances, the same problem recurs; if they are accidents subsisting in non-substances, they do not necessitate different contacts; but if they subsist in the atoms, then their difference necessitates division. Regarding the center example, he argues that the center aligns with all parts uniformly, but the contacts in the atomists' argument are multiple and distinct, so the analogy fails.

Key Terms
الجهات
التماس
الجواهر
الأعراض
المركز
النقعة
السطوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the center-of-the-circle analogy proves that multiple directions can coexist without division.
Correction: The commentator shows that the center aligns uniformly with all parts, so it does not involve multiple distinct contacts. In atomism, contacts from different directions are distinct and non-overlapping, which is unlike the center.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what happens if differing directions are accidents subsisting in the atoms themselves?
A) They do not necessitate difference of contact.
B) Their difference necessitates division of the substances.
C) The argument returns the same as before.
D) There is no distinction among them.

Key Takeaway
The commentator systematically dismantles Razi's response by showing that directions, whether non-existent or existent, cannot explain distinct contacts without implying division, and the center analogy is inapplicable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a position, do you examine all logical possibilities for the underlying assumptions, or do you cling to what seems convenient? How can the commentator's method of exhausting options inspire you to be more rigorous in your own beliefs?
?
Consider a time you used an analogy to defend an argument. Did you check whether the analogy truly applies, or did you ignore key differences? The commentator shows that the center analogy fails because contacts are not uniform. Is there an area of your faith or practice where you rely on weak comparisons? How will you seek a more solid understanding?
?
The commentator points out Razi's inconsistency: denying surfaces and points but then using them. Are there inconsistencies in your own life where you claim one thing but act on another? How can the pursuit of intellectual honesty bring you closer to Allah's truth?

Impossibility as Privation: A Dialectical Refutation
Author presents an objection on motion's impossibility and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that the impossibility of motion must be necessary to the essence by showing that impossibility is a privation and that the objection backfires on the opponent.

Core Idea
Impossibility is not a positive attribute; it is non-existence, thus cannot be caused.

In this passage, the author engages with an opponent's objection concerning the impossibility of motion. The opponent argues that such impossibility must either be essential or accidental. The author skillfully turns the argument by first noting that impossibility is a privation, then showing that even if conceded, the objection applies equally to the opponent's own position. This is a classic example of dialectical reasoning in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent claims the impossibility of motion must be either necessary to the essence or not. Recognize the dilemma and what is at stake.
This is a standard dialectical move: presenting a dichotomy to force a conclusion.
2
Examine the author's first response
The author denies that impossibility can be caused because it is a privation (non-existence). Grasp the concept of 'adam as a lack of being, not a positive quality.
In kalam, privations do not require causes; only existents have efficient causes.
3
See the argument backfire
Even if we concede that impossibility is caused, the author shows that the opponent's own view on the world's impossibility of being eternal faces the same dilemma, leading to a contradiction.
The opponent's position is that the world is not eternal, so it is impossible for it to be eternal. This impossibility, if necessary, would make the world eternally impossible; if not necessary, then eternity is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله امتناع الحركة إما أن يكون لازما للماهية أو لا يكون قلنا الامتناع عدم فلا يعلل، سلمنا كونه معللا لكنه لازم ووارد عليكم أيضا فإن العالم يمتنع أن يكون أزليا فهذا الامتناع إن كان لازما للماهية وجب أن يبقى ممتنعا أبدا وإن لم يكن لازما كان هذا اعترافا بحراز كون العالم أزليا وذاك يبطل قولكم .
His statement: The impossibility of motion either is necessary for the essence or not. We said: Impossibility is non-existence, so it is not caused. We concede that it is caused, but it is necessary and also applies to you, for the world is impossible to be eternal. So this impossibility: if it is necessary for the essence, then it must remain impossible forever; and if it is not necessary, then this is an admission of [the possibility of] the world being eternal, and that invalidates your statement.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair. The opponent argues that the impossibility of motion (i.e., that the world could have been at rest eternally) must either be necessary to the essence of the world or not. The author responds by first denying that impossibility can be caused, since it is a privation. Then, even if conceded that it is caused, he shows it would be necessary; but then it would apply to the opponent's own view regarding the world's impossibility of being eternal. If that impossibility were necessary, the world would always remain impossible, contradicting the opponent's claim of its actual existence. If not necessary, then it is possible for the world to be eternal, which the opponent denies. Thus the objection backfires.

Key Terms
امتناع
لازم للماهية
عدم
أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Impossibility is a positive attribute that requires a cause.
Correction: Impossibility is a privation (عدم), which has no need for a cause.

Checkpoint
What is the author's first response to the claim that the impossibility of motion could be necessary for the essence?
A) The impossibility is a caused accident.
B) The impossibility is a non-existence, so it cannot be caused.
C) The impossibility is eternal.
D) The impossibility is a necessary attribute of the world.

Key Takeaway
Impossibility is a privation, not a positive attribute, so it cannot be caused, and attempting to use it as a necessary quality of essence leads to contradictions when applied to the opponent's own views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label something in your life as 'impossible,' do you treat it as a fixed, essential part of your situation (like a necessary attribute) or as a temporary privation that could be removed? What would change if you saw it as non-existence instead of a positive barrier?
?
The opponent's argument collapsed because it contradicted his own worldview. Is there any conviction you hold that, if applied consistently to your own actions, would force you to change your behavior? Will you examine that inconsistency today?
?
Impossibility is not a thing that needs a cause; it is simply the absence of existence. What excuse have you been hiding behind by thinking you need a 'reason' or 'cause' to overcome an obstacle, when in fact the obstacle is merely an absence of action? What is one step you can take right now to fill that void?

The Circularity Argument Against Rest as a Positive Attribute
Author denies rest is a positive attribute requiring an effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses circular reasoning to refute the claim that rest (sukūn) is a positive attribute requiring a cause.

Core Idea
Rest cannot be proven to need a cause without circular reasoning.

This passage presents a second objection in a debate about whether rest is a positive attribute that requires a cause. The opponent bases the need for a cause on the principle of origination (ḥudūth). The author challenges this by showing that the opponent cannot prove rest is originated without relying on the very point they are trying to prove, leading to circularity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the passage to see that the opponent claims rest is a positive attribute and thus needs a cause. The opponent's principle is that need for a cause arises from origination.
2
Identify the two-stage refutation
The author first denies that rest is a positive attribute. Then, even if conceded, the author argues that rest's need for a cause cannot be proven without circularity.
Focus on the logical structure: cause of need = origination, so to prove need you must prove origination; but origination is proven by need? That's the circle.
3
Trace the circular reasoning
The opponent must show rest is originated to claim it needs a cause. But they have made its origination conditional on the premise that rest needs a cause, creating a circle. Understand why this refutes the opponent.
Circular reasoning (dawr) is a logical fallacy.
4
Apply to broader theology
This argument shows the precision of kalam reasoning. Rest is not necessarily a positive attribute; it can be a mere absence of motion. The burden of proof is on the opponent.
Connects to discussions about divine attributes and whether God's rest is a positive attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الوجه الثاني فنقول : لا نسلم كون السكون وصفا ثبوتيا سلمناه لكن لا نسلم افتقاره إلى المؤثر لان علة الحاجة عندكم الحدوث فلا يمكنكم بيان افتقار هذا السكون إلى المؤثر إلا إذا بينتم حدوثه وأنتم فرعتم حدوثه على هذه المقدمة فيصير ديرا .
As for the second aspect, we say: We do not concede that rest is a positive attribute. Even if we concede it, we do not concede its need for an effect, because the cause of need according to you is origination (ḥudūth). So you cannot explain the need of this rest for an effect unless you demonstrate its origination, but you have made its origination conditional upon this premise, leading to circularity.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a second objection-and-answer. The opponent claims that rest (sukūn) is a positive attribute that requires a cause (muʼaththir). The author first denies that rest is a positive attribute. Then, even if granted, the opponent's own principle is that need for a cause arises from temporality (ḥudūth). Thus, to prove rest needs a cause, they must first prove rest is originated. But they have made the origination of rest depend on this very premise (that rest needs a cause because it is originated), resulting in circular reasoning (dawr).

Key Terms
سكون
وصف ثبوتي
افتقار إلى المؤثر
علة الحاجة
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rest is undeniably a positive attribute that requires a cause.
Correction: The author shows that rest may not be a positive attribute, and even if it were, proving its need for a cause leads to circular reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against rest needing an effect?
A) Rest is not a positive attribute.
B) Rest is eternal.
C) The need for a cause is caused by origination, but you cannot prove rest is originated without circularity.
D) Rest is a motion.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the claim that rest is a positive attribute needing a cause by exposing circular reasoning in the opponent's argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you think of a belief or assumption you hold about yourself (e.g., that you are 'good' or 'sincere') that might be circular? What would it take to honestly test that belief without relying on the very thing you want to prove?
?
The author denied that rest is a positive attribute. Is there an area of your spiritual life where you assume a state (like 'faith at rest') is a positive quality without examining its basis? How might you re-evaluate that assumption using the same logical scrutiny?

Refutation of the Eternity of the Type of Motion
Author argues motion's essence precludes eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the essence of motion, even as a type, is incompatible with eternity due to its inherent connection to being preceded.

Core Idea
Motion's essence is tied to precedence; eternity cannot coexist with it.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: perhaps while individual motions come to be, the universal type of motion could be eternal. The author demonstrates that this distinction fails because motion's very definition involves a succession of passing away and coming into being, which inherently requires a prior state. Understanding this argument deepens our grasp of how essential properties determine what can be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic and its literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Note that 'type' (naw') refers to the general category, while 'particular' (shakhs) refers to individual instances.
2
Analyze the opponent's position
The opponent claims the type of motion is eternal. Consider why someone might think that: the series could be infinite even if each member is temporal.
3
Understand the refutation
The author argues that motion's essence (māhiyya) is composed of something that passes away and something that occurs, so it is inherently preceded. Eternity requires no precedence. Therefore, they are incompatible.
Focus on the logical necessity: if motion's essence requires precedence, then it cannot be eternal, regardless of type vs. individual.
4
Reflect on the implication
This argument shows that not only individual motions but motion as a category is temporal. It reinforces the principle that only what is uncaused and unpreceded can be eternal.
It connects to broader kalam discussions on the createdness of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله الأزلى نوع الحركة لا شخصها . قلنا هذا باطل ، لأن الحركة ماهيتها بحسب نوعها مركبة من أمر ينقضى ومن أمر حصل فاذا ماهيتها متعلقة بالمسبوقية بالغير وماهية الأزلية منافية لهذا المعنى فالجمع بينهما محال .
His statement: The eternal is the type of motion, not its particular. We said: This is false, because the essence of motion according to its type is composed of something that passes away and something that occurs. So its essence is connected to being preceded by another, and the essence of eternity is incompatible with this meaning, so combining them is impossible.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The opponent argues that while individual motions are temporal, the type or genus of motion could be eternal (i.e., there could be an eternal series of motions). The author refutes this by analyzing the essence (māhiyya) of motion. Motion, even as a type, involves both a passing away (the previous moment) and a coming into being (the current moment); it is inherently connected to being preceded by something else (mutaqaddim). The essence of eternity (al-azaliyya) is that it is not preceded by anything. Since motion requires being preceded, it cannot be eternal. Thus, even the infinite series of motions cannot be eternal because each individual motion is temporal, and the type cannot escape this characteristic.

Key Terms
نوع الحركة
شخص الحركة
ماهية الحركة
أزلية
مسبوقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The type of motion could be eternal even if individual motions are not.
Correction: The essence of motion, even as a type, necessarily involves being preceded by something else, which is incompatible with the definition of eternity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the claim that the type of motion is eternal as false?
A) Because motion is a body.
B) Because motion's essence involves being preceded by another, which is incompatible with eternity.
C) Because motion is not real.
D) Because only God is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Motion's essence is inherently temporal because it requires precedence, so neither its individuals nor its type can be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a persistent pattern in your life—like a habit, a sin, or a phase—that you assume will continue indefinitely? How does this lesson's insistence that motion's very essence entails precedence force you to confront the temporality of that pattern? What will you change today to break its hold?
?
You claim to believe that only Allah is eternal, but do you live as if your current state—your health, your youth, your relationships, or even your faith—is permanent? Where exactly in your heart have you 'eternalized' something that is, by its nature, passing away?
?
The opponent argued that the type of motion could be eternal even if individuals are not. In your life, do you hide behind general categories—'I am a procrastinator,' 'I am not good at prayer'—as if these types are unchangeable? How does the author's refutation that even the type is temporal expose the lie of such fatalism? What concrete step will you take this week to prove that your 'type' can change?
