The Four-Claim Proof for the Origination of Bodies
Presentation of the four-claim proof for the origination of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four-claim proof (al-da‘āwī al-arba‘) that the majority of mutakallimūn used to demonstrate the origination (ḥudūth) of bodies.

Core Idea
Four claims build the proof: body has accidents, cannot lack them, accidents are contingent, so body is contingent.

The proof that all bodies are created (ḥādith) rests on a compact logical structure of four premises. In this lesson, you will read the formulation given by the author and grasp how each claim leads to the conclusion that bodies are originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and locate the four claims
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the four claims explicitly listed after 'al-da‘āwī al-arba‘ hiya'.
The claims are: affirming accidents, impossibility of body being free, necessity of non-existence preceding their totality, and necessity of non-existence preceding whatever cannot be separated from what must be preceded by non-existence.
2
Understand the logical sequence
Notice how the claims build on each other: from accidents (ḥawādith) existing, to body always having them, to accidents being contingent, to body itself being contingent.
This proof is foundational in kalām cosmology.
3
Reflect on the purpose of the proof
Consider why this proof was central: it establishes that the physical world is not eternal, which supports the existence of a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : هذه الحبيبة مما أوردها صاحب الكتاب وذكرها في تصانيفه والحجة التي اعتمد عليها جمهور المتكلمين هي التي تشتمل على أربع دعارى ، وهى أن كل جسم لا يخلو من الحوادث وكل مالا يخلو من الحوادث فهو حادث ، والدعاوى الأربع هي إثبات الحوادث وامتناع خلو الجسم منها ووجوب سبق العدم على مجموعها ووجوب سبق المدم على ما يمتنع أن ينفك عما يجب أن يسبق عليه المدم ، وكان من الواجب على مصنف الكتاب أن يبين ما هية الأول حتى يقرر معنى لو كان الجسم أزايا المكان فى الأزل إما كذا وإما كذا ، وقد فسر بعض المتكلمين الأزل بغنى الأزلية وفسره بعضهم باستمرار وجود فى أزمنة مقدرة غير متناهية في جانب الماضى ، ولا شك أن كل واحد من الحركات لا تكون أزلية على أى تفسير فسر به الأزل كما ذكره في إبطال القسم الأول ، إنما الكلام في مجموع الحركات التي لا أول لها كما عبر عنه صاحب الكتاب في الاعتراض على هذه الحجة بقوله لم قلت إن الأولية تنافى وجود حركة قبل حركه لا إلى أول . وجوابه عن ذلك بأن ماهية الحركة بحسب نوعها مركبة من أمر ينقضى ومن أمر حصل ، فإذا ماهيتها متعلقة بالمسبوقية بالغير وماهية الأزلية منافية لهذا المعنى ليس مفيد ، لأن النوع باق مع الأمور المنقضية والأمور الحاصلة وهو لم يورد حجة على أن ذلك النوع مسبوق بالعدم وما هية الحركة يمكن أن توصف بالدوام لا أشخاصها وذلك لا ينافي الأزلية ويلزمه شيء آخر ، وذلك أنه فسر الحركة بالحصول فى حيز بعد الحصول في حيز آخر ، فليس هي نفس الحصول وحده بل يجب أن يقترن بها معني بعدية الحصول السابق وهى أمر إضافي، والإضاءات عنده
(1) I say: This beloved [point] is among what the author of the book brought up and mentioned in his works. The proof (ḥujjah) upon which the majority of the mutakallimūn relied is that which comprises four claims: that every body is not free from accidents (ḥawādith), and whatever is not free from accidents is originated (ḥādith). The four claims are: affirming accidents, the impossibility of the body being free from them, the necessity of non-existence preceding their totality, and the necessity of non-existence preceding whatever cannot be separated from what must be preceded by non-existence. It was incumbent upon the author of the book to clarify the quiddity of the first so that he could establish the meaning that if the body were [related] to place in pre-eternity (azal), it would be either such and such. Some mutakallimūn have interpreted pre-eternity as the independence of eternity, and others interpreted it as the continuation of existence in estimated infinite times in the past direction. There is no doubt that every individual motion is not pre-eternal according to any interpretation of pre-eternity, as he mentioned in refuting the first division. Rather, the discussion is about the totality of motions that have no beginning, as the author expressed in objecting to this proof by saying: Why did you say that beginninglessness (awwalīyah) contradicts the existence of a motion before a motion ad infinitum? His answer to that, that the quiddity of motion according to its type is composed of something that passes away and something that occurs, so its quiddity is related to being preceded by another, and the quiddity of pre-eternity is incompatible with this meaning, is not useful, because the type remains with the passing away matters and the occurring matters, and he did not provide a proof that that type is preceded by non-existence. The quiddity of motion can be described as perpetual, not its individuals, and that does not contradict pre-eternity. And it entails something else: that he defined motion as obtaining in a place after obtaining in another place, so it is not the obtaining alone, but there must be connected to it the meaning of the afterness of the previous obtaining, which is a relational matter (amr iḍāfī), and relations (iḍāfāt) according to him are non-existent.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage discusses the standard proof for the creation of bodies that relies on four claims. The author criticizes the author of the book (likely Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) for not clarifying the quiddity of the first (i.e., the body in pre-eternity) and points out disagreements on the definition of pre-eternity (azal). It then examines the objection regarding the totality of motions without a beginning. The response that the type of motion is composed of a passing and a present part is deemed insufficient because the type persists. The author also notes that if motion is defined as obtaining in a place after another, it involves a relational element (afterness) which is considered non-existent by the author, leading to a contradiction.

Key Terms
الحجة
الدعاوى الأربع
الحوادث
الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the four claims (al-da‘āwī al-arba‘) of the proof?
A) Existence of accidents, body free from accidents, accidents eternal, body eternal
B) Existence of accidents, body cannot lack accidents, necessity of non-existence preceding accidents, necessity of non-existence preceding what cannot separate from contingent
C) Body is composed of atoms, accidents are real, body is eternal, accidents are contingent
D) Accidents are created, body is eternal, accidents are inseparable, body is contingent

Key Takeaway
The four-claim proof establishes that every body is originated because it always contains accidents, which are themselves contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every body, including your own, is originated and contingent, how would your attachment to your physical existence and worldly possessions change? What step will you take today to live in light of that truth?
?
The proof shows that the world cannot be eternal. You have likely heard this before. Why do you still live as if this world will last forever? What is the one specific fear that prevents you from prioritizing the Hereafter?
?
Reflect on your daily routine: which habits or desires treat the body as if it were self-sufficient and unaccountable to a Creator? What three practical changes must you make tonight to remind yourself that your body is a contingent signs of Allah?

Critique of the Proof: Motion, Eternity, and Relational Matters
Critique of the four-claim proof regarding pre-eternity, motion type, and relational elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of the four-claim proof, focusing on the definitions of pre-eternity (azal), the type of motion, and the problem of relational elements (iḍāfāt).

Core Idea
The proof fails because the type of motion can be eternal even if its individuals are contingent.

The author raises objections to the four-claim proof concerning the nature of pre-eternity and the possibility of an eternal series of motions. He argues that the type of motion may be eternal, undermining the conclusion that bodies are originated. Additionally, he points out a dilemma about motion involving a relational element that the author considers non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique and note the central objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the author's criticism: the proof fails to clarify the quiddity of the first, and the objection about the totality of motions without a beginning.
The author distinguishes between individual motions and the type (species) of motion.
2
Understand the two definitions of azal (pre-eternity)
The passage mentions two interpretations: independence of eternity and continuation of existence in infinite past time. Consider how each definition affects the argument.
3
Examine the insufficient answer about the type of motion
The author argues that the type of motion persists across individuals and is not proven to be preceded by non-existence. Therefore, the type could be eternal, undermining the proof.
This is the key flaw in the proof according to the critique.
4
See the dilemma about relational elements (iḍāfāt)
The author shows that motion defined as 'obtaining in a place after another' involves an additional relational element (the 'afterness') which the opponent considers non-existent, leading to a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refuting the Impossibility of Eternal Rest
Imam Razi refutes the argument against eternal rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that a body cannot be at rest from pre-eternity by appealing to the possibility of eternal motion and the nature of non-existence.

Core Idea
Eternal rest is not refuted by motion's impossibility, because motion can be eternal and non-existence is not a cause.

This lesson examines Imam Razi's refutation of an argument against a body being at rest from pre-eternity. The objector claimed that motion's validity requires its existence, which is impossible in pre-eternity, so rest cannot be eternal. Razi responds by showing that motion itself can be eternal, and that the impossibility of motion is a non-existential attribute that cannot serve as a necessary condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument, then read the literal translation. Note the structure: Razi presents two distinct refutations.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the objector's premise
The objector argues that motion's validity depends on its existence, and since motion cannot exist in pre-eternity, the body cannot be at rest (since rest is the opposite of motion). Understand this before Razi's response.
3
Analyze Razi's first refutation
Razi states that the possibility of the continuation of motion in pre-eternity has already been established, so the basis of the objector's proof is nullified. Recognize that this refutes the premise that motion is impossible.
This is a direct counter to the objector's starting point.
4
Analyze Razi's second refutation
Razi argues that the impossibility of motion is not intrinsic but non-existential. Since the objector holds that non-existence cannot be a cause, effect, or relation, it cannot serve as a necessary condition for eternal rest. This undermines the logical link between impossibility and rest.
Note the reference to the objector's own admission that impossibility is non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله في الوجه الأول في إبطال القسم الثاني بامتناع كون الجسم في الأزل ساكنا إن صحة الحركة تتوقف على وجود الحركة في نفسها وقد مر بيان استحالتها في الأزل فيقال له قد تبين مما مر إمكان استمرار نوع الحركة في الأزل، وإذا كان كذلك فقد بطل أصل هذا الدليل. وأيضاً امتناع الحركة لا يكون لذاتها وهو عدمي والعدم عنده لا يكون علة ولا معلولا ولا مضافا إذ الإضافة عنده أيضا عدمية فلا يكون لازما لمامر، وهو أن اللزوم من غير اعتبار العلية والمعلولية غير معقول، وأشار إلى ذلك في الاعتراض بقوله الامتناع عدم فلا يعال.
And as for his statement in the first aspect of nullifying the second division by the impossibility of the body being at rest in pre-eternity, that the validity of motion depends on the existence of motion in itself, and the explanation of its impossibility in pre-eternity has passed, so it is said to him: It has become clear from what has passed the possibility of the continuation of the type of motion in pre-eternity. And if that is the case, then the basis of this proof has been nullified. Also, the impossibility of motion is not from its own essence, and it is non-existential, and non-existence according to him is neither cause nor effect nor relation, since relation is also non-existential according to him, so it is not necessary for what has passed, and that is that entailment without consideration of causality and effectuality is not intelligible. And he pointed to that in the objection by his saying: 'Impossibility is non-existence, so it is not [؟].'
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage addresses the first aspect of nullifying the second division—the claim that a body cannot be at rest from pre-eternity. The objector argued that for motion to be valid, motion must exist in itself, and since motion's existence in pre-eternity was previously shown impossible, rest cannot be eternal either. Imam Razi responds that the possibility of the continuation of the type of motion in pre-eternity has already been demonstrated, so the proof collapses. Additionally, the impossibility of motion is not intrinsic—it is a non-existential attribute. Since the objector holds that non-existence cannot be a cause, effect, or relation, the impossibility cannot serve as a necessary condition. Razi notes that the objector himself indicated this in his objection by saying 'impossibility is non-existence, so it is not...' (the text is unclear at the end).

Key Terms
امتناع
عدم
علة
معلول
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That impossibility of motion in pre-eternity automatically implies the impossibility of eternal rest.
Correction: Razi shows that motion can be eternal, and that impossibility is a non-existential attribute that cannot ground a necessary condition.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi say the objector's proof that a body cannot be at rest in pre-eternity fails?
A) Because motion's continuation in pre-eternity is possible.
B) Because rest is not a type of motion.
C) Because the body is not in a place.
D) Because the objector did not define rest.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that the impossibility of motion does not entail the impossibility of eternal rest, because motion can be eternal and non-existence cannot be a causal condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time when you felt trapped by your own lack of progress in worship. Did you treat your inaction as an unchangeable cause? How does Razi's argument that non-existence is not a cause free you to seek change? What concrete step will you take this week to break that imagined causal chain?
?
Imam Razi argues that a type of motion can be eternal. If Allah can sustain motion in pre-eternity, can He not also sustain action in your heart? What excuses of 'impossibility' have you used to avoid consistent prayer or charity? Will you commit to one small action today, trusting in Allah's power to sustain it?
?
Razi says impossibility is not intrinsic but due to a non-existential attribute. In your life, what habit do you believe is impossible to change? Is that impossibility intrinsic or merely due to the absence of effort? What would it mean to rely on Allah to turn that non-existence into existence?

Essential Possibility and Impossibility by Another
Imam Razi refutes a fallacious answer and distinguishes essential possibility from impossibility by another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi resolves the apparent contradiction between the world's possibility and its non-existence in pre-eternity by distinguishing essential possibility from impossibility due to another.

Core Idea
Essential possibility and impossibility due to another can coexist without contradiction.

Imam Razi tackles a key objection against the possibility of the world's creation: if the world is possible from eternity, then saying it is impossible in pre-eternity seems contradictory. In this passage, he exposes a fallacy in a common answer and provides the correct distinction. This lesson will clarify how essential possibility and external impossibility are not conflicting.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's critique
The passage begins with the objector's claim and then Razi's refutation. Read carefully to see where the opponent's answer goes wrong.
The opponent added the word 'occurrence' to create a false distinction.
2
Identify the two types of possibility
Razi introduces the key terms: essential possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī) and impossibility due to another (al-imtināʿ bi'l-ghayr). Understand how they differ.
Essential possibility is inherent in the thing itself; impossibility due to another comes from an external factor.
3
Understand why there is no contradiction
Razi argues that essential possibility and impossibility by another are not contradictory. The world is possible in itself but its existence in pre-eternity is impossible because of its dependence on a free agent.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.
4
Apply the distinction to other cases
Consider how this distinction applies to other theological debates about divine attributes or actions.
This is a powerful tool in kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله في الوجه الأول من المناقضة إن إمكان وجود العالم لا أول له فالقول بأنه ممتنع الوجود في الأزل مناقض له وقوله في الجواب إنه لا بداية لإمكان حدوث العالم، لكن أزليته مع فرض الحدوث محال فزاد في الجواب لفظ الحدوث ليصح له المغالطة، وكان من الصواب أن يقول الإمكان الذاتي والامتناع بالغير لا يتناقضان وإنما يمتنع وجود العالم أزلا مكانه لاستناده إلى فاعل مختار أو غير ذلك ما يقتضى حدوثه.
And as for his statement in the first aspect of contradiction, that the possibility of the existence of the world has no beginning, so the statement that its existence in pre-eternity is impossible contradicts it. And his statement in the answer: 'There is no beginning to the possibility of the world's occurrence, but its pre-eternity with the assumption of occurrence is impossible' — so he added in the answer the word 'occurrence' to make the fallacy correct. The correct thing would be to say: Essential possibility and impossibility due to another are not contradictory. Rather, the world's existence in pre-eternity is impossible because of its reliance on a free agent or other than that which necessitates its occurrence.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage addresses the first of what appears to be several objections (manāqaḍa) against the possibility of the world's creation. The objector claims that if the world's existence is possible from eternity (i.e., it is possible for it to exist without beginning), then saying it is impossible for it to exist in pre-eternity is a contradiction. The opponent's answer attempted to avoid this by saying that the possibility of occurrence (ḥudūth) has no beginning, but the world's pre-eternity combined with the assumption of occurrence is impossible. Imam Razi points out that this answer is fallacious because it smuggles in the word 'occurrence' to create a false distinction. The correct response is that essential possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī) and impossibility due to another (al-imtināʿ bi'l-ghayr) are not contradictory. The world is possible in itself, but its existence in pre-eternity is impossible because of an external factor—namely, its dependence on a free agent or something else that necessitates its temporal origination.

Key Terms
إمكان
امتناع بالغير
إمكان ذاتي
مناقضة
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility of existence at any time implies possibility of existence in pre-eternity.
Correction: Essential possibility does not entail possibility in every time; external factors can prevent existence in pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what is the correct way to resolve the apparent contradiction between the world's possibility and its non-existence in pre-eternity?
A) The world is impossible in itself.
B) Essential possibility and impossibility by another are not contradictory.
C) The world's creation is impossible.
D) Possibility and impossibility are the same.

Key Takeaway
Essential possibility and impossibility due to another are not contradictory, resolving the apparent conflict between the world's possibility and its non-existence in pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you confused what is possible for you in yourself with what is possible for you given your circumstances? How does this lesson help you distinguish between the two?
?
Are there areas in your life where you use verbal tricks or excuses to avoid accepting a divine limit that seems to contradict your potential?
?
Can you accept that something may be possible in itself but impossible for you right now because of Allah's wisdom and plan? How does that change your patience and submission to His decree?

Critique of Redefining Motion and Rest for the World
Imam Razi refutes a new definition of motion and rest based on contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why redefining motion and rest solely in terms of contact leads to absurd consequences for the world as a whole and why the objector's attempt fails.

Core Idea
Redefining motion and rest by contact fails for the world as a whole.

Imam Razi carefully examines a proposed redefinition of motion and rest that tries to avoid the problem of the world not being in a place. He shows that this new definition collapses under its own logic, leading to contradictions that undermine the very proof it was meant to salvage. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: first the redefinition, then Razi's criticisms.
Pay attention to the terms 'حركة', 'سكون', 'مماسة'.
2
Identify the redefinition and its purpose
The objector defines motion and rest for two touching substances. Rest is remaining in contact, motion is leaving contact. This was meant to answer how the world (not in a place) can be described as moving or resting.
The objector is trying to find a definition that applies even when there is no external place.
3
Follow Razi's three objections
Razi points out: (1) A single body would not be moving or resting. (2) If a body moves, its parts stay in contact and thus are resting. (3) The world as a whole, having no external body, would not move or rest, even if its parts do. This destroys the original proof.
Also note the inconsistency with the earlier interpretation of motion and rest as 'al-husul' (occurrence/position).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله في الجواب عن الاعتراض الذي بعده: وهو أن العالم ليس في مكان فلا يكون متحركا ولا ساكنا فإنا إذا فرضنا جوهرين متماسين عنينا بالسكون بقائهما على ذلك الوجه وبالحركة زوالهما عنه، تفسير جديد للحركة والسكون بما لا يفيده، وذلك القول يقتضى أن الجسم الواحد لا يكون متحركا ولا ساكنا، وأيضا أن الجسم إذا تحرك كانت أجزاؤه ساكنة لبقائها على المماسة، وأيضا لما كان العالم عبارة عن جميع الأجسام ولا يمكن أن يكون معه جسم آخر فلا يكون متحركا ولا ساكنا، وإن كانت أجزاؤه متحركة وساكنة، وحينئذ بطل أصل الدليل ومن قبل فسر الحركة والسكون بالحصول.
And his statement in the answer to the objection that follows: which is that the world is not in a place, so it is not moving nor resting. For if we suppose two touching substances, we mean by 'rest' their remaining in that manner, and by 'motion' their separation from it — a new interpretation of motion and rest that does not benefit. That statement necessitates that a single body is not moving nor resting; also that if a body moves, its parts are resting because they remain in contact; also, since the world is the entirety of bodies and it is not possible for another body to be with it, it is not moving nor resting, even if its parts are moving and resting. And here the basis of the proof is nullified, and previously he interpreted motion and rest by 'al-ḥuṣūl' (occurrence/position).
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection regarding the world's motion or rest. The objector claimed that since the world is not in a place (beyond space), it cannot be described as moving or resting. The response offered a new definition: for two touching substances, rest means remaining in contact, motion means separation. Imam Razi criticizes this as a new, unhelpful interpretation. It leads to absurdities: a single body (not composed of parts) would not be moving or resting; if a body moves, its parts are resting because they stay in contact with each other. Applied to the world, the world as a whole cannot move or rest because there is no external body to touch or separate from, yet its parts may still move or rest. This undermines the original proof (which presumably relied on the world being either moving or resting to prove its temporality). Additionally, the objector previously defined motion and rest differently (by 'obtaining' or 'position'), so the inconsistency is clear.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
مماسة
جوهر
مكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion and rest can be defined solely by contact and separation for all cases, including the world as a whole.
Correction: Imam Razi shows this definition leads to absurdities when applied to single bodies, composite bodies, and especially the world as a whole, proving it is inadequate.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi reject the new definition of motion and rest as remaining in or leaving contact?
A) Because it implies a single body cannot be moving or resting.
B) Because the world is in a place.
C) Because motion is only defined by place change.
D) Because the objector did not define touch.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that redefining motion and rest by contact leads to logical absurdities and is inconsistent with previous definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever redefined a concept in your own life to avoid a difficult truth, only to find that the new definition creates more contradictions? What did you do when you realized it did not benefit?
?
When you find yourself facing a logical conclusion that challenges your position, do you honestly examine the consequences or try to escape by changing definitions? Which approach did Razi teach you to take?
?
Are you willing to accept the logical implications of your beliefs, even when they seem inconvenient, or do you seek ways to avoid them? Reflect on a time when consistency demanded a hard choice.

Proof from Matter: The Dilemma of Possibility
Razi presents a dilemma: matter either originated or eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of infinite regress and the positive nature of possibility combine to prove that matter is created.

Core Idea
Possibility requires a subject, and infinite regress is impossible, so matter must be directly created.

Imam Razi opens his triple proof by focusing on the concept of possibility (imkan). He argues that before something exists, its possibility is a real attribute needing a bearer. That bearer is matter. But if that matter is itself originated, it would require another matter, leading to an infinite chain. If not, matter would be eternal. The only way out is to posit that matter was created from nothing by God. This passage sets up the dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage carefully. Notice the key terms: حادث, إمكان, مادة, تسلسل, قدم.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: matter is either originated or eternal. Trace the consequences of each.
3
Reflect on why infinite regress is unacceptable
In kalam, an infinite regress of causes is considered impossible (impossible in actuality). Think about why this would be a problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بالنظر إلى المادة فلان كل محدث فقد كان قبل حدوته ممكنا والإمكان وصف ثبوتى في الممكن فيستدعى موصوفا ثابتا وذلك هو المادة ، ثم هي إن كانت حادثة افتقرت إلى مادة أخرى ولزم التسلسل وإلا لزم قدم المادة .
وأما بالنظر إلى الصورة فلان الزمان لا يقبل العدم الزماني لأن كل محدث فعدمه سابق على وجوده فمفهوم ذلك السبق أمر مغاير للعدم ، لأن العدم قد يكون قبل وبعد والقبل لا يكون بعد ، وتلك القبلية صفة ثبوتية فقبل أول الحوادث حادث آخر والكلام فيه كما فى الأول فقبل كل حادث حادث لا إلى أول .
وأما بالنظر إلى الغاية فهو أن موجد العالم إن كان مختارا فلابد له من غاية الإيجاد فكان مستكملا بذلك الإيجاد فكان ناقصا لذاته ، وإن لم يكن مختارا لكان موجبا لذاته فيلزم من قدمه قدم الأثر
And as for the consideration of matter: because every originated thing (muhdath) was possible before its origination, and possibility (imkan) is a positive attribute in the possible, so it requires a fixed subject, which is the matter. Then if it is originated, it depends on another matter, and an infinite regress follows; otherwise, the matter would be eternal (qadim).
And as for the consideration of form: because time does not admit temporal non-existence, for every originated thing, its non-existence precedes its existence. The concept of that precedence is something other than non-existence, because non-existence may be before and after, and the 'before' cannot be 'after'. That precedence is a positive attribute. So before the first originated thing there is another originated thing, and the discourse concerning it is as in the first, so before every originated thing there is an originated thing without first.
And as for the consideration of purpose: because the one who brings the world into existence, if He is free and voluntary (mukhtar), then He must have a purpose for bringing it into existence, so He would be perfecting Himself by that bringing into existence, so He would be deficient in His essence. And if He is not free and voluntary, then He would be necessary by His essence (mujib bi-dhatihi), so the eternity of the effect would follow from His eternity.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents three classical arguments (from matter, form, and purpose) used by the mutakallimun to prove that the world is originated and not eternal. The author states each argument concisely as part of a larger discussion. The first argument (from matter) says that every originated thing was possible before existing, and possibility is a positive attribute requiring a subject (matter). If that matter is itself originated, an infinite regress occurs; if not, matter would be eternal, which is what the opponent wants to avoid. The second argument (from form) argues that the precedence of non-existence over existence is a positive relation (qabliyya), which requires a previous time or originated thing, leading to an infinite regress. The third argument (from purpose) is a dilemma: if the Creator is free, He would need a purpose, implying imperfection; if necessary, His effect would be co-eternal. Each argument is meant to force the opponent to accept the world's createdness.

Key Terms
حدوث
قدم
إمكان
مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof from matter, what follows if matter is itself originated (haditha)?
A) It would be eternal
B) It would require another matter, leading to infinite regress
C) It would be self-sufficient
D) It would be necessary

Key Takeaway
The proof from matter shows that possibility is a real attribute requiring a subject, and that subject cannot be either infinitely regressive or eternal, implying direct creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that every possibility requires a subject. When you reflect on your own existence, do you truly feel that every moment of your being is not self-sustained but depends entirely on the One who created matter without a cause? How would your actions change if you lived with that constant awareness?
?
Razi presents a dilemma: either matter is originated (leading to infinite regress) or eternal. If you accept that infinite regress is impossible, then matter must be directly created. Does your daily reliance on material things blind you to the reality that matter itself is a direct gift from God? What would it take for you to see the world as a sign of His immediate creative power?
?
Possibility is not just a mental concept but a real attribute demanding a subject. Do you treat your own possibilities—your potential, your future—as mere abstractions, or do you recognize that they too are anchored in the reality that God creates and sustains? When you plan for tomorrow, are you humble enough to acknowledge that your very possibility of acting depends on a Creator who brought matter into being without any prior cause?

Proof from Form: The Precedence of Non-Existence
Razi argues from the positive nature of temporal precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the temporal precedence of non-existence over existence is a positive relation that leads to an infinite sequence of prior originated things.

Core Idea
Beforeness is a real attribute, so there is no first moment of origination.

Razi's second argument examines the concept of time and order. He argues that when we say 'non-existence precedes existence', the 'precedence' is not nothing but a real relation. This relation must have a subject, and that subject is a prior originated thing. Consequently, there must be an infinite chain of originated things with no beginning. This argument is used to show that the world cannot have a temporal start without an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Pay attention to the terms: قبلية, عدم, زمان, حادث.
2
Understand the distinction between precedence and non-existence
The passage argues that precedence (qabliyya) is not mere non-existence; it is a positive attribute.
3
Trace the regress
Start from any originated thing: its non-existence precedes its existence. That precedence requires a subject, which is another originated thing. Repeat ad infinitum.
This leads to an infinite regress with no first member.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بالنظر إلى المادة فلان كل محدث فقد كان قبل حدوته ممكنا والإمكان وصف ثبوتى في الممكن فيستدعى موصوفا ثابتا وذلك هو المادة ، ثم هي إن كانت حادثة افتقرت إلى مادة أخرى ولزم التسلسل وإلا لزم قدم المادة .

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Proof from Purpose: The Dilemma of Divine Agency
Razi presents a dilemma: either God is mukhtar or mujib bi-dhatihi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a free creator must have a purpose (implying imperfection) and a necessary creator implies a co-eternal effect.

Core Idea
God's creative act must be either free or necessary; both have unacceptable implications.

The third argument tackles the nature of God's will. Razi presents a dilemma: if God is free and voluntary (mukhtar), His act of creation must have a purpose, which would suggest He was incomplete without creation; if God is not free but acts by necessity (mujib bi-dhatihi), then His effect (the world) would be eternal like Him. Both conclusions are theologically unacceptable, so the world must be temporally originated by a free act of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Focus on the terms: مختار, موجب بالذات, غاية, إيجاد, نقص.
2
Analyze the first horn: free creator
If God is free, He must have a purpose for creation. Having a purpose implies He was lacking something, thus imperfect. Is this conclusion necessary?
3
Analyze the second horn: necessary creator
If God is not free but creates by necessity, then His effect would be co-eternal with Him. This would mean the world is eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بالنظر إلى المادة فلان كل محدث فقد كان قبل حدوته ممكنا والإمكان وصف ثبوتى في الممكن فيستدعى موصوفا ثابتا وذلك هو المادة ، ثم هي إن كانت حادثة افتقرت إلى مادة أخرى ولزم التسلسل وإلا لزم قدم المادة .
وأما بالنظر إلى الصورة فلان الزمان لا يقبل العدم الزماني لأن كل محدث فعدمه سابق على وجوده فمفهوم ذلك السبق أمر مغاير للعدم ، لأن العدم قد يكون قبل وبعد والقبل لا يكون بعد ، وتلك القبلية صفة ثبوتية فقبل أول الحوادث حادث آخر والكلام فيه كما فى الأول فقبل كل حادث حادث لا إلى أول .
وأما بالنظر إلى الغاية فهو أن موجد العالم إن كان مختارا فلابد له من غاية الإيجاد فكان مستكملا بذلك الإيجاد فكان ناقصا لذاته ، وإن لم يكن مختارا لكان موجبا لذاته فيلزم من قدمه قدم الأثر
And as for the consideration of matter: because every originated thing (muhdath) was possible before its origination, and possibility (imkan) is a positive attribute in the possible, so it requires a fixed subject, which is the matter. Then if it is originated, it depends on another matter, and an infinite regress follows; otherwise, the matter would be eternal (qadim).
And as for the consideration of form: because time does not admit temporal non-existence, for every originated thing, its non-existence precedes its existence. The concept of that precedence is something other than non-existence, because non-existence may be before and after, and the 'before' cannot be 'after'. That precedence is a positive attribute. So before the first originated thing there is another originated thing, and the discourse concerning it is as in the first, so before every originated thing there is an originated thing without first.
And as for the consideration of purpose: because the one who brings the world into existence, if He is free and voluntary (mukhtar), then He must have a purpose for bringing it into existence, so He would be perfecting Himself by that bringing into existence, so He would be deficient in His essence. And if He is not free and voluntary, then He would be necessary by His essence (mujib bi-dhatihi), so the eternity of the effect would follow from His eternity.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents three classical arguments (from matter, form, and purpose) used by the mutakallimun to prove that the world is originated and not eternal. The author states each argument concisely as part of a larger discussion. The first argument (from matter) says that every originated thing was possible before existing, and possibility is a positive attribute requiring a subject (matter). If that matter is itself originated, an infinite regress occurs; if not, matter would be eternal, which is what the opponent wants to avoid. The second argument (from form) argues that the precedence of non-existence over existence is a positive relation (qabliyya), which requires a previous time or originated thing, leading to an infinite regress. The third argument (from purpose) is a dilemma: if the Creator is free, He would need a purpose, implying imperfection; if necessary, His effect would be co-eternal. Each argument is meant to force the opponent to accept the world's createdness.

Key Terms
مختار
موجب بالذات
غاية
إيجاد
نقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof from purpose, what follows if the Creator is not free (ghayru mukhtar)?
A) He is imperfect
B) The world would be eternal
C) He has no purpose
D) He is necessary by essence

Key Takeaway
The dilemma of divine agency shows that either God's freedom implies imperfection or necessity implies eternal world; both are avoided by affirming temporal creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's actions in your life, do you secretly expect Him to act according to your timeline or needs because you assume He must have a purpose that benefits you? How does this dilemma challenge your understanding of divine freedom and purpose?
?
If God's creative act cannot be explained by either pure freedom or pure necessity, how does that unsettle your tendency to put God in a box of human logic? Are you willing to accept that God's agency is beyond your categories, even when it leaves you without neat answers?
?
This passage shows that both extremes lead to unacceptable conclusions about God. Do you ever lean towards one extreme—either thinking God's actions are arbitrary (free) or mechanical (necessary)? Which tendency do you see in yourself, and how can you cultivate a more balanced and humble trust in God's wisdom?

Two Ratio Arguments: Against Void and Proving Inclination
Razi presents two ratio-based arguments: one against void, one for inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi uses proportional reasoning to argue that void cannot exist and that every body must possess inclination (mayl).

Core Idea
Void is impossible because it would make a resisting medium and its absence equivalent.

This lesson examines two related arguments from Razi's note. The first uses a ratio between the consistency of a medium and motion time to disprove void, while the second uses a similar ratio involving inclination to prove that every body possesses inclination (mayl). Both arguments rely on the principle that contradictions arise if certain proportionalities hold.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage slowly
Carefully read the Arabic and English to grasp the structure: a warning statement, then an explanation that the problem applies to both denying void and proving inclination.
Notice the two distinct arguments are separated by 'As for proving inclination'.
2
Understand the first argument (against void)
The argument supposes a body thinner than water with a specific consistency ratio. If the ratio of its consistency to water equals the ratio of motion time in void to motion time in water, then the time in this thin plenum equals time in void, making the presence of the plenum equivalent to its absence—impossible.
This shows that void cannot exist because it would collapse the difference that resistance makes.
3
Understand the second argument (for inclination)
The second argument supposes a body whose inclination (mayl) ratio leads to motion time equal to motion with no inclination. This would mean the presence and absence of inclination are equivalent—contradiction. Therefore every body must have inclination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : الحركة في الملأ الذي نسبة رقته إلى رقة الماء كنسبة زمان الحركة في الخلاء إلى زمانها في الماء إنما يقع لا في زمان إذا لم يكن استحقاقها الزمان لذاتها بل للعائق لكن ذلك معلوم الفساد (1) أقول : المسألة التي أوردها ههنا تستعمل في نفي الخلاء وفي إثبات الميل أعني الاعتماد فيقولون الحركة في الخلاء تقع في زمان لا محالة وفي الماء مثلا في زمان أطول ، لكون قوام الماء مفارقا للمتحرك وقوامات الأجسام قابلة للتزيد والتنقص ، فإذا فرض جسم أرق من الماء بحيث يكون نسبة قوامه إلى قوام الماء نسبة الزمانين وجب أن تكون الحركة فيه في زمان مساو للحركة في الخلاء فيكون وجود المفارق وعدمه سواء وهذا محال ، فإذا الخلاء ممتنع الوجود . وأما في إثبات الميل فيقولون : الحركة مع عدم الميل تقتضي زمانا ومع ميل مفروض زمانا أقل من ذلك الزمان لمفارقة الميل ، والميل قابل لشدة وضعف، فإذا فرض جسم يكون نسبة ميله إلى الميل المفروض نسبة عدم الميل إلى زمان ذي الميل المفروض ، وكان زمان حركته مساويا لزمان حركة عدم الميل فيكون وجود الميل وعدمه واحدا هذا خلف ، فإذا الجسم لا يخلو عن ميل وهو المطلوب .

قالوا : وليس لقائل أن يقول : أو مع عدم الميل يقع لا في زمان والزمان يتوزع على المحركات بحسب رقة القوام وكثافته أو بحسب قلة الميل وكثرته ، لأن المتحرك يستحق زمانا لذاته ، فإن قطع نصف المسافة يكون قبل قطع تمامها ، هذا ما يقولون في هذا الموضع .

واعترض الشيخ أبو البركات عليه بأن قال : لما كانت الحركة تستحق زمانا لذاتها كان فصل زمان الحركة على الحركة في الخلاء أو مع عدم الميل متوزع على الرقة والكثافة أو على الميل القليل والكثير ، ويكون زمان حركة كل جسم مجموع زمان حركته لولا القيام أو الميل مع حصة القوام أو الميل ماريا الزمان مركة عديمها . وأجيب عنه : بأن الحركة يستحيل أن توجد إلا في حدها من السرعة والبطء ، وزمان السريعة والبطيئة مختلفان ، فالحركة وإن كانت تستحق زمانا لذاتها لكنها من حيث هي حركة فقط يستحيل أن يعنى لها زمان ، فإن كل زمان معنى يجب أن يكون قابلا للنقصان والزيادة وحينئذ كانت مع حد من السرعة والبطء وفرضت مجردة عنها هذا خلف ، فهذا ما قيل في هذا الموضع .

وما في الكتاب جواب سؤال وتقريره هكذا : الحركة في الملأ الذي نسبته فيه إلى رقة الماء إما أن يقع في زمان أو لا في زمان ، لكن يستحيل أن يقع في زمان لأنه يستلزم كون الحركة في الملأ الذي هو أرق من ذلك الملأ أسرع من الحركة في الخلاء ، والمقاوم يجب أن يجعل الزمان أكثر وعلى هذا التقدير بجملة أول ، هذا خلف ، فإذا تلك الحركة تقع لا في زمان، وذلك إنما يمكن إذا لم يكن استحقاقها للزمان لذاتها بل للعائق وذلك معلوم الفساد ، ويلزم منه ما ذكره أبو البركات - رحمه الله بعينه
Warning: Motion in the plenum whose thinness relative to the thinness of water is as the ratio of the time of motion in the void to its time in water occurs only in no time if its deserving time is not due to its essence but due to the obstacle, but that is known to be invalid. (1) I say: The problem introduced here is used in negating the void and in proving inclination, i.e., i'timad. They say: Motion in the void occurs in time necessarily, and in water, for example, in a longer time, because the consistency of water separates the mover, and the consistencies of bodies are susceptible to increase and decrease. So if one supposes a body thinner than water such that the ratio of its consistency to the consistency of water is the ratio of the two times, then motion in it must occur in a time equal to motion in the void, so the existence of the separator and its non-existence are equivalent, and this is impossible. Thus the void is impossible of existence. As for proving inclination, they say: Motion with absence of inclination requires time, and with a supposed inclination, a time less than that time due to the separation of inclination, and inclination is susceptible to intensity and weakness. So if one supposes a body whose inclination's ratio to the supposed inclination is as the ratio of absence of inclination to the time of the supposed inclination, then its time of motion would equal the time of motion of the absence of inclination, so the existence of inclination and its non-existence would be one, which is a contradiction. Thus a body is not devoid of inclination, which is what is sought.

They said: And one cannot say: Or with absence of inclination it occurs in no time, and time is distributed among motions according to the thinness and density of consistency or according to the paucity and abundance of inclination, because the mover deserves time by its essence, for cutting half the distance occurs before cutting all of it. This is what they say on this topic.

And Shaykh Abu al-Barakat objected to it by saying: Since motion deserves time by its essence, the division of the time of motion into motion in the void or with absence of inclination is distributed according to thinness and density or according to the little and much inclination, and the time of motion of each body is the sum of its time of motion without the resistance or inclination together with the portion of consistency or inclination, which is the time of motion of its opposite. And it was answered: Motion can only exist with a limit of speed and slowness, and the time of the fast and the slow are different. So motion, even if it deserves time by its essence, but considered as motion only, it is impossible that a time be intended for it, for every time by meaning must be susceptible to decrease and increase, and then it would be with a limit of speed and slowness and it was supposed free from them, which is a contradiction. This is what is said on this topic.

And what is in the book is an answer to a question, and its formulation is thus: Motion in the plenum whose ratio in it to the thinness of water: either it occurs in time or not in time. But it is impossible for it to occur in time because it would entail that motion in the plenum which is thinner than that plenum is faster than motion in the void, and the resistance must make the time longer, and on this estimation the first is a contradiction. Therefore that motion occurs in no time, and that is only possible if its deserving time is not due to its essence but due to the obstacle, and that is known to be invalid, and from that follows what Abu al-Barakat, may God have mercy on him, mentioned exactly.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a detailed note (tanbīh) on motion in a plenum versus void, containing arguments against the existence of void and proving inclination (mayl) in bodies. It begins by stating that motion in a plenum with a specific thinness would occur instantaneously if time were not essential to motion, which is absurd. The author then explains through a marginal note (aqūl) that this argument is used both to deny the void and to affirm inclination. The note presents two arguments: first, if a body were thinner than water such that its consistency ratio equals the time ratio, motion would take the same time as in void, making the existence of the resisting medium and its absence equivalent—impossible, so void is impossible. Second, to prove inclination, they argue that motion without inclination requires time, but with inclination takes less time; if a body's inclination ratio matches the ratio of no inclination to time, then existence and absence of inclination would coincide, a contradiction—thus bodies always have inclination. Then an objection is raised: one might claim that motion without inclination occurs instantaneously and time is distributed based on consistency or inclination strength. The author rejects this because motion essentially requires time, as seen in the fact that half the distance is traversed before the whole. Shaykh Abu al-Barakat objects that since motion necessarily has time, the division of time should be based on resistance or inclination, and the time with resistance is a sum of times without resistance plus a portion for resistance. The answer is that motion can only exist with a specific speed (fast or slow), and time differs accordingly; motion considered purely cannot be assigned a time, because any time is divisible and variable. Finally, the book's answer is presented: motion in such a plenum must be either in time or not; it cannot be in time because that would make motion in a thinner plenum faster than in void, but resistance must increase time—contradiction; so it is instantaneous, which is only possible if time is not essential but due to obstacle—but that is known false, leading to Abu al-Barakat's point. The passage methodically refutes the concept of void and establishes the necessity of inclination.

اعتماد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Void (empty space) can exist because motion can occur in empty space without resistance.
Correction: Razi argues that if void existed, the presence and absence of a resisting medium would result in equal motion times for a suitably thin plenum, which is impossible because resistance must increase time.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what is the consequence of supposing a body thinner than water with a consistency ratio equal to the time ratio of void to water?
A) Its motion would take longer than in water.
B) Its motion would take the same time as in void.
C) Its motion would be instantaneous.
D) Its motion would prove the existence of inclination.

Key Takeaway
Razi uses proportional reasoning to demonstrate that void cannot exist and that every body must have inclination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi concludes that void is impossible because it leads to an absurd equivalence. In your own life, are there 'voids'—spaces, relationships, or habits—that you think you need, but which actually lead to contradictions with your faith? Commit to identifying one such void and filling it with something meaningful.
?
The second proof shows that every body must have inclination; there is no neutral state. Spiritually, there is no neutrality: either you incline toward God or away from Him. Which way are you currently inclining? What one change can you make today to increase your incline toward Him?

Objections and Refutations on Motion and Time
Razi addresses objections to the ratio arguments, defending the necessity of time for motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the ratio arguments and how Razi defends the essential role of time in motion.

Core Idea
Time is essential to motion; even without resistance, motion cannot be instantaneous.

After presenting his ratio arguments, Razi anticipates and responds to objections. First, he refutes the idea that motion without inclination could be instantaneous by pointing out that motion inherently requires time, as seen in the fact that half a distance is traversed before the whole. Then Shaykh Abu al-Barakat objects that time should be divided according to resistance or inclination, and Razi answers that motion cannot be assigned a time without specifying its speed. Finally, the book's own answer shows that if time were not essential, motion in a very thin plenum would be instantaneous, which is absurd, thus reaffirming the necessity of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial reply
The objection claims that motion without inclination might occur in no time, with time distributed by consistency or inclination. Razi's reply uses the principle that motion essentially requires time (since half the distance is traversed before the whole).
This is based on the idea that temporal order is inherent in motion.
2
Understand Abu al-Barakat's objection
He argues that if motion deserves time by essence, then the time in a resisting medium is the sum of the time without resistance plus a portion for resistance, undermining the ratio argument.
This is a more sophisticated philosophical objection.
3
Graph the answer and final book answer
The answer to Abu al-Barakat is that motion always has a specific speed (fast or slow), and time varies accordingly; pure motion considered alone cannot be assigned a time. The book's final answer shows that the original argument's premise (motion in a thin plenum occurring in time) leads to a contradiction, so motion must be instantaneous—which is impossible—hence the argument stands.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion can occur in no time if there is no resistance or inclination.
Correction: Razi argues that motion inherently requires time, as demonstrated by the half-distance argument. Without time, motion would be impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Shaykh Abu al-Barakat's objection?
A) Time is not essential to motion.
B) Time should be divided according to resistance or inclination, so the ratio argument is flawed.
C) Motion can be instantaneous in a void.
D) Inclination does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Through objections and replies, Razi reinforces that time is essential to motion, making the existence of void impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that time is not a neutral container but is essential to every motion—every action you take. When you delay prayer or repentance, you assume you have a separate 'time' to use later. But your very movements are bound to time; each action creates its own temporal reality. Are you living as if time is a resource to be managed, or as the very fabric of your deeds? What is one specific action you will take today to align your use of time with the truth that time is inseparable from your worship?
?
The objection treated time as something that can be distributed independently of speed. In your life, do you treat time as something external—like you can 'spend' it without affecting the quality of your deeds? This lesson shows that time and action are one. How does this change your understanding of moments of prayer, charity, or even sleep? Commit to one way you will be more mindful of the intrinsic temporal dimension of a specific act of worship today.
?
If time were not essential to motion, then the void could exist and Allah's power over creation would be limited. But the truth is that every movement—even your own heartbeat—is temporal by essence. Are you grateful that Allah has bound your existence to time, making every moment a potential act of devotion? How will you use the next hour to transform your routine motions into conscious worship, knowing that time is not accidental but the very stage of your obedience?

Proof that Bodies are Finite: The Argument of Parallel Lines
Razi presents a proof that bodies are finite by showing infinite lines lead to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses geometric reasoning to prove that bodies cannot be infinite, and see the philosophical debate around this proof.

Core Idea
Infinite dimensions would create impossible contradictions in geometry and motion.

Razi's proof that bodies are finite is a classic theological argument using geometry. He shows that assuming an infinite line leads to a contradiction when paired with a finite parallel line. This lesson walks through the proof and the subsequent discussion by the philosophers about circular motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse every word. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage has two parts: Razi's direct proof and a note from the philosophers.
2
Understand the contradiction in Razi's proof
Razi supposes an infinite line and a finite parallel line. If the finite line deviates, it would need a first point of alignment with the infinite line. But such a first point cannot exist because for any point on the infinite line, there is always another above it.
This is the core of the proof: the assumption leads to an infinite regress.
3
Examine the philosophers' alternative argument
The philosophers argue that if dimensions were infinite, circular motion would be impossible because the diameter would need a first moment of alignment with an infinite line, which is impossible.
They conclude that since circular motion exists, dimensions must be finite.
4
Consider the criticism of the philosophers' argument
The author notes that alignment occurs in time, not at an instant, so the beginning of alignment can be defined as the end of parallelism. Thus the impossibility is not necessary.
This critique shows the subtlety of the argument about time and motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الأجسام متناهية خلافا للهند

لنا أنا إذا فرضنا خطا غير متناه وفرضنا خطا آخر متناهيا موازيا الأول ، فأما إذا مال المنهي عن المرازاة أول نقطة المسامتة ، لكن ذلك محال ، إذ لا نقطة إلا وفوقها أخرى فتكون نقطة إلى المسامتة فلابد من هي زمان جو.

فلذلك فرض خط غير متناهية يفضي إلى هذا المحال (1) أقول : هذا دليل أورده الحكماء في هذا الموضع ، قالوا لو كانت الأبعاد غير متناهية لامتنعت الحركة على الاستدارة ، إذ يجب أن ينتقل القطر الموازي لبعد غير متناه عند الحركة المستديرة من الموازاة إلى المسامتة فيكون للمسامتة أول ، ويمتنع أن يكون لها أول لما ذكره المصنف ، فإذا الحركة المستديرة على ذلك التقدير ممتنعة الوقوع لكنها موجودة ، فإذا البعد غير المتناهي ممتنع الوجود وفيه نظر ، لأن الأمور الواقعة في الزمان إنما يكون أوائلها أن هو مبدأ ذلك الزمان كالحركة وإن مبدأها هو الآن الذي لم يشرع المتحرك في الحركة بعد وكل آن بعد ذلك الآن ، فإن الحركة قد عبر عنها جزء حين وصل إليه وذلك الجزء يقبل القسمة إلى ما لا نهاية لذلك مسامتة الخط للخط بعد الموازاة فإنها تقع في زمان بخلاف مسامنة الخط للنقطة الواقعة في آن فمبتدأ المسامتة تكون أن الموازاة وكل آن بعد ذلك الآن يكون الخط فيه مسامتته بعد آن غير من المسامتة شيء ينقسم إلى ما لا نهاية ، وبأن من ذلك أن المحال الذي ذكره غير لازم ولا متعلق بتناهي الخط ولا تناهيه
Question: Bodies are finite, contrary to the Indians.

Our proof: If we suppose an infinite line and suppose another finite line parallel to the first, then if what is deviated from parallelism [to align?] the first point of alignment, but that is impossible, for there is no point without another above it, so there would be a point to the alignment, so there must be a time [?]... Thus supposing an infinite line leads to this impossibility. (1) I say: This proof was presented by the philosophers on this topic. They said: If dimensions were infinite, rotational motion would be impossible, because the diameter parallel to an infinite dimension during circular motion must move from parallelism to alignment, so alignment would have a first moment, but it is impossible for it to have a first moment due to what the author mentioned. Thus circular motion on that estimation would be impossible of occurrence, yet it exists, so the infinite dimension is impossible of existence. Yet there is consideration: because events occurring in time have their beginnings only at the start of that time, like motion, and its beginning is the now when the mover has not yet begun motion, and every instant after that now, motion has traversed a part when it reaches it, and that part is divisible infinitely. Therefore, the alignment of the line with the line after parallelism occurs in time, unlike the alignment of the line with the point occurring in an instant. So the beginning of alignment is when parallelism ends, and every instant after that now, the line is in alignment after an instant, which is something that divides infinitely. And that the impossibility he mentioned is not necessary nor related to the finitude or infinity of the line.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that bodies are finite, refuting the Indian philosophers who claim otherwise. The argument uses geometric reasoning: suppose an infinite line and another finite line parallel to it. If the finite line deviates from parallelism, there would be a first point of alignment with the infinite line. But this is impossible because for any point on the infinite line, there is always another above it, so there is no first point. Therefore, the assumption of an infinite line leads to contradiction, so lines (and thus dimensions) must be finite. The author then adds a marginal note (aqūl) explaining that philosophers use a different argument: if dimensions were infinite, circular motion would be impossible, because the diameter initially parallel to an infinite dimension would have to become aligned with it, requiring a first moment of alignment—impossible due to infinite divisibility. Yet circular motion exists, so infinite dimensions are impossible. However, the note notes a consideration: events in time have beginnings only at the start of time, and the alignment of lines occurs in time, not at an instant. The line after parallelism aligns gradually, and the beginning of alignment is when parallelism ceases; after that, at every instant the line is aligned, so the moment of first alignment can be defined as the limit, and the impossibility is not necessary. The proof concludes that the assumption of an infinite line leads to contradiction, establishing finitude.

Key Terms
خط
متناهي
غير متناهي
موازاة
مسامتة
نقطة
حركة استدارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite line could have a first point of alignment with a parallel finite line.
Correction: The argument shows that for any point on the infinite line, there is always another above it, so no first point exists, leading to infinite regress.

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence of assuming an infinite line in Razi's proof?
A) It would make finite lines impossible.
B) It would require a first point of alignment with a parallel line, which is impossible.
C) It would prove circular motion is possible.
D) It would make time infinite.

Key Takeaway
The assumption of an infinite body leads to logical impossibilities, so bodies must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If all bodies are finite, including your own, how does this reality confront your tendency to postpone repentance and good deeds? What specific action will you take today to break the illusion of infinite time?
?
Razi argues that infinite dimensions lead to logical impossibilities. In your spiritual life, you might allow sins or distractions to multiply without end. How does the finitude of your body and lifespan force you to set a limit on your negligence? Name one habit you will stop enabling today.
?
The philosophers' argument against infinite dimensions uses circular motion as evidence. Your life moves in cycles—work, rest, sin, regret. Are you stuck in an endless loop of excuses? What is the first moment of alignment with truth you must establish today, without putting it off any longer?

The Objection: Finite Bodies Require Differentiated Space
Razi presents the opponent's objection as a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that finite bodies supposedly pose: the external space must have distinct sides or not, both leading to absurdity.

Core Idea
Finite bodies force a choice between two impossible options.

Razi sets up an opponent's objection against the finitude of bodies. The objection proceeds as a dilemma: if bodies are finite, the space outside them either has distinguishable sides or it does not. Each horn leads to contradiction, undermining the proof that bodies are finite. This lesson walks through that logical challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two-part structure of the dilemma.
The objection is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two horns
The dilemma: either a side is distinguished from a side, or not. Find the consequences of each possibility.
3
Why pure non-existence cannot differentiate
The objection argues that differentiation cannot occur in pure non-existence because non-existence has no specificity or actuality.
This is key to understanding the first horn.
4
The second horn and its absurdity
If no side is distinguished, this contradicts reason, which clearly sees a difference between poles.
The objection claims this is stubbornness against self-evident truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم بأن الأجسام لو كانت متناهية لكان الخارج عنها بأسرها إما أن يتميز فيه جانب من جانب فيكون محالا وإما أن لا يتميز فإن تميز لم يكن ذلك عدما محضا لأن النفي المحض لا خصوصية فيه ولا تحقق فكيف يحصل الامتياز ، بل لابد وأن يكون أمرا وجوديا ولاشك في أنه إما أن يكون مشارا إليه فيكون مقدارا أو يكون جسما فالخارج عن كل الأجسام جسم هذا خلف ، وإن لم يتميز جانب فيه عن جانب فهذا محال عن بداهة العقل ، لأن العقل الصريح يشهد بأن الطرف الذي يلي القطب اليمين غير الذي يلي القطب الشمالي ، ذلك مكابرة في البديهيات .

فإنكار
الجواب أما المتكلمون فقد سلموا أحيارا متميزة خارج العالم غير متناهية ، وزعموا أنها أمور تقديرية غير موجودة وهذا ضعيف ، لأن المقدر هو الذي لا وجود له إلا في الذهن والذي لا وجود له إلا في الذهن إن لم يكن مطابقا للخارج كان ذلك فرضا كاذبا وإن كان مطابقا لزم منه وجود الأحياز في نفس الأمر وحينئذ يعود الإلزام وأما الحكماء فإنهم أصروا على أن خارج العالم لا يتميز فيه جانب من جانب وأن الحاكم بهذا التميز هو الوهم لا العقل وحكم الوهم غير مقبول (2) .

(2) أقول : المتكلمون سلموا أحيارا غير متناهية ولم يزعموا أنها تقديرية ، بل زعموا أن التمايز فيها تقديري وذلك هو القول بالخلاء الذي شغلته الأجسام ويكون مكانا حيزا لها .
وأما قوله : الذي يلي القطب الشمالي غير الذي يلي الجنوبي ، يقولون في جوابه أن هذا التمايز في القطبين وهما وجوديان وفي الخلاء الذي يليهما تقدري يتوهم بالقياس إليهما ولولا هما لم يكن مبين أصلا والحكماء القائلون بأن الأمكنة سطوح الحادثات يقولون هذه الأحياز وهمية والحكم بوجودها في الخارج كاذب وما لا وجود له أصلا لا يكون فيه امتياز أصلا
The opponent argued: If bodies were finite, then the exterior of all of them would either be such that a side is distinguished from a side—that would be impossible—or not distinguished. If distinguished, that would not be pure non-existence, because pure negation has no specificity and no actuality, so how can differentiation occur? Rather, it must be an existential thing, and there is no doubt that it is either pointed to, so it is a magnitude, or it is a body, so the exterior of all bodies is a body—a contradiction. And if a side is not distinguished from a side, that is impossible by the obviousness of reason, because sound reason testifies that the side that faces the south pole is not the one that faces the north pole—that is stubbornness against self-evident truths.

Denial.
The answer: As for the theologians, they conceded distinct infinite spaces outside the world, claiming they are estimative things not existent, but this is weak, because the estimated is that which has no existence except in the mind, and what has no existence except in the mind, if it does not correspond to the external, would be a false supposition, and if it corresponds, it would entail the existence of spaces in reality, and then the objection returns. As for the philosophers, they insisted that outside the world no side is distinguished from a side, and that the judge of this distinction is estimation (wahm), not reason, and the judgment of estimation is not accepted. (2)

(2) I say: The theologians conceded infinite spaces and did not claim they are estimative, rather they claimed that the differentiation in them is estimative, and that is the doctrine of the void which bodies occupy, and it becomes a place and space for them.
And as for his statement: 'That which faces the north pole is not that which faces the south pole,' they say in answer that this differentiation is in the two poles, which are existential, and in the void that follows them it is estimative, imagined by analogy to them, and without them there would be no indication at all. And the philosophers who hold that places are surfaces of generated things say that these spaces are imaginary, and the judgment of their existence externally is false, and what has no existence at all has no differentiation in it at all.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's objection to the proof that bodies are finite, followed by two answers. The objection argues: if bodies are finite, the space outside them must either have distinct sides or not. If it has distinct sides, that cannot be pure non-existence (because non-existence lacks specificity), so it must be an existing thing—either a magnitude or a body, making the outside a body, which contradicts the assumption that only bodies exist. If sides are not distinct, that contradicts reason, which clearly sees a difference between directions like north and south poles. Thus, finite bodies lead to absurdity. The word 'فإنكار' (denial) may indicate a rejection of this. The answer first gives the view of the theologians (mutakallimūn): they concede infinite distinct spaces outside the world but consider them estimative (taqdīriyya) non-existent. The author criticizes this as weak because what exists only in the mind either corresponds to reality (then spaces exist) or does not (false supposition). Then the philosophers' view: they hold that outside the world there is no real distinction; only estimation (wahm) imagines such distinction, and estimation is not valid. The author adds a marginal note clarifying that theologians actually said differentiation is estimative, not the spaces themselves—they affirm the void as a place. Regarding the pole argument, they respond that the differentiation of poles is based on existing poles, and the void's direction is estimative relative to them; without poles, no basis. The philosophers say spaces are imaginary, not real, so no real differentiation exists.

Key Terms
خارج العالم
امتياز
عدم محض
وجودي
مقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that pure non-existence can have differentiation, like empty space having distinct regions.
Correction: The objection argues that pure non-existence has no features, so differentiation cannot occur in it.

Checkpoint
What does the opponent argue would happen if the external space had distinct sides?
A) It would be pure non-existence.
B) It must be an existent, either a magnitude or a body.
C) It would be infinite.
D) It would be identical to nothing.

Key Takeaway
The objection uses a dilemma to attack the finitude of bodies: either the external space has distinct sides (impossible) or not (contradicts reason).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the edge of the universe, does your mind instinctively reject the possibility of a finite boundary? How does this lesson force you to confront the limits of your own rational intuition? Are you willing to accept that your immediate sense of space might be misleading, or do you cling to it as self-evident?
?
The opponent's argument presents a dilemma that seems inescapable. What dilemmas in your own life—where both options appear impossible—do you tend to ignore rather than critically examine? How can you learn from the method of this argument to scrutinize your own assumptions, even when they feel self-evident?
?
This lesson highlights that pure non-existence cannot have any differentiation. Reflect on a time when you assumed that 'nothing' could be a place or have properties. How does this realization about the nature of non-existence reshape your understanding of what it means for something to exist? Commit to questioning one assumption you hold about the physical world today.

The Theologians' Answer: Estimative Spaces
Razi presents and critiques the theologians' answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' response—conceding infinite distinct spaces as estimative—and the critique that this is weak.

Core Idea
Theologians say spaces are estimative, but that only shifts the problem.

After the objection, Razi presents the answer of the theologians (mutakallimūn). They concede that there are infinite distinct spaces outside the world, but claim these spaces are merely estimative (taqdīriyya) and not actually existent. Razi then criticizes this position, arguing that an estimative thing either corresponds to reality (making spaces real) or is a false supposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the theologians' answer
Read the passage describing the theologians' view and Razi's critique.
Pay attention to the definition of 'estimative' (taqdīri).
2
Understand the critique
Razi argues that if something is only in the mind, it either matches external reality (then spaces exist) or it doesn't (false supposition).
This is a classic critique of mental existence without external correspondence.
3
Recognize the weakness
Razi concludes that the theologians' answer is weak because the objection returns if spaces are real.

Source Text

According to Razi's critique, what happens if the estimative spaces correspond to external reality?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever treat difficult truths as 'estimative'—ideas you keep in your mind so you don't have to face their real implications? What would happen if you let one of those ideas become truly real for you today?
?
When you encounter a contradiction in your beliefs, do you tend to accept an easy-sounding answer that just 'shifts the problem'? What is one area where you are settling for a weak answer instead of pressing for the truth?
