The Philosophers' Answer: Estimation, Not Reality
Razi presents the philosophers' answer and adds a clarifying note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' answer that any differentiation outside the world is merely imagined by estimation (wahm), not real, and how they defend this against the pole argument.

Core Idea
Philosophers deny real distinction outside the world; it's all estimation.

Razi then presents the answer of the philosophers (ḥukamā'). They insist that outside the world there is no real distinction of sides; the apparent differentiation is only a product of estimation (wahm). Since estimation is not a valid source of knowledge, its judgments are not accepted. Razi adds a marginal note clarifying the theologians' position and the philosophers' reply to the pole argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' answer and note
Read the passage including the footnote. Notice the philosophers' core claim and the counter-argument about poles.
The footnote is Razi's own addition.
2
Understand 'wahm' (estimation)
Wahm is a cognitive faculty that can imagine things not present; its judgments are not authoritative in rational discourse.
This is key to the philosophers' defense.
3
Analyze the pole argument response
The philosophers respond that the differentiation of poles is based on existent poles, and the void's direction is estimative relative to them; without poles, there is no basis for differentiation.
This shows how estimation relies on existent things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم بأن الأجسام لو كانت متناهية لكان الخارج عنها بأسرها إما أن يتميز فيه جانب من جانب فيكون محالا وإما أن لا يتميز فإن تميز لم يكن ذلك عدما محضا لأن النفي المحض لا خصوصية فيه ولا تحقق فكيف يحصل الامتياز ، بل لابد وأن يكون أمرا وجوديا ولاشك في أنه إما أن يكون مشارا إليه فيكون مقدارا أو يكون جسما فالخارج عن كل الأجسام جسم هذا خلف ، وإن لم يتميز جانب فيه عن جانب فهذا محال عن بداهة العقل ، لأن العقل الصريح يشهد بأن الطرف الذي يلي القطب اليمين غير الذي يلي القطب الشمالي ، ذلك مكابرة في البديهيات .

فإنكار
الجواب أما المتكلمون فقد سلموا أحيارا متميزة خارج العالم غير متناهية ، وزعموا أنها أمور تقديرية غير موجودة وهذا ضعيف ، لأن المقدر هو الذي لا وجود له إلا في الذهن والذي لا وجود له إلا في الذهن إن لم يكن مطابقا للخارج كان ذلك فرضا كاذبا وإن كان مطابقا لزم منه وجود الأحياز في نفس الأمر وحينئذ يعود الإلزام وأما الحكماء فإنهم أصروا على أن خارج العالم لا يتميز فيه جانب من جانب وأن الحاكم بهذا التميز هو الوهم لا العقل وحكم الوهم غير مقبول (2) .

(2) أقول : المتكلمون سلموا أحيارا غير متناهية ولم يزعموا أنها تقديرية ، بل زعموا أن التمايز فيها تقديري وذلك هو القول بالخلاء الذي شغلته الأجسام ويكون مكانا حيزا لها .
وأما قوله : الذي يلي القطب الشمالي غير الذي يلي الجنوبي ، يقولون في جوابه أن هذا التمايز في القطبين وهما وجوديان وفي الخلاء الذي يليهما تقدري يتوهم بالقياس إليهما ولولا هما لم يكن مبين أصلا والحكماء القائلون بأن الأمكنة سطوح الحادثات يقولون هذه الأحياز وهمية والحكم بوجودها في الخارج كاذب وما لا وجود له أصلا لا يكون فيه امتياز أصلا
The opponent argued: If bodies were finite, then the exterior of all of them would either be such that a side is distinguished from a side—that would be impossible—or not distinguished. If distinguished, that would not be pure non-existence, because pure negation has no specificity and no actuality, so how can differentiation occur? Rather, it must be an existential thing, and there is no doubt that it is either pointed to, so it is a magnitude, or it is a body, so the exterior of all bodies is a body—a contradiction. And if a side is not distinguished from a side, that is impossible by the obviousness of reason, because sound reason testifies that the side that faces the south pole is not the one that faces the north pole—that is stubbornness against self-evident truths.

Denial.
The answer: As for the theologians, they conceded distinct infinite spaces outside the world, claiming they are estimative things not existent, but this is weak, because the estimated is that which has no existence except in the mind, and what has no existence except in the mind, if it does not correspond to the external, would be a false supposition, and if it corresponds, it would entail the existence of spaces in reality, and then the objection returns. As for the philosophers, they insisted that outside the world no side is distinguished from a side, and that the judge of this distinction is estimation (wahm), not reason, and the judgment of estimation is not accepted. (2)

(2) I say: The theologians conceded infinite spaces and did not claim they are estimative, rather they claimed that the differentiation in them is estimative, and that is the doctrine of the void which bodies occupy, and it becomes a place and space for them.
And as for his statement: 'That which faces the north pole is not that which faces the south pole,' they say in answer that this differentiation is in the two poles, which are existential, and in the void that follows them it is estimative, imagined by analogy to them, and without them there would be no indication at all. And the philosophers who hold that places are surfaces of generated things say that these spaces are imaginary, and the judgment of their existence externally is false, and what has no existence at all has no differentiation in it at all.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's objection to the proof that bodies are finite, followed by two answers. The objection argues: if bodies are finite, the space outside them must either have distinct sides or not. If it has distinct sides, that cannot be pure non-existence (because non-existence lacks specificity), so it must be an existing thing—either a magnitude or a body, making the outside a body, which contradicts the assumption that only bodies exist. If sides are not distinct, that contradicts reason, which clearly sees a difference between directions like north and south poles. Thus, finite bodies lead to absurdity. The word 'فإنكار' (denial) may indicate a rejection of this. The answer first gives the view of the theologians (mutakallimūn): they concede infinite distinct spaces outside the world but consider them estimative (taqdīriyya) non-existent. The author criticizes this as weak because what exists only in the mind either corresponds to reality (then spaces exist) or does not (false supposition). Then the philosophers' view: they hold that outside the world there is no real distinction; only estimation (wahm) imagines such distinction, and estimation is not valid. The author adds a marginal note clarifying that theologians actually said differentiation is estimative, not the spaces themselves—they affirm the void as a place. Regarding the pole argument, they respond that the differentiation of poles is based on existing poles, and the void's direction is estimative relative to them; without poles, no basis. The philosophers say spaces are imaginary, not real, so no real differentiation exists.

Key Terms
الحكماء
الوهم
الخلاء
تمايز تقديري
سطوح الحادثات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the distinction of directions like north and south outside the world proves real differentiation.
Correction: Philosophers argue that this distinction is only in estimation, not in external reality.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why is the apparent distinction between north and south outside the world not real?
A) Because the poles are imaginary.
B) Because the distinction is only in estimation (wahm), not in external reality.
C) Because the world is infinite.
D) Because reason cannot perceive difference.

Key Takeaway
Philosophers deny real external differentiation; any apparent distinction is mere estimation, which carries no rational weight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine 'outside the world' or 'beyond the heavens', do you automatically picture a physical space? How often in your daily thoughts do you mistake an estimative judgment for a rational certainty? Will you adopt a more critical stance toward such mental images starting today?
?
The philosophers say the judgment of estimation is not accepted. Consider a time you were absolutely convinced of something based on a feeling or a mental picture. Were you possibly relying on wahm rather than reason? Are you willing to examine your deepest certainties for unevaluated estimative assumptions?
?
This lesson suggests that what we think is a real distinction may be merely an analogy derived from known objects (like poles). What in your understanding of the afterlife or the unseen might be an analogy from this world rather than a direct rational truth? How can you purify your belief to be only what reason and revelation affirm?

The World is Not Necessarily Everlasting
Razi gives a brief argument that what is not pre-eternal cannot be everlasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that anything which begins in time cannot be necessarily everlasting, and why this refutes the philosophers and Karramiyya.

Core Idea
Temporal origin implies possible termination; what is not pre-eternal cannot be everlasting.

Imam Razi presents a concise but powerful argument against the necessity of the world's perpetuity. He establishes a logical link between having a beginning and being susceptible to an end. This brief passage cuts to the heart of the debate about the world's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice how Razi states his position clearly before giving the proof.
The passage is very short but dense; every word matters.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the words 'azalī' (pre-eternal) and 'abadī' (everlasting). Understand their distinct meanings: azalī refers to something without a beginning, abadī refers to something without an end.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow Razi's reasoning: if something is not azalī, its essence is receptive to non-existence (qābil li-l-'adam). Therefore it cannot be abadī. Ask yourself why this connection holds.
This argument is a form of modal proof: contingency implies possible non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : العالم لا يجب أن يكون أبديا خلافا للفلاسفة والكرامية

لنا أن ما لم يكن أزليا وجب أن لا يكون أبديا لأن ما لا يكون أزليا كانت ماهيته قابلة للعدم وذلك القبول
Question: The world does not have to be everlasting, contrary to the philosophers and the Karramiyya.

Our proof: That which is not pre-eternal must not be everlasting, because that which is not pre-eternal has its essence receptive to non-existence, and that acceptance...
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents a brief argument that the world is not necessarily everlasting (abadī), refuting the philosophers and the Karramiyya who affirm its perpetuity. The proof is concise: anything that is not pre-eternal (azalī) cannot be everlasting, because if it began in time, its essence is such that it could cease to exist. The passage ends with 'wa dhālika al-qabūl' (and that acceptance), which seems to begin a further explanation that is cut off. The core idea is that contingency implies both a beginning and a potential end; since the world is not pre-eternal (it began), it must also be possible for it to end, so it is not necessarily everlasting. This argument is commonly used in kalām to prove the temporal origination of the world and its possible finitude.

Key Terms
أزلي
أبدي
ماهية
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that temporal origin does not imply possible termination; e.g., something could begin and then last forever necessarily.
Correction: Razi argues that a non-pre-eternal thing's essence is inherently receptive to non-existence, so it cannot be necessarily everlasting. The very fact that it began shows its existence is contingent, not necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the logical connection between not being pre-eternal (azalī) and not being everlasting (abadī) according to Razi?
A) Everything pre-eternal is also everlasting.
B) What is not pre-eternal has an essence that can cease, so it cannot be everlasting.
C) What is not pre-eternal must be preceded by nothing.
D) The world is an exception to this rule.

Key Takeaway
Anything that begins in time has an essence that can cease, so it cannot be absolutely everlasting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world is not everlasting, and your life in it is temporary, what have you prepared for what comes after? Are you building for something that will perish or for something that will remain?
?
Your own existence began. The essence of everything that begins is receptive to non-existence. Are you living as if you will never die, or as if your time is limited? What changes would you make today if you truly believed you could cease at any moment?
?
This argument shows that the world cannot be absolutely everlasting. But you will be held to account for your actions. If you knew this world will end, how would you prioritize your goals and desires? Write one concrete commitment you will make to align your life with the truth that only the eternal (Allah) deserves ultimate attachment.

First Proof: The Necessary Cause Implies World's Permanence
Philosophers' first argument: necessary cause implies permanent world

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first proof for the impossibility of the world's annihilation based on the necessary cause.

Core Idea
A necessary cause produces a permanent effect.

The philosophers begin their case against annihilation by appealing to the nature of the cause of the world. They argue that since the cause is necessary in its action, it cannot cease producing its effect. This lesson unpacks that simple but powerful claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the key term 'mūjib bi-dhātihī' (necessary by essence).
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument has two premises: (1) the cause is necessary, (2) a necessary cause implies a permanent effect. See how the conclusion follows.
3
Reflect on the implication
If the cause is eternal and unchanging, its effect must also be eternal. This challenges the concept of a temporal beginning or end for the world.
This is a key point of disagreement in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة فقد احتجوا بأمور أحدها أن المؤثر في العالم موجب بالذات فيلزم من دوامه دوام العالم وثانيها أنه لو عدم الزمان لكان عدمه بعد وجوده بعديه بالزمان فيكون الزمان موجودا حال ما فرض معدوما هذا خلف ، وثالثها أن كل ما يقبل العدم فإمكان عدمه حاصل قبل عدمه ، وذلك الإمكان لا بد له من محل أى لابد من شيء محكوم عليه أنه الاتصاف بذلك العدم، وذلك ليس هو وجود الشيء لأن الذي يمكن اتصافه بالشيء لا بد وأن يكون ثابتا مع ذلك الشيء ، ووجود ذلك الشيء عدمه فإذا فلابد من شيء آخر يقوم إمكان عدمه وذلك هو الهيولى ، فإذا كل ما صح عليه العدم فله هيولى فلو صح العدم على الهيولى لافتقر إلى هيولى أخرى لا إلى نهاية ، فإذا الهيولى لا تقبل العدم ، وقد ثبت أن الهيولى لا تخلو عن الصورة الجسمية فإذا عدم الجسم محال
As for the philosophers, they have argued by means of three things: First, that the efficient cause in the world is necessary by essence, so from its permanence follows the permanence of the world. Second, that if time were to become non-existent, its non-existence after its existence would be posterior by time, so time would exist at the moment it is assumed non-existent — this is a contradiction. Third, that everything that admits non-existence, its possibility of non-existence obtains before its non-existence, and that possibility necessarily has a substrate, i.e., there must be something that is described as being qualified by that non-existence, and that is not the existence of the thing because that which can be described by something must be established with that thing, and the existence of that thing is its non-existence. Therefore, there must be another thing that supports the possibility of its non-existence, and that is the hyle. So everything that is valid for non-existence has a hyle. If non-existence were valid for the hyle, it would need another hyle, ad infinitum. Thus, the hyle does not admit non-existence. And it has been established that the hyle is never devoid of corporeal form. Therefore, the non-existence of the body is impossible.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments from the philosophers (falāsifa) for the impossibility of the world's annihilation. The first argument: the cause (mu'aththir) of the world is a necessary agent (mūjib bi-dhātihī), meaning it acts by essence without choice. Since the cause is eternal and unchanging, its effect—the world—must also be eternal and cannot cease. The second argument: if time were to cease after having existed, its non-existence would be a temporal event, requiring time itself to exist to measure the 'after,' leading to a contradiction. The third argument: anything that can become non-existent requires a substrate (maḥall) that possesses the potential for that non-existence; this cannot be the thing's own existence, as that would require simultaneity of existence and non-existence. Hence, there must be a prime matter (hayūlā) that supports this potential. Since the hyle itself cannot admit non-existence (or an infinite regress would result), and since hyle always subsists with corporeal form, the body (jism) cannot become non-existent. Thus, the philosophers conclude the world is imperishable.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة
موجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers believe the world is eternal because God is eternal.
Correction: Actually, they argue from the nature of necessary causation, not from God's attribute of eternity. The cause is necessary in its action, so its effect is permanent.

Checkpoint
What does 'mūjib bi-dhātihī' mean in the philosophers' first argument?
A) The cause is eternal
B) The cause acts by essence without choice
C) The cause is powerful
D) The cause is the world

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that because the world's cause is necessary by essence, the world cannot cease to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you live as if the world around you is permanent and self-sufficient, forgetting that every moment it is sustained only because its Cause is acting? What habit could you start today to remind yourself of your continuous dependence on Allah?
?
The philosophers assumed a necessary cause has no choice. Do you believe your Creator acts with will and wisdom? And if so, how should that change the way you approach Him in your daily decisions and struggles?
?
If the world were indeed necessary and permanent, would you feel less urgency to worship a God who is not actively sustaining you moment by moment? How does the Islamic teaching of continuous creation (that Allah creates every instant) affect your sense of reliance on Him?

Second Proof: The Impossibility of Time's Annihilation
Philosophers' second argument: time's non-existence is contradictory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second proof against annihilation based on the contradiction involved in time ceasing to exist.

Core Idea
Time cannot cease without a contradiction.

The second argument tackles the concept of time itself. If the world were to cease, time would also cease. But the notion of 'after' existence requires time to exist, leading to a contradiction. This lesson examines that paradox.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if time were to become non-existent...'
2
Understand the contradiction
The argument says: if time ceases, its non-existence is 'after' existence, which implies time exists during that 'after'. So time would both exist and not exist—a contradiction.
This relies on the concept of temporal succession.
3
Consider the implication
If time cannot cease, then events in time (like the world) cannot cease either, because cessation is a temporal event.
This argument assumes the world is temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة فقد احتجوا بأمور أحدها أن المؤثر في العالم موجب بالذات فيلزم من دوامه دوام العالم وثانيها أنه لو عدم الزمان لكان عدمه بعد وجوده بعديه بالزمان فيكون الزمان موجودا حال ما فرض معدوما هذا خلف ، وثالثها أن كل ما يقبل العدم فإمكان عدمه حاصل قبل عدمه ، وذلك الإمكان لا بد له من محل أى لابد من شيء محكوم عليه أنه الاتصاف بذلك العدم، وذلك ليس هو وجود الشيء لأن الذي يمكن اتصافه بالشيء لا بد وأن يكون ثابتا مع ذلك الشيء ، ووجود ذلك الشيء عدمه فإذا فلابد من شيء آخر يقوم إمكان عدمه وذلك هو الهيولى ، فإذا كل ما صح عليه العدم فله هيولى فلو صح العدم على الهيولى لافتقر إلى هيولى أخرى لا إلى نهاية ، فإذا الهيولى لا تقبل العدم ، وقد ثبت أن الهيولى لا تخلو عن الصورة الجسمية فإذا عدم الجسم محال
As for the philosophers, they have argued by means of three things: First, that the efficient cause in the world is necessary by essence, so from its permanence follows the permanence of the world. Second, that if time were to become non-existent, its non-existence after its existence would be posterior by time, so time would exist at the moment it is assumed non-existent — this is a contradiction. Third, that everything that admits non-existence, its possibility of non-existence obtains before its non-existence, and that possibility necessarily has a substrate, i.e., there must be something that is described as being qualified by that non-existence, and that is not the existence of the thing because that which can be described by something must be established with that thing, and the existence of that thing is its non-existence. Therefore, there must be another thing that supports the possibility of its non-existence, and that is the hyle. So everything that is valid for non-existence has a hyle. If non-existence were valid for the hyle, it would need another hyle, ad infinitum. Thus, the hyle does not admit non-existence. And it has been established that the hyle is never devoid of corporeal form. Therefore, the non-existence of the body is impossible.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments from the philosophers (falāsifa) for the impossibility of the world's annihilation. The first argument: the cause (mu'aththir) of the world is a necessary agent (mūjib bi-dhātihī), meaning it acts by essence without choice. Since the cause is eternal and unchanging, its effect—the world—must also be eternal and cannot cease. The second argument: if time were to cease after having existed, its non-existence would be a temporal event, requiring time itself to exist to measure the 'after,' leading to a contradiction. The third argument: anything that can become non-existent requires a substrate (maḥall) that possesses the potential for that non-existence; this cannot be the thing's own existence, as that would require simultaneity of existence and non-existence. Hence, there must be a prime matter (hayūlā) that supports this potential. Since the hyle itself cannot admit non-existence (or an infinite regress would result), and since hyle always subsists with corporeal form, the body (jism) cannot become non-existent. Thus, the philosophers conclude the world is imperishable.

Key Terms
الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the world, not time.
Correction: The argument focuses on time because if the world ceases, time would also cease, and that leads to a contradiction.

Checkpoint
Why does the philosophers' second argument lead to a contradiction?
A) Time would exist before itself
B) Time would be eternal
C) Time would exist at the moment it is assumed non-existent
D) Time would cease without cause

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that if time were to cease, it would have to exist at the moment of its non-existence, which is impossible.

Third Proof: The Necessity of a Non-Annihilable Substrate (Hyle)
Philosophers' third argument: possibility of non-existence requires a non-annihilable hyle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' third and most complex proof for the world's imperishability, involving the concept of hyle as a necessary substrate for potential non-existence.

Core Idea
Every possible non-existence needs a substrate that itself cannot cease.

The final argument delves into the metaphysics of possibility and substrate. The philosophers reason that any potential non-existence requires an underlying matter (hyle) to support that potential. Since hyle cannot itself cease (on pain of infinite regress), and hyle always takes corporeal form, the body of the world cannot be annihilated. This lesson unpacks the steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic and translation. This argument has several premises. Try to follow the logic.
2
Identify the need for a substrate
The argument starts: anything that can become non-existent has a possibility of non-existence that needs a substrate. This substrate cannot be the thing's own existence (why?).
Because existence and non-existence cannot coexist.
3
Recognize the substrate as hyle
The only candidate is hyle, the underlying matter. So everything that can cease has hyle.
4
Understand the regress argument
If hyle itself could cease, it would need another hyle, leading to infinite regress. So hyle cannot cease.
This is a classic argument against the annihilation of prime matter.
5
Apply to the body
Since hyle always exists with corporeal form, the body cannot cease to exist.
Thus the world (as a body) is imperishable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة فقد احتجوا بأمور أحدها أن المؤثر في العالم موجب بالذات فيلزم من دوامه دوام العالم وثانيها أنه لو عدم الزمان لكان عدمه بعد وجوده بعديه بالزمان فيكون الزمان موجودا حال ما فرض معدوما هذا خلف ، وثالثها أن كل ما يقبل العدم فإمكان عدمه حاصل قبل عدمه ، وذلك الإمكان لا بد له من محل أى لابد من شيء محكوم عليه أنه الاتصاف بذلك العدم، وذلك ليس هو وجود الشيء لأن الذي يمكن اتصافه بالشيء لا بد وأن يكون ثابتا مع ذلك الشيء ، ووجود ذلك الشيء عدمه فإذا فلابد من شيء آخر يقوم إمكان عدمه وذلك هو الهيولى ، فإذا كل ما صح عليه العدم فله هيولى فلو صح العدم على الهيولى لافتقر إلى هيولى أخرى لا إلى نهاية ، فإذا الهيولى لا تقبل العدم ، وقد ثبت أن الهيولى لا تخلو عن الصورة الجسمية فإذا عدم الجسم محال
As for the philosophers, they have argued by means of three things: First, that the efficient cause in the world is necessary by essence, so from its permanence follows the permanence of the world. Second, that if time were to become non-existent, its non-existence after its existence would be posterior by time, so time would exist at the moment it is assumed non-existent — this is a contradiction. Third, that everything that admits non-existence, its possibility of non-existence obtains before its non-existence, and that possibility necessarily has a substrate, i.e., there must be something that is described as being qualified by that non-existence, and that is not the existence of the thing because that which can be described by something must be established with that thing, and the existence of that thing is its non-existence. Therefore, there must be another thing that supports the possibility of its non-existence, and that is the hyle. So everything that is valid for non-existence has a hyle. If non-existence were valid for the hyle, it would need another hyle, ad infinitum. Thus, the hyle does not admit non-existence. And it has been established that the hyle is never devoid of corporeal form. Therefore, the non-existence of the body is impossible.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments from the philosophers (falāsifa) for the impossibility of the world's annihilation. The first argument: the cause (mu'aththir) of the world is a necessary agent (mūjib bi-dhātihī), meaning it acts by essence without choice. Since the cause is eternal and unchanging, its effect—the world—must also be eternal and cannot cease. The second argument: if time were to cease after having existed, its non-existence would be a temporal event, requiring time itself to exist to measure the 'after,' leading to a contradiction. The third argument: anything that can become non-existent requires a substrate (maḥall) that possesses the potential for that non-existence; this cannot be the thing's own existence, as that would require simultaneity of existence and non-existence. Hence, there must be a prime matter (hayūlā) that supports this potential. Since the hyle itself cannot admit non-existence (or an infinite regress would result), and since hyle always subsists with corporeal form, the body (jism) cannot become non-existent. Thus, the philosophers conclude the world is imperishable.

Key Terms
إمكان العدم
المحل
الهيولى
الصورة الجسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hyle can be annihilated like any other matter.
Correction: The philosophers argue that if hyle could be annihilated, it would require another substrate for its possibility of non-existence, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, hyle itself cannot cease.

Checkpoint
Why does the philosophers' third argument conclude that the body cannot become non-existent?
A) Because the body is eternal by itself
B) Because hyle, which underlies the body, cannot cease and always has form
C) Because the soul prevents it
D) Because time would stop

Key Takeaway
The philosophers conclude that since every possible non-existence requires a substrate that itself cannot cease, and the world's body has such a substrate (hyle), the world cannot be annihilated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers conclude that the body cannot cease to exist because of an endless chain of necessary substrates. Do you find yourself relying more on such philosophical arguments or on divine revelation when thinking about the permanence of this world? What does your reliance reveal about your trust in Allah's absolute power?
?
If hyle itself were subject to non-existence, an infinite regress would follow. Yet you, as a Muslim, affirm that Allah can annihilate the entire universe without needing any substrate. Are there areas in your life where you assume some 'substrate' that cannot be removed—like a habit, a relationship, or a security—that you should instead submit to the possibility of Allah's absolute control?
?
The proof asserts that the world cannot be annihilated because of logical necessity. But you believe in the Resurrection and the transformation of the heavens and earth. How does this tension between human reasoning and revealed truth challenge you to prioritize faith over your own intellect when they seem to conflict?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Karamiyya's Argument Against Annihilation of the World
Karamiyya enumerate three ways annihilation could occur and refute each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karamiyya's rational proof that the world cannot be annihilated, based on three exhaustive possibilities.

Core Idea
The world's annihilation is impossible because every possible means leads to contradiction.

The Karamiyya, a theological sect, argued that the world is eternal a parte post (cannot cease). In this passage, they present a proof by division: they enumerate the only possible ways annihilation could occur and show each is rationally impossible. This lesson walks through their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three divisions
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that the Karamiyya begin by stating that if the world were to cease, it would have to be through one of three means: annihilation by an agent, occurrence of a contrary, or removal of a condition.
2
Understand the argument against annihilation by an annihilator (iʿdām)
Focus on the first division. The Karamiyya argue that if the annihilator is an existential act, it would lead to existence being identical to non-existence; if it is purely non-existent, it cannot be attributed to the agent. Both options are absurd.
This refutes the possibility of direct annihilation by an agent.
3
Understand the two arguments against annihilation by occurrence of a contrary
The second division is refuted by two independent arguments: first, a vicious circle would result; second, either the contraries would negate each other (leading to simultaneous existence and non-existence) or they would not (leading to coexistence of contraries). Both are impossible.
4
Reflect on the third division (removal of condition)
The third division is mentioned but not argued separately. The Karamiyya likely considered it reducible to the first two or self-evidently impossible. Note that the argument as a whole is exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الكرامية المسلمون على وجوب أبدية العالم بأن عدم العالم بعد وجوده ، إما أن يكون بإعدام معدم أو بطريان ضد أو بانتفاء شرط والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة ، فالقول بعدم العالم باطل بعد وجوده ، إنما قلنا أنه لا يجوز أن يعدم بالإعدام لأن الإعدام إن كان أمرا وجوديا لم يكن ذلك الوجود عين عدم العالم وإلا لكان الوجود عين العدم ، بل غايته الجوهر فيكون ذلك إعداما بالضد ، وليس هذا هذا القسم بل هو القسم الثانى ، وإن لم يكن وجوديا كان عدما محضا فيمتنع إسناده إلى المؤثر لأنه لا فرق فى العقل بين أن يقال لم يفعل البتة ، وبين أن يقال فعل العدم وإلا فيكون أحد العدمين مخالفا للثانى فيكون لكل واحد من العدمين تغير وثبوت فيكون لعدم ثبوتيا هذا خلف . وإنما قلنا إنه لا يجوز أن يعدم لحدوث الضد لوجهين . أحدهما : أن حدوث الضد يتوقف على انتفاء الضد الآخر فلو كان انتفاء الضد الآخر معللا بحدوث هذا الضد لزم الدور وهو محال . الثاني : أن التضاد حاصل فى الجانبين وليس انتفاء أحدهما أولى من العكس ، فإما أن ينتفى كل واحد منهما بالآخر وهو محال ، لأن المؤثر فى عدم كل واحد منهما وجود الآخر والمؤثر حاصل مع المؤثر فلو حصل المؤثران معا لوجد الوجودان معا فيكونان موجودين معدومين دفعة واحدة وهو محال، أو لا ينتفى واحد بالآخر فيلزم اجتماع الضدين .
The Muslim Karamiyya argued for the necessity of the world's perpetuity [by saying] that the non-existence of the world after its existence must be either by the annihilation of an annihilator, or by the occurrence of a contrary, or by the removal of a condition; all three divisions are invalid. Therefore, the claim that the world becomes non-existent after its existence is false. We say: it is not permissible for it to become non-existent by annihilation, because if annihilation is an existential thing, then that existence is not the very non-existence of the world—otherwise existence would be identical to non-existence; rather, its ultimate is the substance, so that would be annihilation by a contrary, and this is not this division but the second division. And if it is not existential, then it is pure non-existence, so it is impossible to attribute it to the efficient cause, because there is no difference in reason between saying 'he did not act at all' and saying 'he brought about non-existence'—otherwise one of the two non-existences would be different from the other, so each of the two non-existences would have change and establishment, so non-existence would have a positive aspect—this is a contradiction. And we say: it is not permissible for it to become non-existent by the occurrence of a contrary for two reasons. First: the occurrence of the contrary depends on the non-existence of the other contrary; if the non-existence of the other contrary were caused by the occurrence of this contrary, a vicious circle would result, which is impossible. Second: contrariety exists on both sides, and the non-existence of one is not more likely than the opposite. Therefore, either each of them is negated by the other—which is impossible, because the efficient cause of the non-existence of each is the existence of the other, and the cause exists simultaneously with the effect; so if both causes obtained together, both existences would exist together, and thus they would exist and be non-existent simultaneously, which is impossible—or neither is negated by the other, which entails the coexistence of contraries.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the Karamiyya's proof that the world cannot be annihilated after its existence. They begin by dividing the possible ways annihilation could occur into three: (1) by an act of an annihilator (iʿdām), (2) by the advent of a contrary (ṭuriyān ḍidd), (3) by the removal of a condition (intifāʾ sharṭ). They argue each is impossible. For the first: if the annihilator is an existential act, then that act would be the non-existence of the world, leading to the absurdity that existence equals non-existence; if it is purely nonexistent, it cannot be attributed to the agent, as no rational distinction exists between non-action and bringing about non-existence. For the second (occurrence of a contrary): they offer two reasons. First, a vicious circle would result because the occurrence of a contrary depends on the prior nonexistence of the opposite contrary. Second, both contraries are mutually opposed; either each would be negated by the other, which would require both to exist and not exist simultaneously, or neither would be negated, leading to the coexistence of contraries, which is impossible. Thus, the world cannot cease to exist.

Key Terms
الكرامية
إعدام
ضد
انتفاء شرط
دور
اجتماع الضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Karamiyya deny any possibility of annihilation, including by God's direct act.
Correction: The Karamiyya only argue that annihilation is impossible through the three specified means; they do not consider direct act as possible because it reduces to those means.

Checkpoint
What are the three possible means of annihilation that the Karamiyya consider and then reject?
A) Annihilation by an agent, by a contrary, or by removal of a condition
B) Annihilation by time, by space, or by motion
C) Annihilation by God's will, by natural decay, or by chance
D) Annihilation by fire, by water, or by air

Key Takeaway
The Karamiyya's proof shows that the world's annihilation is rationally impossible because each possible means leads to an absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed this world could never cease to exist, would your priorities shift away from the akhira? The Karamiyya argued for its perpetuity—does that thought make you uneasy or complacent? What would it take for your heart to remain attached to Allah even if the world were eternal?
?
The Karamiyya rejected the idea that non-existence can be brought about by an agent. Reflect: have you ever felt that your own actions could 'destroy' something spiritually? How does the rational impossibility of annihilation challenge you to trust more in Allah's power over existence and non-existence?
?
The proof against annihilation hinges on logical absurdities. When you examine your own faith, do you rely more on logical proofs or on direct experience? If a logical argument contradicts a deeply held belief, how do you respond? This lesson demands you submit to reason—are you willing to let go of positions that cannot be rationally defended?
